## Summary
This academic volume explores the comprehensive history and economics of international shipping, identifying it as a critical industry in the global economy. It features contributions from prominent scholars covering topics such as merchant shipping, maritime logistics, and the historical evolution of trade routes and freight rates. The text examines the industry through various lenses, including business history, institutional development, and the effects of globalization across several centuries.

## The World's Key Industry History and Economics of International Shipping (G. Harlaftis, S. Tenold, J. Valdaliso) (z-lib.org)
The World's Key Industry
History and Economics of International
Shipping

John Armstrong, until recent retirement, was Professor of Business
History at Thames Valley University. He has served as editor of theJournal
of Transport Historyyy, deputy chair of the British Commission for Maritime
History and as vice-president of the International Maritime Economic
History Association. He has an interest in all forms of transport and has pub-
lished extensively. He has organized the British Commission for Maritime
History seminars at King's College London, for over a decade. His books
include (with Andreas Kunz) Coastal Shipping and the European Economy,
1750-19800 (Verlag Philipp von Zabern, 2002). His most recent book is The
Vital Spark: the British Coastal Trade 1700-19300(IMEHA, 2009).
Michaela G. Barnard is Research Fellow in Maritime History at the
University of Hull, UK. As well as managing the Data Facility of the
History of Marine Animal Populations (HMAP) project, she teaches mar-
itime and marine environmental history and undertakes research into
the business dimensions of maritime activity, notably the role of family
firms in British shipping during the late nineteenth and twentieth cen-
turies. Her publications include the co-edited Oceans Past: Management
Insights into the History of Marine Animal Populations (Earthscan, 2007)
and numerous articles on the business of shipping.
Gordon Boyce is Professor of International Business and Director of
the Centre of Institutional and Organisational Studies (CIOS) at the
University of Newcastle. He serves on several editorial boards and
academic societies. Boyce has been a Visiting Fellow at the Australian
National University, Duke University and the Centre for Business
History. He has published several books and numerous articles in
leading journals, including Information, Mediation and Institutional
Development, How Organisations Connect (with Simon Ville and Stuart
MacIntyre),OverHalf a Million Careful Owners andCo-operative Structures
in Global Business. He has been the recipient of the Cass Prize, a Caird
Fellowship, and a British Commission for Transport History Prize.
Espen Ekbergg is Postdoctoral Research Fellow at the University of
Agder, Norway. He has published books and articles on the history of
the consumer co-operative movement in Norway and internationally,

of History of the Ionian University. She graduated from the University

of Cambridge (M.Phil.) and Oxford (D.Phil). She was President of the
International Maritime Economic History Association 2004-2008 and has

C. Knick Harley is Professor of Economic History at the University
of Oxford. He is a former editor of the Journal of Economic History. His
research has concentrated on expanding globalization in the nineteenth
century and the relationship of globalization to economic development
in Britain and America. He contributed to the Cambridge Economic
History of Modern Britain with chapters entitled ‘Trade: discovery, mer-
cantilism and technology' inVolume 1:Industrialization, 1700-1860, and
‘Trade, 1870-1939: From globalization to fragmentation' in Volume 2:
Economic Maturity, 1860-1939. He has worked extensively on the course
of freight rates, their determinants and implications and has also pub-
lished extensively on the British Industrial Revolution.
Yrjö Kaukiainen is Emeritus Professor of European History at the
University of Helsinki and a former President of the International
Maritime Economic History Association. His research has focused, in
particular, on the history of shipping in the nineteenth and twentieth
centuries, as well as the history of information transmission in the nine-
teenth century. Kaukiainen is the author of A History of Finnish Shipping
(Routledge, 1993), and his recent publications include contributions on
information transmission and the main trends in international ship-
ping since 1850.

## 1
Introduction
Gelina Harlaftis, Stig Tenold and Jesús M. Valdaliso
[T]he best way to understand what was after all an
international industry is in some kind of comparative
perspective … Comparative history was very much in
the vogue in the 1960s. In the case of international
maritime history, we need to insist on the wisdom of
reviving that tradition. Only in this way will we ever
be able to understand the nature of the system that
moved goods so efficiently between continents and
hence enabled the international economy to grow and
prosper in this critical period.1
Almost a century ago, C. Ernest Fayle characterized maritime transport
as ‘the world's key industry'.2 Indeed, maritime transport has been the
main driver of trade growth and hence of the emergence and expan-
sion of a global economy. Shipping not only served to integrate world
markets, it was the international businesspar excellence in most national
economies and preceded trends that later became visible in many other
sectors of international economic activity. In consequence, an interna-
tional and comparative perspective should be a central ingredient in
both maritime history and maritime economics.3 For anyone interested
in the growth and development of the world's economy, knowledge of
the history and mechanisms of shipping provides important insights.
This volume is about the history and economics of the global ship-
ping industry as it evolved in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries.
Our aim has been to shed light on this industry's vital contribution to
the development of the global economy. Despite the accolades awarded
to shipping in the literature on the early modern period, the role of
the industry in more recent periods has traditionally been neglected or,
1

## 2 Introduction
at best, the focus has only been on its effects on international market
integration.4 Global economic history deals with connections and com-
parisons, and there are several analyses of the importance of sea trans-
port, the movements of people and goods.5 However, in the literature
on global connectedness, or globalization, the industry that, more than
any other, has made this possible is usually invisible; the functioning of
the world shipping industry is taken for granted.
As Frank Broeze pointed out, ‘[A]lthough globalisation has become
a household word, there is little recognition that our world could not
function without the complex system of maritime transport sustaining
intercontinental and regional trade'.6 This book attempts to remedy
this neglect; we want to explore the mechanisms, the institutions and
the organizational developments that have shaped the world's mari-
time industries. The coverage of the essays is wide-ranging, embracing
technological advances, freight rates, sea trade and the institutions of
shipping, and also taking into account the role of the state, port devel-
opment and maritime businesses.
The authors have joined together to create this volume as a tribute
to the scholar who, above all others, has succeeded in promoting
maritime economic history as a truly international and highly respected
discipline, Lewis R. (Skip) Fischer. Skip has been the backbone of the
international body of maritime historians. This book is a small attempt
to repay some of his efforts, enthusiasm and generosity. Many of the
contributors to this book - including its three editors - have had Skip
as a formal or informal mentor. However, the debt is not confined to
those who have been directly taught, supervised, encouraged or helped
by him. Rather, the international community of maritime historians
has a collective debt; without Skip, this would have been a very dif-
ferent, and academically much weaker, community. Fittingly, the next
chapter in this book is an appreciation by two of Skip's contemporar-
ies,Lars U. SchollandDavid M. Williams. They examine the ways in
which Skip has contributed to the progress of maritime history in the
last twenty-five years.
The emergence of a global economy is closely intertwined with the
development of the maritime industries. However, the growth of the
international economy did not offer equal opportunities to all coun-
tries. In Chapter 3, C. Knick Harley shows that shipping and related
maritime activities provided a route into the international arena for a
number of peripheral regions that, due to lack of staple exports, could
not be drawn into the rapidly growing global economy. As a result of
Malthusian economics, advantageous location and entrepreneurship,

Gelina Harlaftis, Stig Tenold and Jesús M. Valdaliso 3
shipping came to play a crucial role in the economic development of
New England and the Middle Colonies. Given the limited potential for
other types of exports, almost 40 per cent of balance of payments earn-
ings came from shipping and mercantile services around 1780.
Following the American Civil War, the new possibilities for manufac-
turing ‘exports' to the rest of the United States led to the rapid decline of
shipping in most of the region. In other peripheral areas, however, the
sector still played a vital role. The maritime regions of Canada depended
on shipping exports for a longer time than did the northeastern part of
the United States. In Europe, two small nations, Greece and Norway,
could establish important positions in world shipping based on staple-
driven developments in resource-abundant regions abroad. Indeed,
shipping became the most dynamic sector of the economy in both of
these countries, surpassing all other industries in its contribution to
export earnings well into the second half of the twentieth century.
Despite the important role of nations in the periphery that depended
on shipping, Britain dominated the industry in the late nineteenth
century, with her leading position at the start of the century further
enhanced by technological changes. The role of iron and steam gave
Britain a first-mover advantage, and in Chapter 4, John Armstronggand
David Williamsassess the progress of the steamship in the nineteenth
century. As they point out, the transition from sail to steam has been
one of the most frequently discussed topics in maritime history. The
assessment of this transition has, however, varied over time.
Armstrong and Williams show that whereas the research up to World
War II portrayed the development as dramatic, rapid and revolutionary,
subsequent analyses have tended to emphasize the gradual, even slow,
nature of the transition. They also bring a much needed empirical foun-
dation to this debate by using data on shipping movements to examine
the change from sail to steam at the aggregate and sectoral levels. The
findings reinforce the case for the transition being a gradual process.
The authors also indicate that the transition perhaps occurred later than
has been previously thought. However, they claim that viewing the
change as dramatic and revolutionary can be justified when the com-
munication dimension, rather than the transport of bulk commodities,
is considered.
The shipping sector also saw important technological changes after
the transition from sail to steam. In Chapter 5, Yrjö Kaukiainen
describes the long-term growth of technical efficiency, with an empha-
sis on developments after World War II. He provides a detailed discus-
sion of the changing nature of ‘the hardware' - the ships - and shows

## 4 Introduction
that increased efficiency was primarily related to size, rather than speed.
The technological improvements, accompanied by changes in the land-
sea interface and in the organization of the shipping industry, have
ensured that the shipping industry has been able to handle the rapid
growth of world trade. Kaukiainen points out that since the 1870s the
cost of shipping a ton of grain over a given distance has declined by 90
per cent. It is precisely this kind of efficiency improvement that sup-
ports the argument that the shipping industry has been a key factor in
increased international economic integration. If shipping costs were ten
times higher, gains from trade - and, by implication, trade itself - would
have been dramatically lower.
The questions of the cost of shipping and the improvement of tech-
nologies are also central to Chapter 6.Espen Ekberg,Even Lange and
Eivind Merokclaim that the notion that maritime transport had limited
influence on the globalization process in the second half of the twen-
tieth century is misguided. They emphasize that freight rates declined
substantially, but that this development has been masked in many
previous studies by unfortunate selection of price deflators. Moreover,
the authors posit that analyses based solely on price movements do not
fully reflect the impact of the shipping industry on international inte-
gration; the dramatic improvements in the quality of shipping services
should also be taken into account. Through analyses of technological
development in the transport of two important cargoes - dry bulk goods
and chemicals - Ekberg, Lange and Merok show the crucial role played
by shipping entrepreneurs in introducing new transport solutions. The
ingenuity, innovation and risk-taking behind such new arrangements
have ensured that the shipping industry performs its task - transporting
goods from A to B - in an increasingly efficient way.
The productivity and efficiency of shipping have increased largely
due to the formal and informal institutions of shipping that have
brought about sometimes ‘invisible' organizational changes. The pro-
duction of shipping services depends on the assessment of a chain of
institutions that developed mechanisms to safeguard trust in business
and minimized risks. Throughout the nineteenth and twentieth centu-
ries this has been an almost self-regulated business between principals
and agents. The institutions of shipping, in order to provide an unfet-
tered flow of information, took on their modern form by the second
third of the nineteenth century. In Chapter 7,Gordon Boyce discusses
the development of commercial infrastructures for the world shipping
industry and traces the evolution of those elements that in combina-
tion provided a vital commercial infrastructure for the global shipping

Gelina Harlaftis, Stig Tenold and Jesús M. Valdaliso 5
industry. Specifically, he examines the emergence of key institutions,
including the Baltic Shipping Exchange, Lloyd's of London, relevant
commodity and financial markets, and shipowner organizations, all of
which facilitated the exchanges needed to sustain oceanic transport.
These organizations supported the industry by disseminating informa-
tion, reducing uncertainty, and mitigating transaction costs. Without
them shipping would not have achieved sufficient efficiency to provide
a low-cost transport model for the world economy.
The centre of these global shipping institutions remains, to the
present day, London. This is quite impressive if one considers the spec-
tacular decrease in British shipping in the second half of the twentieth
century. Formal institutions like the state and national shipping policy
can have a profound effect on the direction of a fleet. In Chapter 8,
Sarah Palmerr examines the response of the British government to the
decline of British shipping; she stresses that state shipping policy is not
only formulated along economic lines but also along geo-political ones
related to national prestige and defence. She distinguishes and analyses
four periods: the 1950s, the 1960s and 1970s, the 1980s to the mid-
1990s and the mid-1990s to the present day. The political twists and
turns of successive British governments, committees and reports are
examined, revealing the logic - or the lack of it - of state policy towards
what was one of the country's prime economic sectors.
Seaports have been centres of global economic growth.7 The techno-
logical changes in shipping brought fundamental changes in port devel-
opment, which in their turn have formed an integral part of the process
of global economic integration. The case of steamship technology is
indicative. Another example of recent decades is containerization.8
‘The impact of containerization', wrote Frank Broeze, ‘on the ports
and port systems of the world was … revolutionary… To be part of the
new container world, ports needed to undergo a … metamorphosis.'9
Shipping factors, ship technology, size of ships and organization of
shipping services defined the development of seaports, port systems,
port hierarchies and the connectivity of sea and land. Adaptation to
new technology was not easy, however, as labour market institutions
and institutional lock-ins impeded strategic flexibility, as the next chap-
ters on ports indicate. In Chapter 9, Stephan Vanfraechem discusses
the different evolution of the two main ports of northern continental
Europe, Antwerp and Rotterdam, from a comparative and international
perspective. There has been fierce competition between the two for the
top position in Europe. This chapter investigates the different choices
they made and the ways they organized their labour in the docks when

## 6 Introduction
confronted with the rapid increase in sea traffic and technological
change. In Antwerp traditional labour organization continued to limit
efficiency, while Rotterdam was more successful in adjusting to new
demands.
Combining theory and historical evidence,Malcolm Tull andJames
Reveley in Chapter 10 write about institutional path dependence in
port regulation, comparing the ports of New Zealand and Australia.
The study applies Douglass North's idea that institutional ‘lock-in' can
impede strategic flexibility and the growth of port productivity to the
Australian and New Zealand port industries. Drawing on the authors'
historical research and ongoing programme of cross-national port com-
parisons, evidence is adduced to illustrate how this path dependence
operated. The paper applies North's theoretical framework to a key
topic - ‘sticky' port institutions - within the maritime economic his-
tory field. The chapter provides a theoretically-informed, historically-
based analysis of how institutional legacies retard the development of
efficient port governance arrangements. In a way it tells a different, yet
similar, story to that of the port of Antwerp analysed in the previous
chapter.
Global politics and national policies also have an impact on port
formation.10 For example, in the nineteenth century two competitive
port systems defined the Asiatic seas, one local Asiatic and the other
based on the shipping nodes of European trading companies and state
authorities.11 In most of the twentieth century politics defined the
port system of China, something that changed after the late 1970s. In
Chapter 11, Adolf K. Y. Ngg and Ka-chai Tam analyse the governance
and development of China's seaports from 1978 to 2002. They point
out that ‘the port question' was crucial to the economic development of
China. Based on an analysis of institutional, financial and legal reforms,
the authors show that the main challenge for the Chinese authorities
was to find the balance between government intervention and market
initiatives.
The next three chapters go into the microeconomics of the shipping
industry, offering different insights into its business organization and
the shipowners' incentives and strategies. Maritime industries, particu-
larly merchant shipping, have been a very fertile soil for the analysis of
crucial issues of economics and business, such as market cycles, collu-
sive agreements and business strategy, among many others.12 Shipping
was also the sector from which in the 1950s and 1960s a new school
of business history emerged in Britain: the Liverpool school, which in
December 1958 launched a new journal, Business History.13

Gelina Harlaftis, Stig Tenold and Jesús M. Valdaliso 7
Steam shipping, given its highly capital-intensive nature, was one of
the sectors where specialist and limited firms first appeared.14 However,
asMichaela Barnard and David Starkey stress in Chapter 12, in Hull's
maritime sector private companies persisted well into the early years of
the twentieth century. Using largely neglected company records, they
assess the extent and significance of the private company in British busi-
ness during the late nineteenth century and ask why there was appar-
ently a lack of organizational change in that period. After the passing
of 1862 Consolidating Act, company formation - already significant in
the port industry - increased in the shipping, trawling and shipbuilding
industries during the third quarter of the nineteenth century. However,
the authors' closer examination of the businesses that were registered
in this period reveals that many of these concerns were private limited
companies - a status not recognized in law until 1907. This type of
business organization exhibited a strong degree of organizational conti-
nuity with the proprietorships and private partnerships of the pre-1862
period. Barnard and Starkey test whether this business form was a ‘typi-
cal British compromise', for instance an attempt by entrepreneurs to
secure the benefits of limited liability without compromising existing
and long-established modes of organization.
Business history is, in the end, about entrepreneurs, their incentives
and strategies, and shipping has always proved a fertile field for their
analysis.15 Stig Tenold, in Chapter 13, discusses how Norwegian ship-
owners have dealt with the question of risk from a long-term perspec-
tive. Using a simple analytical framework, he shows that the manner, in
which Norwegian shipowners approached risks, has varied across time.
This was partly a result of changes in the owners' willingness to take
risks, partly a result of institutional changes. Tenold presents four ‘case
studies' from the sailing ship era up until today, where changing busi-
ness environments and specific opportunities affected the risk profile
of Norwegian shipowners. Still, Tenold argues that the two tasks at the
core of the shipping companies' business - the production and sale of
transport services and the investment in the ships necessary to produce
these services - have remained relatively constant.
Some of the earlier revisions of Chandler's paradigm of the origins
and development of the modern firm came from the analysis of the
shipping industry too. Boyce's book on the rise of large-scale enterprise
in British shipping emphasized the role of networks and other coop-
erative structures instead of the large centralized firm. Jones' book on
British trading companies brought a complementary concept to the
forefront, that of the business group.16 Chapter 14, byGelina Harlaftis

## 8 Introduction
and Jesús Valdaliso, is a comparison between Greek and Spanish ship-
ping business groups in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. Greeks
and Spaniards became low-cost tramp operators in the international
market in the first third of the twentieth century, but from the mid-
1930s onwards Greece became the world's leading shipping nation
while Spain converted into a minor player in the international shipping
market. Despite this divergent path of development the authors show
that business groups driven by entrepreneurial families have pervaded
the shipping industry of both countries even until today. Drawing
on the theoretical literature on business groups and on in-depth case
studies of the most important shipping groups in both countries, the
authors examine the origins and evolution of business groups in ship-
ping, their strategy and structure, and the factors that account for their
long-term survival, offering an historical and dynamic perspective such
as that demanded by the theorists.17
In the final chapter, the three editors discuss whether shipping is
indeed ‘the world's key industry', or whether the term ‘the invisible
industry' might be more appropriate. The basis for this alternative term
is the fact that shipping has traditionally been absent from the main-
stream research in economics and economic history, despite its major
contribution to global economic integration. The editors emphasize
that certain aspects of the shipping industry, as well as the ways in
which economic growth and development have traditionally been ana-
lysed, can explain the apparent ‘invisibility'.
We started this introduction by quoting a ‘call to arms' from Skip
Fischer and Helge Nordvik about the need for an international and
comparative perspective in the writing of maritime history. Although
the international aspect is evident in all chapters, there are fewer
explicit comparative studies in this book. Yet, the comparative interna-
tional perspective permeates all chapters. In this respect, the call to arms
has been answered. However, it would have been impossible to do this
without the creation and dissemination of knowledge that has taken
place in the 25 years after Fischer and Nordvik addressed the problem.
No person has been more important in the formation and development
of this community of maritime economic historians than Skip Fischer
himself.

## 2
Lewis R. Fischer and the Progress
of Maritime Economic History
David M. Williams and Lars U. Scholl
The editors of an important volume of essays on shipping history pub-
lished in 1990 observed that ‘the study of man's relationship with the
sea is an old and honoured topic within the historical profession. Indeed,
some of the foremost scholars of the past century have asked significant
questions about this connection.'1 For all such a long tradition, however,
the last forty years or so have seen such study gain impetus and a new
direction in the form of a focus on the economic and social dimensions
of that relationship. The shift is evident in the study of all spheres of
maritime activity - including naval - but it is most marked in the sector
of merchant shipping. Merchant shipping, for centuries considered and
interpreted from romantic, nationalistic and imperial standpoints, has
come to be examined for what it always has been, a business. Ships are
capital goods - built and operated for profit - with consequent implica-
tions for markets and all factors of production.
The forces underlying the emergence of what might be termed a ‘new'
mercantile maritime history were many. Of prime significance was the
expansion of the social sciences in the post-World War II era and, within
this, the development of economic history. This promoted the study
of all forms of economic activity, including the maritime, in a more
scientific and quantitative fashion, for economic history is above all
concerned with the measurement of trends and change. The enhanced
status of the social sciences and economic history in particular -
occurring as it did at a time of rapid expansion in higher education and
academic research - gave rise to the development of a variety of sub-
disciplines of history such as agricultural, business, industrial, labour,
transport and urban history. Similarly, maritime history emerged as a
specific field of study. Seminal publications by Hyde in 1956, by Davis
and by Sturmey in 19622 and an important paper by North in 19583
11

12 Lewis R. Fischer and the Progress of Maritime Economic History
served to encourage all those working in the field of shipping to view
their subject in a new light and, equally importantly, achieved greater
respect amongst historians generally for the maritime dimension.
Yet the profile and ‘presence on the ground' of maritime history - in
terms of a society or association, regular conferences and publication
programme - was modest compared with the other emerging ‘economic
and social' sub-disciplines of history. By the 1970s almost all of these
had, in Britain and in some other countries, formal structures embrac-
ing national associations, annual conferences and established journals.
In fairness, things might have been different if a promising journal,
Maritime History,launched in 1970 - with a distinguished editor, Robin
Craig - had not folded after a few issues due to the demise of its publish-
ers. Thus maritime history in the 1970s and early 1980s, although it had
taken on a new direction, was enjoying greater academic respectability
and was being enthusiastically prosecuted by a host of young and eager
scholars (in retrospect a remarkably productive cohort), remained a rela-
tive backwater within the overall historical context.
Today, in 2012, the potential of maritime history - evident some three
or four decades ago - has now been realized. It can boast an international
association, the International Maritime Economic History Association
(IMEHA), formed in 1988 with a worldwide membership of around 500.
The Association has its own journal, theInternational Journal of Maritime
History, a twice-yearly publication enjoying the highest esteem, now in
its twenty-fourth year.4 Published alongside the Journal is a monograph
and aids to research series, Research in Maritime History, now number-
ing some 45 volumes. The Association also organizes the International
Congress of Maritime History every four years. The first was held in
1992 in Liverpool and others have subsequently been convened in
Amsterdam/Rotterdam, Esbjerg, Corfu and Greenwich. Each Congress
has seen an increase in delegates, with over 300 at Greenwich. A sixth
meeting takes place in 2012 in Ghent, where even greater numbers
are expected. These developments represent the formal structures of a
vibrant international community of scholars engaged in maritime stud-
ies that embodies networks of support, common interests and research
projects.
Such achievements stem from the enthusiasm and work of many
scholars, some of whom have contributed greatly as office holders and
congress organizers, yet we suspect that all who have participated would
agree that their efforts individually and even collectively would not
have resulted in the present outcome had it not been for the leader-
ship, energy and career-long commitment of one individual, Professor

David M. Williams and Lars U. Scholl 13
Lewis Ross Fischer. He has been at the heart of all progress made in
the Association's creation, the editorship and management of the
Journal and Research series and as the instigator of the highly success-
ful Congress programme. And there is much more. His contribution
is particularly unique.5 The present volume, the product of a group of
international scholars, recognizes this remarkable achievement. Its title,
The World's Key Industry: History and Economics of International Shipping,
reflects Professor Fischer's field of study and, even more so, his transna-
tional and global vision.
The authors of this introduction have known Lewis R. Fischer for
some thirty years. It has been a privilege and a prized friendship. He has
been an inspiration to us both. Many others friends and scholars would
echo this and we wish to acknowledge the particular assistance of Peter
N. Davies, Gordon Jackson and John Armstrong - all friends of long
standing - for information provided.6 Lewis R. Fischer's great contribu-
tion to maritime economic history is best considered under three heads.
First, his part in the initiatives that propelled maritime history forward
and his role in publishing; these aspects are closely linked. Second, his
encouragement and support to literally hundreds of scholars worldwide
and to other organizations in maritime history. Third, his research,
which has invariably been transnational in its approach, imaginative
and innovative in analysis, and has ranged over a variety of themes.
Lewis R. Fischer is universally known as ‘Skip'. There could be no more
apposite name for a ‘leader' operating in the field of shipping history,
although there is reason to believe this nomenclature pre-dates his links
with maritime studies.7 Be that as it may, from now on, Lewis shall be
‘Skip', his favoured name and familiar to all. Skip is a very public figure
and there is no doubt that for the past three decades he has been inter-
nationally the best known figure in maritime history. And, very unusu-
ally for someone with such a high profile, he has been readily accessible
to all. However, in contrast to this very visible and public career, Skip,
in some respects, is a very private person. The authors have been friends
of Skip for decades but we - and the many other colleagues of Skip
whom we have consulted - while knowing him fully in the context of
maritime history have but little knowledge beyond this. Probably, with
innate modesty, Skip may feel that his early years and student days are
of no interest and activities outside the academic are of no importance.
We respect his privacy and have not sought early biographical details.8
Perhaps one day Skip will tell us more about these formative years, but
this tribute is concerned with the past forty years and Skip's association
with, and contribution to, maritime economic studies. As Skip himself

14 Lewis R. Fischer and the Progress of Maritime Economic History
has observed, ‘In their own hectic lives, historians too often make the
mistake of ignoring their own history'.9 This is an occasion for maritime
economic historians to rectify such an omission.
Skip's first links with maritime history and, as important, his links
with other scholars working in the field, began in the mid-1970s. The
circumstances are worth recalling. In 1966 the UK Public Record Office
decided, for reasons of cost and storage space, to dispose of much of its
collection of crew agreements and official logbooks for British vessels
from 1861-1913. The proposal aroused the concern of a number of
individuals, especially archivists and historians, for whom these records
were a valuable source for the study of the shipping industry and sea-
faring labour. Their representations met with success, the collection
was preserved and the great bulk of the agreements were transferred
to the Maritime History Archive at Memorial University, St. John's,
Newfoundland in the early 1970s. Primarily on the basis of this archive
acquisition, the Atlantic Canada Shipping Project was set up in 1976.
This aimed to study and explain the rise and fall of Atlantic Canadian
shipping in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. Included in the
research team was Skip Fischer who joined the staff of the history
department at Memorial in 1976. The Project represents a significant
development in the progress of maritime history and as such has its
own extensive literature, but three of its features are relevant here. One
was the promotion of new approaches, notably the assembly of huge
databases and the utilization of quantitative analysis.10 Within history
generally this was the era of ‘big' quantitative research projects, but
compared with many other contemporary large and costly research ven-
tures, the Atlantic Canada Project did achieve many of its aims and thus
maritime history gained a heightened profile and wider recognition.
C. Knick Harley and Yrjö Kaukiainen (amongst the most distinguished
of current maritime economic historians), who in 1988 reviewed the
Project and its outcome, referred to a ‘unique study' and ‘undeniable
contribution' to the history of merchant shipping.11 A second signifi-
cant feature of the Project was its immense published output, which was
highly regarded and eagerly received worldwide by scholars engaged in
nineteenth-century economic and social history. Skip's earliest publica-
tions in the maritime field were associated with the Project. The third
feature, perhaps of most importance here, was that the Project during its
six-year existence organized annual conferences that became ‘the center
for maritime history on an international scale.'12 These conferences
were attended by scholars from the Americas, Europe and elsewhere.
Attendees at the first in 1977 included Robin Craig, Basil Greenhill

David M. Williams and Lars U. Scholl 15
and Peter Davies, all of whom became mentors and long-term friends
of Skip. At that meeting Skip also met Ralph Davis, a scholar by whose
work he was greatly influenced, and a young Sarah Palmer, representa-
tive of a new generation of maritime economic historians.13 Many other
scholars were to attend the subsequent conferences. This interchange
was of enormous significance and value and many maritime historians
regard their links with the Atlantic Canada Project as a very special
experience. Thus Skip met up with scholars from many other institu-
tions who themselves were impressed by developments at Memorial and
particularly by the expertise and enthusiasm of Skip himself. Contacts
were made and a network of scholars interested in maritime economic
history began to emerge. Importantly, this network linked the genera-
tions and within it Skip was a vital element.
The impetus provided by the Atlantic Canada Shipping Project in
creating a new community of maritime economic historians soon bore
fruit. An important initiative was that of two distinguished historians,
Keiichiro Nakagawa and Peter Davies. They submitted a proposal to the
organizers of the ninth International Congress of Economic History for
a relatively minor ‘C Session' at Berne in August 1986. Some indication
of the progress of maritime history is that this was not only accepted
but advanced to far more prestigious ‘B' status. The story of this
session - enthusiastically attended and with some twenty presentations
(in retrospect, a very distinguished roll-call) - can be read elsewhere.14
Its outcome was a meeting that agreed to the formation of a Maritime
Economic History Group (forerunner of the IMEHA). Great stress was
laid on the fact that there was no intention to compete or usurp the
functions of established groups in the field; rather, the new group's aim
was to foster and promote communication between scholars working
in economic maritime history. Such an aim arose from the recognition
that many researching maritime themes did so in isolation and that
there was a need to promote co-operation between scholars interna-
tionally. Important initiatives were the proposal to create a register of
scholars engaged in maritime economic history and the publication
of a newsletter. A further aim espoused (and a very important one
for the future) was a proposal to seek a full session on shipping at the
tenth International Congress of Economic History in 1990. A steering
Committee - comprising Keiichiro Nakagawa, Peter Davies, Frank Broeze
and Lars Scholl - was elected, as were Skip Fischer and Helge Nordvik
as newsletter editors. Anyone familiar with the workings of academic
organizations will appreciate that, however eminent the helmsmen, the
future of the group lay with the editors. They were not to disappoint.

16 Lewis R. Fischer and the Progress of Maritime Economic History
Skip met Helge Nordvik of the Norwegian School of Economics and
Business Administration of Bergen (NHH) in the late 1970s. They soon
became close friends. Both possessed boundless energy and a belief
in the importance of maritime history. They were to collaborate pro-
fessionally in research and in the promotion of maritime history for
some twenty years and, for Skip, their association led to a longer and
ongoing association with the NHH and Norway.15 Within weeks of
their appointment as newsletter editors, Fischer and Nordvik began to
compile a register of maritime historians and the first number of the
Newsletter of the Maritime Economic History Group (incorporating a list of
scholars active in maritime economic history) appeared in March 1987,
a mere seven months after the Berne Congress meeting. Similarly it was
Fischer and Nordvik who achieved the aim of gaining a full session on
shipping at the tenth International Congress of Economic History to be
held in 1990 at Leuven and acquired support for a pre-congress session
in Bergen in 1989. These meetings brought recognition for maritime
history but, perhaps more importantly, they saw the beginnings of an
international community of scholars under the aegis and inspiration
of Fischer and Nordvik. The paper authors and commentators invited
by Fischer and Nordvik to the Bergen and Leuven sessions were drawn
from thirteen different countries and embraced North America, Europe
(north and south), Asia (Ceylon, India and Japan) and Australasia. This
reflected the determination of Fischer and Nordvik that the Association,
from the outset, should be truly international. A further aim was to
support young talent, and amongst the authors of papers were Gelina
Harlaftis and David Starkey, at the time very junior scholars, and in
retrospect, choices indicative of Fischer's perception.
To any outside observer the progress made between the Berne (1986)
and Leuven (1990) conferences represented a considerable achieve-
ment. But there was a further more remarkable development, brought
about entirely through the initiative, drive and unrelenting effort of
Fischer and Nordvik. They were encouraged by the response to the
Newsletter, for by the second year of publication this had reached more
than 800 maritime economic and social historians in over sixty nations.
Buoyed up by this success and the requests of many correspondents for
something more than a newsletter, Fischer and Nordvik, both still in
their thirties - a time when their careers might be furthered in other
ways - took the momentous step of establishing a journal. Ideas are
one thing, the practicalities are something else. Above all, for any
journal there is the matter of financial backers. Fischer and Nordvik
persuaded their respective home institutions - Memorial University

David M. Williams and Lars U. Scholl 17
and the NHH - to support this new, more ambitious venture.16 The
first issue of the International Journal of Maritime Historyy was published
in June 1989. It did not appear on the scene as a slight or experimental
venture. The editors, Fischer and Nordvik, despite their protestations of
being ‘neophytes' in journal creation and editorship, presented a vol-
ume of over 250 pages with seven substantial papers, a lengthy review
essay and 32 book reviews. This impressive first issue was fronted by
an introduction from the editors in which they set out the contexts
and aims of the Journal; we shall refer to these later.17 A second issue
appeared in December. This exceeded 400 pages and incorporated two
novel features, a Forum and a Roundtable. The format of the Forum
was to invite experts in a particular field to expound their approaches
to research and to respond to those of others. The Roundtable, took the
concept of the book review to a higher level by inviting a number of
scholars (in the first instance, eight) to consider an important new work
and to focus on a few critical issues. The author would then respond
to the critiques.18 The aim of both of these new features reflected the
intent of Fischer and Nordvik to stimulate interest and to improve the
quality of research and writing in maritime history. These innovations
became regular and much appreciated features of the Journal, but both
placed additional demands on the editors in the matters of liaison with
participants and co-ordination of contributions. Moreover, while Skip
and Helge were joint editors, as the Journal was published in St John's
the burdens of correspondence with authors and referees (in an era
before email was widespread), putting each issue together and ensuring
printing and worldwide distribution, fell on Skip.
In launching the Journal, Fischer and Nordvik, albeit with the bless-
ing of many, had undertaken a venture almost entirely on their own
initiative. They had conceived of the momentous step from newsletter
to journal, acquired the financial support and delivered impressive early
issues. Moreover, all this had been achieved with only a nominal edito-
rial board in place. This was rectified when the board met during the
Leuven conference with David M. Williams elected as its first chairman,
to be succeeded in due course by Lars U. Scholl. Nearly twenty years on,
the latter continues in this role, an ongoing source of support to Skip
and theJournal.
At this stage it might be thought that Skip, with the new Journal
launched - aiming at an international market and already setting high
standards - would have settled for a period of consolidation. Editing
the Journal involved all the demands of what seemed to many (and
would have been for most) a full-time job, but Skip also had a heavy

18 Lewis R. Fischer and the Progress of Maritime Economic History
schedule of teaching and a busy research programme involving overseas
visits and conferences. Yet within two years of the first appearance of
the Journal, far from slowing down, Skip, driven by his desire to pro-
mote maritime economic history, embarked on two other publishing
ventures. One was the creation of a journal for the Canadian Nautical
Research Society (CNRS). The Northern Mariner/Le Marin Du Nordd made
its first appearance in January 1991.19 Although smaller in length than
theJournal it was produced in a stylish format and, moreover, operated
on a schedule of four issues a year. Skip along with Olaf U. Janzen and
Gerald E. Panting were the editors.20
While serving primarily as the organ of the CNRS, the Marinerr from
the outset had an international flavour in its review section and its
remit for articles soon came to embrace not merely Canadian waters but
all northern seas. Thus a further publishing outlet for maritime writers
was provided. All this meant that Skip was now involved in the produc-
tion of six journal issues per year. The second new venture - and this
was entirely Skip's initiative - was the launch of a new series of stud-
ies entitled Research in Maritime Historyy aimed primarily, although not
exclusively, at IMEHA members who came to enjoy additional volumes
alongside the issues of theJournal. Two such volumes appeared in 1992,
the first of a series that now numbers some forty-five. The nature of
the series has been guides to records, collections of conference papers,
monographs, reprints of important works and volumes of papers hon-
ouring retiring colleagues.
The death of Helge Nordvik in 1998 came as a great blow to maritime
history. Skip flew to Bergen for the funeral, as did other international
colleagues from less far afield. He continues to mourn the loss of a
companion in the cause of promoting maritime history, a co-editor,
a research collaborator and, above all, a dear friend. In the same year,
Gerry Panting, a colleague at Memorial and one-time IMEHA treasurer,
also died.21 However, the mission of maritime history continued to be
promoted and Skip was determined to maintain and even enhance the
quality of the Journal. Assistant editors were appointed and provided
much appreciated support; again the securing of Olaf Janzen as review
editor developed what was already a good review section into by far the
biggest and best of its kind.22 But Skip continued as principal editor of
the Journal and the Research series. 2012 marks his twenty-fourth year
asJournal editor and the twenty-first year of the Research series. What a
simple, understated phrase is ‘continued as editor'. Can anyone imagine
the demands that a quarter of a century of editing a journal involves?
Enthusiasm and dedication, yes, but imagine the relentless pressure,

David M. Williams and Lars U. Scholl 19
issue after issue, year after year. And this was no ordinary journal of
a few papers in a specialist field. Skip's determination that the Journal
should be international and cover all periods and that it should be a
journal of quality and substance, ensured that, despite periodic ill health
and other pressures, there was no respite in the demands it imposed.
And these were far more than those that might be encountered by other
editors of distinguished publications whose duties cease when material
is sent to the publisher. Skip has often been directly involved with the
printers and in the process of distribution, packing and despatch of
issues. TheJournal, from its outset, has been published by the Memorial
University of Newfoundland. The continued support of this institu-
tion is something all in maritime history gratefully acknowledge. But
the finances of all universities are constantly under pressure and Skip
on occasions had to justify continued backing. In shipping terms, he
has had to serve on the bridge, in the engine room and in the count-
ing house. However, in one matter, support has been constant. On the
occasion of the first issue of theJournal, the editors paid tribute to their
editorial assistant, now Managing Editor, Margaret Gulliver, describing
her as ‘the real midwife of the International Journal of Maritime History'.
Nearly twenty-five years on, Margaret is still in post. Commenting on
a collection of research papers on ‘The Market for Seamen', Skip noted,
‘As always, Margaret managed to organize the papers, format and type-
set them, and get them into the hands of our printers with an efficiency
that stands in sharp distinction to some of the markets described in the
following papers'.23 This tribute should apply to the nearly one hundred
deliveries of issues in the Journal and the Research series. The gratitude
of all members of the IMEHA is due to Margaret for her resilience and
her support to Skip and maritime history.
By providing new opportunities for the publication of research, Skip's
endeavours have aided hundreds of scholars, but he has supported and
encouraged maritime history more directly in many other ways. Other
organizations besides the CNRS, some regionally based in Northern
Europe, the Mediterranean and elsewhere, have benefited from his
advice, assistance in the organization of, and attendance at, confer-
ences. Again his editing skills and access to publishing outlets have
often been drawn on and freely given. At the international level too, he
has been generous with time and effort. Skip and Helge never conceived
of the IMEHA as in competition with, or impinging on, the remit and
activities of any other organizations. The Association was established to
promote the economic history aspect of maritime studies, as was made
clear through the acquisition of affiliated status to the International

20 Lewis R. Fischer and the Progress of Maritime Economic History
Economic History Association at Leuven in 1990.24 An international
body already in existence at this time was the International Commission
for Maritime History (ICMH), part of the International Congress of
Historical Sciences. Skip viewed the ICMH very much as a partner in the
cause of the promotion of maritime history and indicative of this was
his service as Secretary-General of that body for five years in the early
1990s. Additionally, he has been a constant good friend of the British
Commission, one of the more active bodies within ICMH.
Skip's support, however, has been most valued and most influential
at the individual level. With him, it is a duty to assist all engaged in
maritime history and most especially young scholars, towards whom
he is profligate in his expenditure of time and effort. Indeed the three
editors of this volume would all agree that their careers have been
substantially enhanced by Skip, who has not only listened, encouraged
and advised, but assisted further by providing session opportunities at
major conferences, publishing outlets and time-consuming references
in support of applications for research project funding and academic
posts. They are but a few of the very many individuals aided by Skip.
Nor has it been merely young and promising scholars who have been
supported. Skip has equally assisted the careers of many not-so-young
scholars in the ‘no-man's land' of mid-career, where his encouragement
restored a sense of worth and purpose and, in turn, new enthusiasm in
research. Alongside such support and kindness towards others are traits
of modesty and self-effacement. Skip has always been quick to praise
the role of others and thereby to underplay his own efforts. This is evi-
denced in many of his editorials and introductions to works celebrating
senior scholars on their retirement. Their scholarship and contribution
to maritime studies is always highlighted, described as ‘special', ‘out-
standing' and ‘unprecedented.'25 Such tributes, it must be said, often sit
uneasily with the recipients. They are aware of how important Skip has
been to their lives and careers and know full well that his contribution
outstrips all others.
Although Skip has enjoyed spells as a visiting scholar and research fel-
low at institutions in Norway, Germany, Greece, Australia and the UK,
his entire career has been based at one university, Memorial, in St John's,
Newfoundland. This has been a matter of choice - opportunities and
offers to move to appointments elsewhere have been many - but he has
remained loyal to his adopted location and perhaps more significantly
to the seat of the Journal. St John's is an attractive, vibrant city but not
the most accessible and not perhaps the first location one would choose
as a base from which to make and maintain direct personal contact

David M. Williams and Lars U. Scholl 21
with fellow scholars elsewhere. Yet thanks to his endurance of count-
less transatlantic crossings, Skip is regarded as almost a ‘native' in many
European countries. This is through his close and long-term friendships
with colleagues in maritime history in many countries who have wel-
comed his visits and similarly enjoyed Skip's hospitality when they have
been in St John's. Such regular personal contacts with colleagues - in
Norway, Denmark, Finland, the Netherlands (and a special mention
here must be made of Jaap Bruijn), Germany, the UK, Greece and Spain
in particular - have been a vital element in the international maritime
history network.26
Skip's third great contribution to maritime studies has been in the
field of research. Research has always been Skip's first love; his editorial
duties and publishing and organizational activity have thus represented
a considerable sacrifice. Even so, the extent of his published work is such
that it is not possible to include a full bibliography in this volume. More
remarkable than its extent, however, is the breadth of Skip's scholarship
in terms of the range of major themes embraced. Of these, a number
may be highlighted as areas of pioneering research. These include the
shipping of the North Atlantic, national and international maritime
labour markets, the business of ship brokerage, port efficiency and port
hierarchy, and the integral role of shipping in the global economy.
Skip's first major area of maritime economic research arose from
his involvement with the Atlantic Canada Project. Many publications
flowed from this; there were surveys of the shipping of various British
North American ports and provinces,27 the presentation of volumes of
shipping records,28 considerations of the role of shipping in regional
economic development29 and general studies of shipping and mari-
time labour. All of this early research had four distinct characteristics.
Drawing on the database of the Project, it was quantitative in its
approach; analysis was rigorous and firmly rooted in economic theory;
all studies - including regional and national - were placed in an inter-
national context and comparisons with developments elsewhere served
to offer an transnational approach; and finally, papers were always
immaculately structured and clear in argument. These qualities, evident
from the outset of Skip's research career, are manifest in all his work,
and his principles of a clear statement of the issues, the presentation of
data, analysis, the wider context and balanced conclusion might serve
as a model for all.
The published works of the Project, and those of Skip in particular,
had a profound impact on other scholars. Quantitative studies were
viewed in a new light and their potential better understood through

22 Lewis R. Fischer and the Progress of Maritime Economic History
a series of conference presentations and papers on the relationship
between cliometrics and history. The emphasis on the international ele-
ment of research was also brought home by the fact that the findings
of what was a regional survey (Atlantic Canada) had such wide-ranging
implications and application.
In part it was data drawn from the Atlantic Canada Project that led
to a second major field of research, the market for, and the returns of,
maritime labour. The crew agreements included details on the hiring
of seamen and their rates of pay at ports throughout the world. Skip
had already drawn on this material for ‘The Problem of Desertion',30
which remains the seminal study of this theme, and with Nordvik he
had investigated some of the international aspects of crewing with a
paper on Norwegian sailors in the Canadian mercantile marine.31 But
Skip recognized the wider potential of such data for the study of labour
markets and rates of pay for maritime labour. In the maritime field -
and indeed more generally - labour economics was and still is to some
extent an underdeveloped sub-discipline. Skip (often in collaboration
with Nordvik) sought to rectify this neglect. In some fifteen papers the
issues of labour markets and labour's returns were examined at a variety
of levels. There were studies of these themes in a Norwegian context at
the single port, regional and national levels.32 Outside Norway there
was a study of Antwerp and national studies of the Canadian and
Indian experiences.33 And there were transnational studies embrac-
ing the Baltic grain trade, North Sea ports and international maritime
labour more generally.34 These studies - which merit collection together
in a single volume - stimulated research by others and this was behind
an important International Congress session on ‘The Market for Seamen
in the Age of Sail' in Milan in 1994, although, characteristically, Skip
suggested the impetus had come from elsewhere.35
Skip's studies of maritime wages and labour markets broke new
ground, as did another major area of his research, that of what he
termed ‘maritime middlemen' and in particular the occupation of ship-
broking. The theme of shipbroking was something of a void: not only
were there no serious scholarly studies of the profession but addition-
ally few maritime historians appeared to have any real understanding
of the function of brokers beyond their roles in vessel chartering, selling
freight space and the purchase and sale of vessels. That brokers per-
formed many other functions such as assisting shipowners in acquiring
capital and shipyard space for new vessel construction and, at the other
end of the cycle, played an important role in scrapping was unknown
to many, as was, in more recent times, broker activity in the offshore

David M. Williams and Lars U. Scholl 23
industry. This ignorance of the broker's function was rectified in a series
of papers on the important Norwegian shipbroking firm of Fearnley &
Eger. A case study of the firm's practices between 1869 and 1914 was
published in 1992.36 It was detailed in its examination of international
linkages and explained how, by the turn of the century, the company
had become one of the pioneering global broking firms. Two years later
another paper focused on the themes of economic theory and man-
agement utilizing the firm's experiences over a longer period.37 Other
papers have appeared and research continues with an eagerly awaited
monograph to come.38
Early in his career Skip became a close friend of Gordon Jackson
(author of a seminal study of British ports) and later Adrian Jarvis, the
authority on dock construction and port administration in Liverpool.
Through his interest in their work, port history has become a further
area of Skip's research; a relatively recent paper has examined a previ-
ously little-considered aspect of port operation, warehousing and its
influence on profits in the context of Liverpool docks.39 More gener-
ally he has focused on two themes: one is calculating the efficiency of
ports - a significant issue but highly problematical in terms of criteria
and measurement particularly in a comparative context. The other has
been that of the hierarchy of ports and the influence of seaport plan-
ning within this.40 Both issues are significant today in an era when
technological advances have revolutionized the work of ports and com-
petition between ports is at a transnational regional level rather than
within traditional national boundaries.
Selecting four areas of Fischer's research serves to indicate its impor-
tance and its innovative character. Moreover, the fields briefly surveyed
represent a range of very different themes. Yet this review does not do
full justice to the width of Skip's expertise. Among the many other areas
he has addressed there are papers on information flows,41 government
policies towards shipping,42 migration,43 the integration of the North
Atlantic economy,44 globalization trends and much more. Additionally,
in the field of historiography, there are papers on maritime history as a
sub-discipline, its role and its progress.45
Lewis R. Fischer's career is one of enormous achievement. He has
provided an institutional framework for an important sub-discipline of
history; he (with Helge Nordvik) had the courage to create a significant
academic journal and individually he has devoted twenty-five years to
its editing and enhancing its status; he has been the lynchpin in the
development of a body of maritime history scholars that spans genera-
tions and perhaps uniquely (through his example) is characterized by

24 Lewis R. Fischer and the Progress of Maritime Economic History
friendship and enthusiasm for the research of others rather than rivalry
and self-interest. Alongside all these, in his research he has pioneered
new approaches to the study of previously unexplored themes in mari-
time and global history.
Such a record is apparent to all associated with maritime history,
yet one suspects that Skip would deny the extent of his achievement.
Perhaps it is worth reminding him of the goals for maritime history
when, back in 1989, he launched the Journal. He enunciated four key
aims.46 These were promoting the recognition that maritime industries
are transnational and hence encouraging a comparative perspective;
emphasizing the social and economic contexts within which maritime
industries operate; encouraging maritime historians to place their stud-
ies within the context of the broader questions being posed by histori-
ans in general; and finally, shifting maritime history from the fringes
to the centre of the historical profession. Viewing mercantile maritime
history today, it must be said that in all such regards great progress
has been made over the past thirty years. One special feature of that
progress that embraces all of the listed aims is the new recognition of
the maritime dimension within the mainstream history focus on global
history. This volume in its overall theme and in the approach, analysis
and subjects of its constituent chapters embodies Skip's vision and,
more so, his achievement of a more international, more truly economic
and more widely recognized role for maritime history.

## 3
Shipping and Staple Economies
in the Periphery
C. Knick Harley
Shipping was an important economic activity in various parts of the
periphery during the process of globalization that began with the
European voyages of discovery and lasted until the closing of the con-
tinental frontiers early in the twentieth century. This important ship-
ping activity in the periphery contrasted with the dominance of ocean
shipping in the late nineteenth century that Britain, the dominant core
economy, experienced. Generally globalization before the late twenti-
eth century involved a manufacturing core area trading with a staple
raw material producing periphery. Although India was central to the
imperialism of this period, prototypically, the periphery consisted of
areas of recent settlement where discovery, growing core demand and
falling transportation costs created potential profit opportunities for
staple production (sugar and tobacco in the eighteenth century and
grain, meat and agricultural/industrial raw materials in the nineteenth).
Exploitation of these opportunities involved the mobilization of capital
and labour and its movement to the staple periphery. This process has
been systematized in the staple theory of economic development.1
Although a considerable portion of the recent literature about glo-
balization before the late twentieth century has focused on the late
nineteenth century, I want to go back to the emergence of the Atlantic
economy in the seventeenth century in my discourse. From the early
European empires through to at least World War I, an expanding fron-
tier of settlement was a key element in increasing globalization. The
globalization process has often been fruitfully envisaged as a process
in which the core economies, particularly of Europe, have their influ-
ence over peripheral economies. Often this relationship is analysed in
the framework of imperialism and power relationships; however, as an
economist, I wish to concentrate on market forces.
29

30 Shipping and Staple Economies in the Periphery
In the frontier economies, quintessentially in the Americas and
Australia but elsewhere as well, the outstanding characteristic of the
periphery has been its land and resource abundance. The economic
history of these regions, although it must encompass power relation-
ships and imperialism, fundamentally involves the process of exploit-
ing this land abundance. In its simplest form - as exemplified by the
slave economies of tropical and sub-tropical America in the seventeenth
and particularly the eighteenth centuries - the process began with the
discovery of resources that were quickly perceived as valuable. If capital
and labour could be applied to exploiting these resources at anything
close to their opportunity cost in the core, there were profits to be
made. Thus European entrepreneurs brought capital to the sugar and
tobacco colonies and organized the supply of labour. It is, of course,
one of economic history's most distressing features that although there
was initially recruitment of European labour utilizing the institution of
indentured service, the entrepreneurs of the periphery found that the
cheapest way to mobilize labour was through the African slave trade.
This resource abundance emerged as the slave-based trading econo-
mies of the eighteenth century that produced sugar and tobacco for
European consumption.
With the coming of the Industrial Revolution technological improve-
ments further extended the prospects for globalization and staple-based
peripheral economies. The first great impact came from the explosion of
demand for raw cotton that arose from the dramatic cost reductions in
textile production following on from Arkwright's discoveries. The rapid
urbanization and population growth that accompanied the Industrial
Revolution also put upward pressure on food prices in the core, increas-
ing prospects for primary production in the periphery. In addition,
and perhaps most importantly, the Industrial Revolution's innovations
in iron and steel and in steam engines radically reduced the cost of
distance. In consequence, grain staple-based expansions occurred dur-
ing the nineteenth century, perhaps most spectacularly in the North
American interior but equally importantly in the northern and western
littoral of the Black Sea.
Distance was an important and inherent part of the core-periphery
globalization from the sixteenth to the nineteenth centuries. Throughout
this period, frontiers of the global economy existed. Frontiers occurred
at the limits of profitable staple production where the cost of trans-
portation created by distance offset the economies of land abundance.
As globalization proceeded the proportion of transportation and other
distribution costs in the final price paid by consumers increased.

## C. Knick Harley 31
In this environment, the demand for shipping grew with the expansion
of globalization.
Most of the involvement of the periphery in the global economy
can fit into a staple-theory framework where raw materials created
opportunities for exports to the metropolitan core. There were impor-
tant exceptions, however. For example, the seventeenth-century New
England and northern mainland American colonies lacked natural
resources to attract settlement. Yet, at least in hindsight, these areas
emerged as the most successful of the regions of European expansion
in the age of discovery. In these regions, there were no export prod-
ucts based on abundant raw materials that initially commanded high
prices in the core, as was the case in the staple economies. Instead,
shipping made up an important part of these colonies' business with
the international economy. Although the availability of shipbuilding
materials played a small part in this success, the fundamental econom-
ics was different from that of staple-induced settlement. Somewhat
later two economies in the European periphery, Greece and Norway,
also entered the globalizing economy as important sellers of shipping
services.
The eighteenth-century British Atlantic economy
Staple-based economies lie at the centre of core-periphery relation-
ships that characterized the rise of globalization. The colonies that the
European powers developed in the Americas are key examples. The
European discovery and subsequent exploitation of the resources of the
western hemisphere was a unique example of a major discontinuity in
economic history. In the Old World there had, of course, been ebbs and
flows of resource development and there were many staple-producing
areas. Discovery of mineral sources, pacification of areas previously sub-
ject to control of rival empires or lawlessness, new trading connections
and the rapid expansion of demand from core areas had created periods
of staple development driven booms. Nonetheless, longstanding shared
knowledge and trading ties implied that the disequilibria that these
booms responded to were of limited magnitude and duration. The New
World was different because it had lacked the centuries-long trading ties
and the shared knowledge of the Old World.2 Consequently, the extent
of the initial disequilibrium was much greater than those that occurred
in resource booms in the Old World. The adjustment to equilibrium,
in which the returns to mobile factors of production equalled their
opportunity costs in the Old World and, if property rights were well

32 Shipping and Staple Economies in the Periphery
Supply SR0
established, rents accrued to the ownership of resources, took much
longer to become established.
It is perhaps helpful to conceptualize these staple colonies' develop-
ment with the aid of a simple supply and demand diagram, presented in
Figure 3.1. If we take, for example, the case of sugar, initially Old World
supply was highly constrained (SR0) and the price was very high (P0).
Sugar, for example, was more a spice than an item of staple consump-
tion. The Portuguese were the first to perceive that there were poten-
tials for low-cost sugar production in the New World using techniques,
including the African slave labour, which they had employed in their
Atlantic islands. We can conceive the situation at that point of initial
exploitation as a shift from the equilibrium of the demand curve and
the limited supply curve to one in which there was a poorly defined
potential (once capital and labour had been mobilized) New World
supply with costs of production that lay somewhere in the shaded area
of the diagram. Of course, because there were myriad uncertainties this
potential supply curve was only vaguely comprehended. As develop-
ment occurred, capital and labour were deployed in sugar production,
output increased (SR1) and price fell (P1). At the same time, the condi-
tions of the long-run supply curve became clearer. Eventually the sugar
economy reached an equilibrium at Q* and P*. The spectacular phase
ecirP

## C. Knick Harley 33
of the staple colony's development that involved the mobilization of
capital and labour to exploit the newly discovered valuable abundant
resource expanded production from QQQ0 to Q*.
Once the original disequilibrium was overcome, a staple colony
might continue to grow as a result of outward shifts in the demand
curve caused by changing tastes, growing income and population, the
misfortunes of rival producers, shifts in supply caused by technological
change, and so on. The region might also decline as a result of various
forces such as soil exhaustion or pest infestation or the superior for-
tunes of competitors. At this point, however, the economic history of
the region has lost its most of its initial characteristics as an expanding
staple economy.
In the Americas the greatest of the staple expansions occurred in
the West Indies. The islands developed a sugar monoculture econ-
omy between the last decades of the seventeenth century and the
mid-eighteenth century. It was infamously characterized by the devel-
opment of the transatlantic African slave trade as a massive commercial
enterprise that supplied the labour necessary to initially exploit the
islands' potential. The slave trade continued through the eighteenth
century and into the nineteenth in order to maintain a labour force that
failed to maintain itself by natural reproduction. Great fortunes were
made particularly in the early days. By the second half of the eighteenth
century the sugar colonies had become an integral part of the broader
European economy.
Similar development occurred in tobacco production, as European
colonists discovered tobacco smoking from the Native Americans and
undertook its commercial production. Tobacco-based export colo-
nies expanded in the Chesapeake Bay region of the North American
mainland. Tobacco production lacked the economies of scale that
characterized sugar plantations and the economy was less dominated
by large plantations. The demographic regime was less harsh and the
slave population grew by natural increase. The region also continued to
support a white population made up of indentured servants past and
present. It nonetheless exhibited the expansive character of a staple
economy although with the horrors of West Indian slavery somewhat
attenuated.
Northern colonies: Malthusian dynamic
British America differed importantly from the other empires in the
Americas not in the nature of its staple colonies but because it included

34 Shipping and Staple Economies in the Periphery
substantial colonial populations in colonies on the northern North
American mainland where the economic dynamics differed from those
of the staple regions. These colonies, of course, flourished and became
the centre of the eventual dynamic growth of the United States.
New England and the middle colonies evolved mainly from the
migration of groups of English and other European settlers interested
in creating independent existence in the New World. Their growth was
based primarily on subsequent demographic growth that was unim-
peded by agricultural diminishing returns, in contrast to the staple
colonies where potential profits from exploitation of staple products
drew capital and, through the slave trade, labour. Much of the historical
literature of the northern colonies, in McCusker and Menard's words,
‘locates the central dynamics of American history in internal demo-
graphic processes that account for the principal characteristics of the
colonial economy: the rapid and extensive growth of population, of set-
tled area and of aggregate output combined with the absence of major
structural change'.3 The involvement of these Malthusian colonies in
Atlantic trade was thus different.
Figure 3.2 provides a way of conceptualizing the trading experience of
these colonies. The colonist demanded European commodities - their main
source of manufactured goods and luxuries - and the amount demanded
increased as these goods became cheaper, thus a downward-sloping
