demand curve. This demand curve is drawn in the diagram as down-
ward-sloping in terms of the price of imported goods relative to the
price of domestic goods (and wages) in the colonies. It is downward-
sloping but may have been quite inelastic given the difficulties of
providing colonially produced substitutes for European goods so that
higher prices might have only marginally reduced desired purchases.
The equilibrium colonial price level and import quantity is determined
by the intersection of this curve with a second curve representing
foreign exchange earnings for exports as a function of the local price
level. It is appropriate to think of this curve as having two distinct
segments - one with a clear upward slope and one nearly horizontal.
The upward-sloping segment represents the sale in Europe of colonial
produce. Transportation costs generally precluded the sale of temperate
agricultural products and timber which were abundant in the colonies
except at very low prices in the colonies (in fact, European prices were
often less than transportation costs from America).4 There were some
valuable American goods, to be sure. Furs and fish found European mar-
kets but by the eighteenth century the earnings from these were modest
relative to the demands of the rapidly increasing colonial population.
However, the presence of the Atlantic economy presented a substantial
demand for foodstuffs, timber and so on in the West Indies and for
shipping services. At the appropriate level of colonial prices and wages,
this demand was extremely elastic since the colonies were marginal
suppliers in a much larger imperial market. In the diagram this demand
shows up as the near horizontal segment of the supply curve for foreign
exchange. This horizontal demand determined the colonial price level
and the volume of imports (at QQQ (indicating presence of the West
WI
Indies)as opposed to QQQ which would have prevailed in the absence
No WI
of West Indian trade).
If we look at Shepherd and Walton's estimate of the American balance
of payments just before the Revolutionary War (Table 3.1) we quickly
see the importance of the elastic demand for goods shipped within the
Atlantic Empire and services involved with shipping in the imperial
economy for the northern colonies.5 Exports to Britain were small (less
than 10 per cent of estimated foreign exchange receipts). Exports of
temperate staples to the West Indies were important (about a third of
receipts) and supported the islands' specialization in staple production.
The islands would have found these commodities much more expensive
without the resources of the northern colonies and suffered when the
mainland colonies were excluded from imperial trade by the colonists'
victory in the American Revolutionary War. That said, however, nearly

## C. Knick Harley 37
providing opportunities. Entrepreneurship also mattered, but the elas-
ticity of the services that the large imperial trading economy required
provided opportunities.
After the United States achieved independence, entrepreneurs in the
former northern mainland colonies found their access to the imperial
economy limited by the Navigation Acts. However, the needs of both
the West Indian staple colonies and the northern mainland ensured
that shipowners in the northern states of the newly independent nation
continued to provide services to the Atlantic economy. When the great
wars in Europe broke out following the French Revolution, the oppor-
tunities expanded for neutral shipping from the United States until war
broke out with Britain in 1812.
The war and the subsequent adoption of the protective tariff by the
United States turned northern entrepreneurs' attention away from
maritime pursuits and inward to the new nation; the Boston Associates,
who set up the integrated cotton mills at Waltham and then Lowell,
Massachusetts and strongly promoted the tariff, had made their for-
tunes from the maritime trades. The effect of the tariff in turning entre-
preneurial attention from maritime activities was only partial, however.
The lure of the national market seems to have been offset by the
opportunities presented by the creation of another of the great staple
trades in a relatively nearby region. Cotton cultivation in the American
South emerged in response to the technological changes that radically
cheapened production of cotton textiles in Britain. During the first half
of the nineteenth century American ships achieved international pre-
eminence in large measure as a consequence of their success in captur-
ing the business of shipping American raw cotton to Europe.
A striking aspect of nineteenth-century maritime history is the rapid
decline of the American merchant marine after the American Civil War.
The Northern merchant marine was hurt by the war and the increased
risks it brought. Beyond the war, however, the 1860s marked a sharp
demarcation between American maritime success and the country's
almost complete abandonment of maritime activity thereafter. There
was a range of causes of which technological change in the form of
iron ships and steam propulsion was paramount. Equally important,
however, was the disappearance of the conditions that had nurtured
shipping from colonial times. Starting with the war of 1812 and rein-
forced by the protective tariff that followed (strongly reinforced during
the Civil War), New England's economic situation changed radically.
The protective tariff created a customs union in which the Northeast,
although its costs of manufacturing production exceeded those in

38 Shipping and Staple Economies in the Periphery
Britain, became the manufacturing core.6 Manufacturing exports to the
rest of the United States tended to offer better prospects of fortune than
maritime pursuits, and entrepreneurial enterprise was directed towards
manufacturing for the rapidly growing country. At the same time wages
and costs rose above those prevailing in other maritime centres and
American ships lost the competitive advantage that had persisted from
colonial times.
The Malthusian pattern of developing maritime activity as a way
to finance imports did not entirely disappear from North America in
the final decades of the nineteenth century. Within the United States,
Maine and New Hampshire, which remained largely outside the manu-
facturing region, continued maritime activities, now however confined
almost exclusively to the coastal trade of the United States, which was
protected from international competition. Further east and outside
the United States customs union, the maritime regions of Canada still
lacked good alternative methods of financing imports, did not experi-
ence the rising wages of the United States and found it advantageous to
exploit international shipping opportunities.
Greece
Greece's economic history since the mid-seventeenth century initially
appears completely different from that of the United States. In the
eighteenth century, most of Greece was incorporated in the Ottoman
empire (with some islands remaining in the declining Venetian empire).
The peninsula and the islands had modest resources and low incomes.
Within this environment, however, entrepreneurial groups developed
a vibrant and internationally competitive shipping industry. The
underlying dynamics appear to share important characteristics with
the shipping success of the northern mainland colonies of colonial
British America. The absence of an exportable staple and a fragmented
low-income domestic market provided few opportunities for entrepre-
neurship domestically. However, the international economy provided
a potentially expansive market that might be exploited.7 Furthermore,
like the northern mainland colonies Greece proved conveniently situ-
ated to exploit the opportunities that an expanding staple economy
presented for distribution services.
Greeks, with their long indented coastline and myriad islands, of
course, had a long maritime tradition. This had manifested itself within
the Ottoman Empire and also in Venetian Levantine trade.8 The emer-
gence of Greek shipping as a modern success story coincided with the

## C. Knick Harley 39
expansion of a staple frontier whose location enhanced the opportuni-
ties available to Greek shipping entrepreneurs. The staple frontier in
question lay in the grain producing region along the western and north-
ern shores of the Black Sea. The frontier developed after the Russians
acquired the north shore of the Black Sea as a result of victory in the
Russo-Turkish War in the 1770s, the Treaty of Kutchuk-Kainardji in 1774
and subsequent treaties that opened access to the Black Sea. The Ukraine
emerged as a grain frontier and Greek entrepreneurs seized the opportu-
nity to provide shipping and mercantile services. Entrepreneurial mari-
time families from the Greek islands quickly emerged as the mercantile
class in the newly developed ports of southern Russia and developed
connections to the main ports of western Europe. Similar developments
occurred in the Danubian provinces, with Greek entrepreneurial fami-
lies again playing leading roles. As staple-oriented settlement grew in
the grain producing regions and population growth in western Europe
sustained and enhanced demand, so too did Greek shipping.
Greek exploitation of the largely location-free maritime opportuni-
ties that accompanied staple-driven development in a newly opened
resource-abundant region was arguably even more remarkable than the
experience of the northern colonies of British America. Entrepreneurial
Greek shipping families played seminal roles in the creation of the
independent Greek state in the 1830s. Recent estimates of Greek ship-
ping earnings in international trade suggest that they equalled a third
of the land-based income of the country in the years from the mid-
1830s to the mid-1850s and remained above ten per cent of land-based
income for another generation. During these years shipping was the
most dynamic sector of an economy most of whose income was still
dominated by a traditional primary sector.9 Greek shipowners were in
intimate contact with the world economy, competing with the shipping
of the most advanced countries and establishing offices in leading com-
mercial centres in Europe.
Equally remarkably, the Greek shipowners successfully made the
transition to steam shipping during the last decades of the nineteenth
century.10 This involved familiarization with new technologies and
capital investment on a much larger scale than previously. Despite the
potential obstacles, the registered tonnage of steam ships exceeded that
of sailing vessels by the beginning of the twentieth century. The Greek
entrepreneurs took advantage of their connections to the international
economy. Initially they built a steam fleet with second-hand vessels
and then proved themselves capable of exploiting the opportunities
of the international market even though the technology had shifted.

40 Shipping and Staple Economies in the Periphery
American shipowners did not successfully make that transition but the
Greeks were not distracted by opportunities provided by a dynamic and
protected domestic market.
Norway
Norway provides yet another example of this phenomenon of a
resource-deprived economy successfully exploiting opportunities pro-
vided by maritime connections to the wider global economy, interme-
diated by staple trades. Much of Norway has meagre resources but easy
access to the sea. Again we see the economy's dynamic elements emerg-
ing from the maritime economy. Of course, Norway had long been
involved in the northern fisheries that had provisioned Europe since
medieval times. In the modern period, staple trades developed in the
Baltic to supply the dynamic North Sea economies of the Netherlands
and Britain. Large staple export trades in timber and grain developed on
the northern and southern shores of the Baltic. In the nineteenth cen-
tury this trade was augmented by the export of coal from the coalfields
of the northeast of England. Norwegian shipping entrepreneurs tapped
into this trade which provided much of the dynamics of the economy.
Conclusion: shipping centres
For a number of peripheral regions that lacked staple exports to draw
them into the expanding global economy, shipping and related mari-
time activities provided a route into the international arena. These
regions' lack of export staples implied that their wages and other costs
of production were low in terms of internationally traded goods.11 In
these cases, when suitable entrepreneurship was present, substantial
shipping industries developed, connected to staple-exporting regions.
These periphery shipping centres could become quite large and casual
observation of twentieth-century shipping suggests that they can come
to dominate the industry, or at least the tramp shipping part of it, in
certain circumstances. The late nineteenth-century decline in shipping
that accompanied the emergence of the United States not only as a
major staple exporter in grain as well as cotton but also a major indus-
trial economy is not surprising in this context. The attraction of ship-
ping as an earner of foreign exchange had disappeared.
Nonetheless, Britain, the world's leading industrial nation, dominated
shipping in the late nineteenth century. In the context of shipping eco-
nomics discussed here this is something of a surprise and merits a few

## C. Knick Harley 41
speculative remarks. British leadership certainly had underlying support
that differed from the simple static comparisons of relative costs that
have been emphasized here. Rather it seems to have rested on features
that we associate with the dynamics of growth. Of course, shipping is a
very capital-intensive business and Britain undoubtedly had the cheap-
est capital in the nineteenth-century world economy. Technological
change enhanced the British shipping position. Relatively cheap iron
and the improvements of the steam engine that were features of the
Industrial Revolution had their origins in Britain and British shipping
reaped first-mover advantages from proximity to these innovations. In
the middle of the nineteenth century, British shipping had been losing
out to American shipping in the Atlantic and to Scandinavian shipping
in the Baltic. However, as iron ships and steam ships became the norm,
the British advantage grew. In addition, British shipowners benefited
from Britain's position at the centre of world trade. Institutions to
finance, insure and manage shipping worldwide developed in Britain
in connection with this centrality to trade. Shipping firms with access
to knowledge present in the shipping districts of London benefited.
However, this knowledge was not exclusive to British firms. Greek ship-
ping firms early set up offices in London and shared the information
available. Ultimately, it is probably not surprising that British shipping
dominance declined and less central economies played major roles in
shipping in the twentieth century. As iron and steam became estab-
lished technologies, Britain's first-mover advantage lost much of its
impact. The information from London's financial, shipping and com-
modity markets became increasingly globalized.

## 4
An Appraisal of the Progress
of the Steamship in the
Nineteenth Century
David M. Williams and John Armstrong
The invention and subsequent development of the steamship represents
a great watershed in maritime transport and humankind's relationship
with the sea. For the first time, vessels were not at the mercy of wind
or tide and this, together with the ability to make or leave port at will,
permitted scheduled services. These, to a considerable degree, overcame
the uncertainties that previously had inevitably accompanied maritime
voyages. Over time all elements of shipping - vessels, labour, finance -
were transformed and likewise shipbuilding, the port industry and asso-
ciated shipping services. Steam together with changes in construction
materials - from wood to iron and later steel - along with later advances
in communications and transport technology, the telegraph and the
railway, created a ‘new world of shipping'.1 Such changes contributed
to the integration of the world economy.
The change from a time when steamships did not exist to one where
sail was virtually inconsequential occurred during the long nineteenth
century. To contemporaries it was an exciting transition and histori-
ans have subsequently viewed the process as a remarkable example
of technological change and a vital element in early trends toward a
world economy and globalization. For such reasons there is an exten-
sive literature on the steamboat. Indeed, only recently, Professor Yrjö
Kaukiainen observed, ‘Hardly any other phase in the history of shipping
has received as much scholarly attention as the transition from sail to
steam'.2 This literature, which inevitably focuses on the British experi-
ence, is of considerable interest in historiographical terms as it falls into
two periods, each with their particular characteristics.3
In the nineteenth century, and indeed up until World War II, the rise
of steam was viewed as revolutionary and rapid, a shining example of
technological advance, especially British technology, in the Victorian
43

44 The Progress of the Steamship
era. Contemporary commentators and historians who subsequently
reviewed the changeover to steam saw no need whatsoever to chart, let
alone analyse, the advance; steam's growing superiority over old ways
was obvious and self-evident. The process was speedy. Hence the pio-
neer social scientist G.R. Porter in his famous The Progress of the Nation
observed, ‘since 1813 the progress of this invention has been rapid to
a degree that could never have been anticipated'.4 Likewise, John Scott
Russell, provider of the entry on steam navigation in 1840 for the pres-
tigiousEncyclopaedia Britannica, stressed the speed and wide advance of
the steamboat. He noted ‘the whole surface of our globe is now being
rapidly encircled by a network of lines of communication by steam'.5
Early historians of shipping similarly expressed wonder at steam's
advance. Lindsay, the first serious historian of merchant shipping,
writing in the 1870s, devoted his last and fourth volume - over 650
pages - entirely to the steamship.6 John Kennedy's seminal History of
Steam Navigation, first published in 1903, commenced with the sen-
tence, ‘There is not a more fascinating page in history than that which
tells of the growth of the Mercantile Steam Navies of the world'.7 One
final observation comes from Robert Thornton's 1939 study British
Shipping.He, perhaps with undue emphasis on the Atlantic crossing,
viewed ‘the unchallenged existence of the sailing ship' as defeated by
1838.8 This was clearly an exaggerated view but it demonstrates that
before World War II, commentators had no doubts as to the rapidity of
the progress of steam.
The literature on the first century or so of the steamship effectively
comprised uncritical, qualitative comment. This was understandable.
Sail had seemingly been swept from the seas in the course of the nine-
teenth century and this remarkable shift was underway, or later had
taken place, during the lifetimes of many commentators. That this was
a rapid change, certainly in the long run of history, seemed self-evident
and required no assessment or analysis.
This complacent, and from a British standpoint, self-congratulatory
view, was called into question in the post-World War II period. The
two most significant studies offering new perspectives on the transi-
tion from sail to steam appeared over forty years ago. These introduced
a cautionary note into the story of rapid progress. Interestingly both
were by Canadian scholars, G.S. Graham and C. Knick Harley. The
third quarter of the nineteenth century is often regarded as the water-
shed in the shift of sail to steam; however, this was a period when
the Canadian merchant marine (all sailing vessels) expanded rapidly
and rose to become the fourth-ranked mercantile navy in the world.9

David M. Williams and John Armstrong 45
Understandably, Canadian scholars, with their own national experience
in mind, queried the pace and extent of the advance of steam.
Graham's 1956 paper, ‘The Ascendancy of the Sailing Ship 1850-85',
took the form of a corrective to the traditional view.10 Graham did
not deny the remarkable advance of steam; indeed in a paragraph -
subsequently often ignored - he provided a succinct summary lauding
steam's penetration by the mid-century.11 However, he then proceeded
to explain how and why the transition to steam ‘was not completed for
another three decades or more after 1850' and stressed that the sailing
ship enjoyed its ‘ascendancy'during the third quarter of the nineteenth
century. Graham claimed that under pressure from steam (albeit the
attainment of technical perfection in the steamship was a gradual and
long-drawn-out process) sailing ships became more efficient, especially
on very long voyages carrying low-value, high-bulk products.12
Knick Harley's study, ‘The shift from sailing ships to steamships,
1850-1890: a study in technological change and its diffusion', appeared
in 1971.13 Again focusing on the bulk trades, Harley stressed that the
shift to steam was more a process than an event and reinforced this
viewpoint by suggesting that this technological change took place over
the period 1850 to 1890 or even 1850 to 1900.14 He emphasized that
‘this displacement of sail by steam occurred gradually'' but did not com-
ment explicitly on whether this should be seen as a slow or fast process.
However, unlike many other commentators, Harley provided - in both
text and a chart - some indication of the timing of the shift according
to trade and route.15
Subsequently, over the next thirty years or so, other writers, influ-
enced by the work of Graham and Harley, took these qualifications to
the progress of steam a stage further. Recurring themes were the slow
adoption of the steamship and comparisons with the sailing ship in
terms of market share. Thus Greenhill commented that ‘the steam-
ship . . . .was a very long time coming to fruition as a vehicle for ocean
transport in competition with the sailing ship in all the trades of the
world'.16 Similarly, Sarah Palmer observed that ‘the progress of steam
was not impressive' and remarked upon its ‘lack of progress in relation
to sail'.17 Somewhat later in the 1990s, Robert Gardiner, editor of The
Advent of Steam, sub-titled The Merchant Steamship before 1900, viewed
the coming of steam as ‘a complex and long drawn out affair'.18 Support
for this came from David Starkey, who referred to ‘seemingly protracted
development' and sought ‘the reasons why steamship development was
apparently tardy'.19 Other writers, while not passing judgement on the
pace of change, have stressed the gradual nature of the transition.20

46 The Progress of the Steamship
Thus the historiography of the advance of steam comprises the
unqualified accounts of progress from writers up to around 1940 and,
from the 1950s, suggestions that sail more than held its own for much
of the nineteenth century and that steam's advance was a gradual one.
From the latter standpoint it was but a short move to infer that steam's
progress, in the sense of taking over shipping, might be viewed as
slow.
However, almost all those who have written on the shift from sail
to steam have shared a preference for subjective comment rather than
objective analysis. True, there has been some resort to statistics, such as
proportions of sail and steam in registered tonnage, new ship registra-
tions or within shipbuilding returns, but such aggregate measures are
somewhat crude, often misleading and offer nothing in terms of analys-
ing the process of the shift or sectoral analysis by region or commodity.21
Harley perhaps comes closest to this with his chart of when steam
became competitive with sail in the context of bulk trades. However, this
is not the same as when steamactually came to predominateein particular
trades and regions. As far as we can ascertain, Harley never attempted
fully to test his thesis in terms of a statistical analysis, perhaps because
his key interest was that of freight rates and their constituent elements
rather than the technological shift in practice. This study attempts an
examination of the change from sail to steam at aggregate and secto-
ral levels using data on shipping movements. The examination was
embarked upon with no preconceptions as to what might emerge; the
intention was that its outcome would provide scope for analysis and a
firmer basis for the assessment of the progress of steam.
Shipping movements comprise the entrances of vessels into, and the
clearances of vessels from, a particular location. Historians of British
shipping are blessed with a magnificent set of data on shipping move-
ments. These are located in the official record. The first publication of
the Annual Statement of Trade and Navigation in 1853 provided more
detailed and fuller statistics on trade and shipping than ever before. The
first volume contained over 150 tables, 28 of which related to shipping,
including material on shipping movements that distinguished between
vessels entering and clearing with cargoes and in ballast and, of espe-
cial interest in this context, those propelled by sail and those by steam.
Such data on shipping movements was to be continued on a yearly basis
in the Annual Statement of Trade and Navigation until 1870. Thereafter,
when trade and navigation were separated into two volumes, it was
to appear in the Annual Statement of the Navigation and Shipping of the
United Kingdom.22

David M. Williams and John Armstrong 47
The data on shipping movements comprised details of entrances and
clearances of vessels with cargo and in ballast (together and separately)
both nationally and for individual ports. Shipping movements ‘coast-
wise' and those ‘with foreign countries and British overseas possessions'
were also presented separately. In the case of foreign and overseas move-
ments, the country of the port of departure of incoming vessels and the
destination of outgoing vessels was listed. Sail and steam were also dis-
tinguished as was the nationality of vessels - British or ‘foreign' - with
‘foreign' sometimes broken down into individual nations. The material
thus represents a remarkable storehouse of data on the growth, pattern
and distribution of British trade, shipping and port activity and, within
this overall picture, the progress of technological change, the relative
roles of sail and steam.
Nevertheless, like all great bodies of statistics, the data on shipping
movements requires caution in handling and interpretation. For exam-
ple, if one considers figures of total entrances and clearances of vessels
with cargo and in ballast, the figures, albeit not identical, are remarka-
bly close in both number of vessels and total tonnage terms.23 However,
while overall entrances and clearances correspond, there is a significant
difference between entrances and clearances ‘with cargo only' and ‘with
cargo and in ballast'. There are two important aspects here. First, that
in the definitions used by the compilers, vessels that carried ‘passengers
only' were categorized as in ballast. Second, and this accounts for the
greater discrepancy for entrances than clearances, from the 1860s on,
and especially post-1870, Britain developed a growing coal export trade
(particularly for steamship bunkers). This led to numbers of vessels sail-
ing in ballast from Europe to Britain in order to pick up a coal cargo.
For the same reason vessels from foreign ports arriving in Britain with a
cargo, having once unloaded, then sailed in ballast coastwise to a coal
export port, particularly Cardiff, to acquire an outward cargo.24
Table 4.1 reveals that entrances of steam came to exceed sail during
the 1870s and that thereafter steam's advance and the demise of sail
was rapid. In 1880, entrances of sail with cargo and in ballast were lit-
tle more than half those of steam, and a decade later sail entrances had
shrunk below 20 per cent of the total. By the turn of the century, sail
was virtually irrelevant.
Yet this aggregate picture of the rapid progress of steam and demise of
sail needs to be treated with some caution, for data on shipping move-
ments contain some inherent distortions. Most notably, it gives undue
prominence to short-distance trades and vessels that undertook passen-
ger and mail transport only.25 Hence in the overall picture of overseas

incoming bulk trades (the prime focus of study in the past); addition-

include the crucial passenger liner trades that would otherwise have
been omitted.
Table 4.2 depicts the percentage of steam participation in tonnage
terms of entrances of British and foreign vessels ‘with cargo and in
ballast' into United Kingdom ports by region, 1855-1910.27 In the
analysis that follows, we have chosen to focus on tonnage entrances as
opposed to entrances of the number of ships (although this is presented
in Table 4.3) as tonnage represents a truer measure of the extent of
participation, given that steamships on average were of greater tonnage
than sailing vessels. The data, listed in the original source by country, is
presented on a regional basis as this permits a clearer and more coherent
impression of trends.28 The regions have been chosen to enable a world-
wide survey and the constituent countries of each region in most cases
comprise the shipping movements of the region in their entirety or, if
not, to all intents and purposes.29 The completeness of the survey can
be demonstrated by comparing the sum of entrances embraced by the
survey with the official figures for total entrances. In each of the sample
years the survey represents over 90 per cent of total entrances.

The evidence presented in the tables can be viewed in a variety of
ways: by region, by date and the overall picture. While the trends dis-
played in both tables are the same, the shift to steam is less prominent
in the vessel figures. Overall, Table 4.2 shows the very minor position
of steam at the opening date of 1855; only in Western European trade
did it have a presence of any significance and even here sailing vessels
accounted for two-thirds of entrances by tonnage. Steam's participa-
tion in 1870 was still relatively modest. But one-third of total entrances
comprised steam tonnage and in no region did steam reach anywhere
near 50 per cent of tonnage entrances. Indeed, only in Western Europe
and the Mediterranean and African regions did it exceed 40 per cent.
However, the decade of the 1870s marked a turning point; steam
tonnage entrances markedly increased. In aggregate terms they rose
within the decade of the 1870s from 33.8 per cent to 63 per cent, by
far the largest advance in any decade. All regions with the exception of
Western America saw a significant shift to steam. In Western Europe,
the Mediterranean, Africa and the Far East (where the percentage was
near or exceeded 80 per cent) the shift to steam was virtually com-
plete. However, in 1880, the North American region's steam tonnage
entrances only just exceeded 50 per cent and Northern Europe only just
40 per cent. Steam entrances were still in the minority at 45 per cent in

David M. Williams and John Armstrong 51
the Indian trade, despite a dramatic rise in the 1870s in consequence of
the Suez Canal, and in the Australasian at some 18 per cent.
1890 saw the aggregate percentage reach 82.8. In most regions steam
tonnage ranged from 72 per cent into the high 90 per cents, with the
Far East region virtually completely steam at 99.4 per cent. The most
remarkable advances in the 1880s had been in the North American
region, which rose to 81 per cent, and Australasia where steam was
now in the majority. Steam at last also took the majority share in the
Northern Europe region, albeit sail still retained nearly 40 per cent
of the traffic. The only region where steam had but little presence
remained Western America. 1900 saw the aggregate figure approach 92
per cent and 1910, 97 per cent. In 1910, steam tonnage comprised 90
per cent or more in all regions bar Western America and even in this last
bastion of sail, sail's contribution had fallen to a mere third.
Summarizing the above, it would seem that in aggregate terms the
decades of the 1870s and 1880s were the era of the great shift to steam.
This was true also for most of those regions that had effectively com-
pleted the transition to steam by 1890. In the few regions that lagged,
the leeway was made up in the 1890s. Indicative of the new steam-
dominated regime was the fact that theAnnual Statements of Navigation
and Shippingg ceased to provide separate tables for sailing vessels soon
after 1900.
While the table depicts a gradual progression from sail to steam with
an acceleration in the 1870s and 1880s, it demonstrates, further, that
the pace and timing of the transition varied between regions. So much
so that to base general judgements on the total aggregate figure before
1890 is to court danger, as regional experiences varied quite substan-
tially. Let us now try to explain the differing experiences of the various
regions, the underlying influences at work and how our findings relate
to prevailing views.
The timing and pace of the adoption of steam was an amalgam of a
number of elements. These comprised the efficiency of the steamship
(in terms of coal consumption), the nature of cargo and the distances
involved. These represented the basic constituents of the market for
shipping. Other factors such as the importance of mails and passenger
traffic, the initiative of entrepreneurs and political considerations were
important at various times and in particular regions or trades but the
influences of the market were the prime determinants of the shift.30
Within these was that the steamship had to transport its own fuel: as
has been observed, ‘the factor inputs and costs of steamship transporta-
tion varied inversely with the length of the voyage from the source of

52 The Progress of the Steamship
coal'.31 But during the second half of the nineteenth century techno-
logical advances, notably the screw propeller, iron and later steel hulls
and progress in engine design, particularly the compound and later
triple expansion engine, greatly increased efficiency. All enhanced the
competitive ability of steam, giving rise to the emergence of the tramp
steamship and engagement in bulk freight carriage over longer and
longer distances.32 One further aspect to bear in mind in this particular
context - that of British trade and entrances into British ports - is the
involvement of foreign vessels. Beyond Britain, the shift to steam was
slower and this was to be reflected in those trades in which non-British
vessels participated. These influences underlay the progress of steam in
various trades and regions.
Previous discussions of the shift to steam at a regional level have
eschewed statistical analysis. In terms of a broad sweep of the regional
dimension we have but the comments of Harley made in two papers.
In summary, steamships gradually displaced sail first on the short
trades and then on the long. In the 1850s and 1860s steamships
took the short trades to the Continent. In the late 1860s and 1870s
they took the North Atlantic. In the 1870s and 1880s, with the aid
of the Suez Canal, the steamship took over the trade to India and
the Far East. But sail still held on to the round-the world trades, the
Australian trade and trades to the west coast of the Americas, into
the twentieth century.33
In a later paper, Harley offered a chart of approximate dates for voyages
on which sail and steam were competitive for bulk cargo.34
1855 Northern Europe
1865 Mediterranean fruit and cotton
1870 North Atlantic grain; Bombay via Suez Canal
1875 New Orleans cotton
1880 Calcutta
1895 West Coast of America grain and ore
These two sets of observations by Harley were path-breaking and a gen-
eration of historians utilized them. Some writers, including ourselves,
have drawn on them for wider judgements and we fully acknowledge
Harley's pioneering studies. So authoritative were these in their time
that scholars paid insufficient heed to Harley's own qualifications of

David M. Williams and John Armstrong 53
‘gradually' and ‘approximately'. Nor did they note that the analysis and
findings presented were the outcome of ignoring the non-bulk trades
which had a significance far outweighing their volume.35 Finally, in the
context of a regional survey, Harley's comments do not embrace all of
the regions of the world. As for other writers who have commented on
the regional dimension, we might note the general acceptance that the
‘home trades' were the province of steam by the mid-century; that the
‘conquest of the Atlantic' occurred but years later, and Graham's obser-
vation that by the early 1850s ‘almost the whole British trade of the
Mediterranean and Levant had become the monopoly of the iron screw
steamer, assisted by sails'.36 This latter point was franked by Cottrell's
study.37
Bearing in mind the above discussion of influences on, and prevailing
views of, the regional shift to steam, what does our new examination
have to offer?
Looking first at Western Europe, previous commentators have
rightly noted the progress of steam in the home trades. Alone among
all regions in 1855, steam had a significant presence yet its advance
in the next decade and a half was surprisingly modest. This might be
explained by the nature of the data - for the French trade extended
well beyond the limits of the zone embraced by the ‘home trade'- and
the participation of foreign vessels.38 After 1870 the shift to steam was
rapid but until the turn of the century there remained a tiny residual
of sail, for over short distances the relative extra duration of voyages
for non-urgent cargoes was of little significance. Mediterranean trades
were perhaps the most rapid in their transition: here steam's penetra-
tion approached that in the Western European trades by 1870 and their
subsequent advance was dramatic. The share of steam by 1880 was
overwhelming and virtually total by 1890. The significant point here
is that contrary to the views of Graham and Cottrell, steam's triumph
in the Mediterranean was post-1870 and not during the mid-century.
Within the European sector the region that lagged, both in continen-
tal terms and in a wider perspective, was Northern Europe. Here the
explanation is simple enough: that the trade was almost exclusively a
bulk cargo, timber and that the exporting nations, Norway and much
more so Russia, persisted with sail.
Moving southward, the shift to steam in the West and South African
trades is on the face of it surprising. However, both trades were almost
entirely conducted in British vessels and the nature of the West
African trade - that involved the assembling of cargo from a variety of

54 The Progress of the Steamship
ports - favoured the use of steam vessels. Additionally, in both West and
South Africa, innovative and dynamic entrepreneurs promoted steam-
ship liner trades for cargoes and passengers.39
Transatlantic trades present an interesting and contrasting picture.
In the North American trade, issues of timing and the constituent ele-
ments of the region influence progress. Embracing both Canada and the
USA, the region was heavily associated with bulk trades, timber, grain
and cotton. Harley suggests that in the 1870s steam became competi-
tive in the grain and cotton trades, but the great shift to steam took
place in the 1880s. Sail retained a foothold, as Canadian shipowners
maintained a presence in the timber trade where voyage duration was
of little consequence.40 A further factor in the progress of steam in the
North Atlantic trade was the experience of the USA. Until the mid-
century and beyond, US shipping had dominated Anglo-American trade
and (despite steam's much heralded ‘conquest of the Atlantic') the fact
that steam's participation in 1860 was less than 10 per cent testifies to
this.41 Thereafter, the impact of the Civil War and the subsequent rapid
decline of the US mercantile marine led to a speedy shift to British
shipping involvement in Anglo-USA trade.42 Indeed steam's participa-
tion (largely British) in the USA trade shot up in the 1860s from some
12 per cent to almost 50 per cent, a remarkable change within a single
decade. Elsewhere in Atlantic trades steam made consistent progress in
South American trades, albeit this was not an important area of British
trade. The significant shift in this area came in the 1890s when steam
vessels with refrigerated capacity enabled the full exploitation of the
Argentine meat trade.43
Further afield, the Far Eastern trades demonstrate both the early limi-
tations of steam and its triumph over these through the compound and
triple expansion engine and the impact of the Suez Canal. Until the
opening of the latter, direct sea contact between the Far East and Britain
by steam was not feasible in economic terms, hence the zero depictions
in the table. However, this is not to suggest that steam had not penetrated
the Indian Ocean and beyond. It had, and made significant advances,
but these were regional. Steam linkages relied on the land route across
the Suez Isthmus and therefore appeared in the statistics as entrances
from Egypt. After the opening of the Suez Canal direct entrances from
eastern ports could be registered. Hence the remarkable advance in
the 1870s which was the outcome both of the Canal opening and the
nature of the trade of China and Japan: relatively high value products,
capable of bearing the premium price of steam navigation. The Indian
region with its bulk trades saw a significant but slower advance of steam

David M. Williams and John Armstrong 55
in the 1870s. There remain the long-distance ‘round-the-world' trades.
Australasia, compared with elsewhere, was a significant laggard in the
1870s and indeed thereafter - distance and its bulk grain trade ensured a
place for sail. Likewise, and even more so, there is the Western American
trade. Here low-value bulk trades (and because of this the lack of any
incentive to provide coaling facilities en route) offered a last refuge for
sail. A refuge for vessels - old or unemployable elsewhere - manned by
elderly or inexperienced crews that combined with the hazards of the
route gave rise to a tragically high casualty rate.44
The outcome of our survey of the regional dimension bears out the
accepted picture of the long-distance trades of Australasia and the west
coast of America being the last to shift to steam. The latter's presence
in the early twentieth century was an anachronism, a relic of a bygone
age. For other regions the picture presented is one of a gradual shift to
steam, with the crucial decades often occurring later than previously
believed. Above all else, it shows how blanket judgements obscure the
regional experience.
Our analysis, for reasons mentioned earlier, has focused on Table 4.2
relating to tonnage. Table 4.3, which depicts the participation of steam
in terms of vessel numbers, reveals a similar pattern. There are some
differences: both generally and regionally the pace was slower. In aggre-
gate terms the fact that sail retained the majority of entrances as late as
1880 is perhaps surprising. Likewise, to take some regional examples,
the massive predominance of sail in the Northern Europe and North
American regions in the same year. Interesting though such variants
are, they are of no great consequence given the thrust of this study,
the shift to steam, both tables depicting a gradual process. However,
if the focus of examination were the number of vessels participating
generally in world trade or in the trade of particular regions at any one
time, we would find that the number and proportion of sailing vessels
held up, far more so than the tonnage analysis would suggest. The sail
tonnage figures embody a greater number of vessels than the steam
figures, as steam ships were of larger tonnage and performed, over a
given time, more voyages; the generally accepted ratio is three to one.45
Furthermore, as time went by, sail was left on longer routes that could
only account for a few entrances per year. Hence, the overall picture of
the transfer to steam based on entrance tonnage figures may present a
somewhat distorted impression of the relative numbers of sail and steam
ships in service. This can be viewed in two ways. On the basis of the
shift to steam represented by the tonnage entrance figures, the number
of sailing vessels still in employment relative to the number of vessels

56 The Progress of the Steamship
in the steam fleet could be viewed as quite remarkable. Alternatively, it
might be observed that this seemingly large number of sailing vessels
made but a little contribution to world shipping and trade. In essence,
what we are saying is that sailing vessels, whatever their proportion
of the world fleet, because they were of smaller tonnage, because they
were not so efficient in terms of voyages in a given time and because
they were relegated to longer trades, contributed less and less to world
trading activity.
Our analysis based on shipping movements suggests that the transi-
tion from sail to steam and the progress of steam was a gradual process,
one that varied between trades and regions. In so far as it is possible to
generalize, the shift to steam occurred principally during the 1870s and
1880s. It was complete in most regions by 1890 and, Western America
apart, effectively in all others by 1900. In these respects our research is in
line with that of post-1950 writers who have stressed ‘process' as opposed
to a sudden change or an ‘event'. However, the notion that change gener-
ally might have been sudden was never a real proposition. The extent of
existing capital stock, the limitations of the steamship which were only
overcome over several decades, and differences in distance and commod-
ity structure between trades ensured a gradual and uneven process.
Yet the idea of sudden change should not be totally jettisoned and
an examination of the tables reveals that in some regions the timing of
decisive change was short and sharp. For example, the gradual process
of the shift to steam is apparent from 1855 in the following regions:
Western Europe, West and East Mediterranean, Western and South
Africa and South America. But in these regions the decade of the 1870s
was unlike those before or after in terms of the extent and pace of the
shift: it was of a ‘great leap' character. Why this was so deserves more
research at a specialized level. The 1870s were also clearly the decisive
decade for the Far East region, which is perhaps more easily explained
by the opening of the Suez Canal and the explosion of demand for high
quality products from China and Japan which lent themselves to steam-
ship freight. Elsewhere, one could stress the 1880s as the crucial decade
for the North American region. Thus the shift to steam as an ‘event' or
sudden change may have some currency at a regional level and even
more so if individual countries and ports are considered.
But let us return to our basic findings that the process of change was
a gradual one. Two aspects remain to be considered: one, the outcome
of our analysis in the context of the historiography of the shift from sail
to steam; and two, the question of pace: was the process fast or slow?
On the former, our findings reinforce the case for the transition being

David M. Williams and John Armstrong 57
a gradual process and one that in both aggregate and sectoral terms
occurred later than perhaps has been envisaged. This is particularly so
when one examines the situation as late as 1870 when steam formed
but one-third of tonnage entrances and even in the regions where
steam was most advanced, sail still comprised the majority of tonnage
entrances. Given this, can the view of dramatic and revolutionary
change held by contemporary writers be explained or justified?
Possibly, yes. Obviously contemporaries saw the extension of steam
taking place both generally and in almost all sectors, and this was
sufficient for them. But in addition to this, they viewed the progress
of steam from a different perspective from that of post-World War II
scholars. These different perspectives can be understood if we recognize
that the advance of steam was an advance in both communications and
transport. Contemporaries focused primarily on the communication
dimension, the fact that distance was shortened and information, pas-
sengers and mails could now move with unprecedented speed and regu-
larity. In this respect the steamship had wrought a revolution and had
certainly done so worldwide by 1860 and possibly earlier. By then liner
services covered almost all global routes. This is clear from the Table 4.1
data for 1860, when steam had penetrated virtually all regions, invari-
ably in the form of liner services.46 The steamship had made the world
a smaller place and one in which information and people travelled with
a new speed and certainty. This was progress and, compared with the
situation but a few decades earlier, revolutionary progress.
In contrast, post-1950 commentators have focused on the transport
of goods and, given the nature of world trade in the nineteenth century,
the freighting of bulk commodities. This was the approach of Graham
and Harley. Their views were taken up by others and seemingly gained
greater weight by the adoption of the mantra (attributed to Robin Craig)
that in shipping it is ‘volumes not values' that are the crucial factor.47
In trade terms this is true, but shipping has always conveyed more than
goods; the sea has also been a highway for the transit of people and
information. It was this - which might be considered the ‘value' dimen-
sion of shipping - that fired the imagination and interpretation of
contemporaries. It also fuelled the development of an integrated world
economy in the second half of the nineteenth century.
Finally, the second issue, the pace of change. Here, although both
contemporaries by implication and recent scholars more specifically
have expressed views, it is hard to see how any clear judgement might
be made. ‘Fast' and ‘slow' are relative terms and are difficult to apply
in an objective fashion. Scholars examining the transition must make

58 The Progress of the Steamship
their own judgement. Our own view, given that the steamship only
came into existence in 1810, and moreover, as is evidenced by the table,
was of very little numerical significance in the overall statistics in 1855,
that the virtual complete replacement of sail by steam within less than
half a century (bearing in mind the millennia of the age of sail), cannot
but be regarded as a rapid process. Certainly, this must be the verdict
in the context of the long run of history. It is this standpoint that most
likely influenced nineteenth-century commentators who, in the brief
span of their own lifetimes, witnessed a transformation of all that had
gone before.

Inevitably there are imperfections in allocating countries to regions that emanate
from the form of presentation of the official figures. The most striking examples
of this are France, which possessed ‘home trade', Atlantic and Mediterranean
coasts, and Spain, with Atlantic and Mediterranean seaboards.
Over the 55 years embraced by the tables there were changes in the form and
classification of the official figures. For example, Germany and Italy before 1870
were listed in terms of their subsequent constituent states. We have endeavoured,
as far as it is possible, to maintain a consistent definition of each region over the
years of the table.
These figures relate to shipping entrances as recorded in the Annual Statement of
Trade and Navigation until 1870 and, thereafter, the Annual Statement of the Navigation
and Shipping of the United Kingdom.In a few regions these do not necessarily reflect
trade volumes because, rather ironically, of the very subject of this paper, the shift
from sail to steam. One of the consequences of the shift to steam was a change
in voyage patterns from one of direct voyages from, say, A to B under sail, to one
of voyages with calls en route (sometimes many) by steam. In consequence, for
example, vessels listed as entering from Egypt (in the Eastern Mediterranean) may
well have made calls and taken on cargo in Italy, France, Spain and Portugal (in the
Western Mediterranean). Our paper on the impact of steam on voyage patterns,
‘Changing voyage patterns in the 19th century: the impact of the steamship' has
been published in the International Journal of Maritime History.48

ings to the United States, as well as to France, Holland, Belgium, Hamburg,
Cadiz and Lisbon. After 1840 the paddle-wheeler reached out from India
to China, and gradually from the British Isles to the West Indies and
South America.... By 1852, a bi-monthly service on the west coast of South
America linked Valparaiso with Atlantic steamers by way of the Isthmus of
Panama.'
12. ‘Although the steamship had successfully wedged its way into the overseas
trade, mainly by carrying passengers and subsidised mails, the evolving sail-
ing ship of the 1860s and 1870s - faster than its predecessors, with double
the space for cargo in proportion to tonnage and manned by one third
the number of men - retained on broad oceans a predominance almost
as marked as that of the screw steamer in the coastal … waters of Europe.'
(Graham, op.cit., pp. 74-5)

62 The Progress of the Steamship
the British mercantile marine's advance in its adoption of steam. Bias in the

and vessels in ballast were only of significance in a few regions.

## 5
The Advantages of Water
Carriage: Scale Economies
and Shipping Technology,
c. 1870-2000
Yrjö Kaukiainen
… a broad wheeled wagon attended by two men and
drawn by eight horses in about six weeks time car-
ries and brings back between London and Edinburgh
nearly 4 tons weight of goods. In about the same
time a ship navigated by six or eight men, and sailing
between the ports of London and Leith, frequently
carries and brings back 200 tons weight of goods.
Since such, therefore, are the advantages of water car-
riage, it is natural that the first improvements of art
and industry should be made where this conveniency
opens the whole world to a market for the produce of
every sort of labour.
(Adam Smith,The Wealth of Nations)
Adam Smith was one of the first to emphasize the superiority of water
transport for the carriage of goods. Had he lived long enough to see the
rapid development of railways in the mid-nineteenth century he might
have moderated his views slightly. However, the transition from sail to
steam shipping, which gained momentum in the 1870s, produced a
new leap forward for water transport: between 1880 and the First World
War, transport costs in coastal shipping seem to have declined almost
40 per cent faster than on British railways.1 Similar observations have
also been made for the period 1960-1990, and we may, indeed, con-
clude that ‘the advantages of water carriage' have increased even in the
longer term.2 Adam Smith's example suggests that labour productivity
in coastal shipping outpaced that in road transport by the ratio four-
teen to one. In the beginning of the third millennium, the overall cost
64

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 65
efficiency (price per ton-mile) of short-sea shipping seems to be some 25
times higher than that of long-trader trucking.3 Thus even after the late
nineteenth century, there have been other technical ‘revolutions' lead-
ing to a continuous, more or less regular, growth of transport efficiency
in both ocean and coastal shipping. A macro-level proof is the fact that
the global merchant tonnage grew about sixtyfold in 150 years, while
the respective growth of output - the physical volume of cargo carried -
grew at least twice as much.4
The long-term growth of technical efficiency, and in particular the
development after World War II, was directly related to an improvement
in the hauling efficiency of typical merchant ships. Table 5.1 shows how
much cargo different ships at different times could load, and how far
they could move this in one day (how many ton-miles they produced
in 24 hours). Such a gauge is, of course, simplistic in disregarding eco-
nomic variables, but all ships included in this eclectic list were (or are)
well established types and, therefore, economically viable in their own
times. Moreover, putting a strong emphasis on physical performance is
by no means irrelevant in our globalized world. Huge quantities of raw
materials and the counter-currents of finished goods are carried across
the oceans all the time.
Table 5.1 shows that even quite modest-sized modern ships are fifty
times more efficient than medium-sized sailing vessels of the 1870s,
or early steamers. A comparison of the biggest ships would show an
even greater difference. While the table cannot pretend any degree of
statistical representativeness, we can still make a few basic propositions.
First, in the long run, improvements in the mechanical efficiency of
shipping have depended on physical size rather than speed through
the water. The average size of merchant ships increased from about
700 to over 24,000 deadweight tons, or thirty-fourfold, between 1880
and 2008, while cruising speeds have hardly grown fourfold.5 This is
explained by hydrodynamics: wave-making resistance, in particular,
limits economical speeds, while there are no absolute upper limits -
apart from water depth - to the size of floating structures.6 Only during
the transition from sail to steam were substantial advantages gained
from added (average) speed and predictability of voyages. However,
there also was a trade-off: as weight and volume of machinery and
bunkers decreased the payload capacity of steamers, they were required
to have larger hulls than sailing vessels of similar deadweight and cubic
volume.
The first strong boost for big ships started in the late nineteenth
century with the North Atlantic passenger liners. These have not been

Notes: Cargo capacity indicated in metric tons and/or cubic metres (cbm). Ships' capacity for
heavy goods is limited by deadweight tonnage (dwt) minus the weight of fuels and stores,
while their capacity for light cargo is limited by the cubic volume of their holds plus the
possible deck cargo. Capacities are therefore expressed both in weight tons and cubic metres
(‘cubic tons'). For container cargoes, TEU capacity has been computed by dividing dwt by
14 and the volume of one TEU has been taken as 32,9 cubic metres. Speeds refer to practical
cruising speeds, for sailing vessels (initalics) actual observed averages in ocean trades.
Sources: Different volumes of Conway's History of the Ship, tables of typical ships; Y. Kaukiainen
(1995) ´Tons and Tonnages: Ship Measurement and Shipping Statistics´International Journal
of Maritime History, VII, 1; Stopford (2009), p. 394-409; Containership-Info (2011), http://
www.containership-info.com/, (accessed 23 August 2011).
included in Table 5.1 as reliable data on their cargo spaces (including
cabins and other rooms for passengers) and dwt are very difficult to
estimate. In any case, they developed into highly specialized vessels,
built for the carriage of very light cargo, human passengers (even a late
nineteenth-century emigrant required the space of three tons of grain).7

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 67
Thus, liners did not need high deadweight capacity and, travelling
lightly loaded, were able to achieve high speeds - in the 1930s over 30
knots. They also grew bigger than any other ships so far. Not until 1899
was a ship longer than the 211 metres of Brunel's extravagant 1858
giant the Great Eastern launched. However, this was followed by a race
for the biggest ship of the world; in the 1930s, no less than three ships
exceeding an overall length of 300 metres were built.8 The passenger
liner boom started with the rapid growth of emigration to the USA and
it died out with the transatlantic airlines. Recently, however, it has expe-
rienced a rebirth in the guise of modern cruising ships; the biggest ones
have already exceeded the old Atlantic liners in sheer size.9
The real workhorses of the seas, however, remained much smaller
until after World War II. At the beginning of the twentieth century,
cargo ships of over 10,000 tons deadweight (or 150 metres long) were
still rare. While a few cargo liners and oil tankers approaching 15,000
tons and ore carriers of about 20,000 tons deadweight were built in the
interwar period, the Liberty ships (11,000 dwt) and T2 tankers (around
17,000 dwt) still represented the upper end of the size scale for cargo
ships in the early 1950s.10 Soon, however, these were dwarfed by a
‘revolution' that was starting in oil shipping. In the wake of the Korean
War the first tanker exceeding 50,000 dwt was launched. This started
an escalation during which several ‘magical' limits were rapidly passed:
100,000 dwt in 1959, 200,000 in 1966, 300,000 in 1968 and 500,000
in 1976.11
This was basically demand-induced growth, mainly because mineral
oil was replacing coal as the most important source of energy in rapidly
growing industrialized countries. Actual demand was, however, also
affected by unexpected non-economic factors. First, the Korean War
suddenly boosted the demand for fuel transports across the Pacific and
tanker freights soared dramatically. At the same time, Iran nationalized
the British oil industry on the Persian Gulf, resulting in a fundamental
structural change in oil transports. Before this, refineries were built close
to oil fields; transports, therefore, mainly consisted of refined products.
Now oil companies gradually moved their production closer to consum-
ers in Europe and Japan and more crude oil had to be carried from the
Persian Gulf to these destinations. As both volumes and distances were
great, large tankers proved much more profitable than small ones. Even
the limits posed by the Suez Canal became less important as the canal
was closed both in 1956-57 and 1967-75. Finally, an institutional factor
contributed additional advantages for large tankers: the international
load line convention, concluded in 1966, allowed deeper lading than

Source: Lloyd's Register, World Fleet Statistics.
before for such ‘closed' cargo ships. In the size class of 150,000-200, 000,
deadweight tonnages increased by ten per cent and more.12
The spectacular growth of tanker tonnage can be seen in Table 5.2.
Total fleet (measured in gross register tons) more than doubled in each
decade between 1950 and 1980, and the proportion of oil tankers
jumped from 21 to 42 per cent. The existence of many older and smaller
as well as new distribution tankers implies that average sizes were not
as impressive as the ‘giant' ships might suggest. Indeed, their impact on
the fleet average remained quite modest in the 1950s, though after the
implementation of new load line rules, even the averages soared.
Rapid economic growth also increased the demand for dry cargo ship-
ping. In particular, the transport of iron ore from South America, India
and Australia to Europe and Japan grew massively. On a more modest
scale, this also applied to trade in other major bulks. On the other hand,
as British coal was replaced by American and Australian, the traditional
joint production, with ‘tramp trading' ships exporting coal from Europe
and bringing back other dry cargoes, was replaced by a more one-sided
structure: like tankers, tramps steamed long distances with ballast.13
This gave an additional impetus to build more economical bulk carriers.
The most common answer was the same as within oil: increasing ship
sizes beyond anything seen before. As early as the mid-1950s a few ore
carriers of about 60,000 tons deadweight were constructed but, overall,
the escalation of size was slower than for tankers. Both the scale of the
bulk trades and lack of deep ports restricted the development. Naturally,
new load line regulations also encouraged the building of bigger ships.
In the late 1980s, there was already one bulk carrier of 360,000 dwt and
some twenty of over 200,000, but the most popular class were still the
handysize (25,000-40,000 dwt) ships.14
An interesting element in the development of bulk carriers were
ships designed to carry both liquid and dry bulk cargoes, in order to

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 69
find cargo (or at least part cargo) for return legs. In the 1920s some oil
and ore carriers that loaded ore in a central tank (or tanks) and oil in
separate wing tanks were constructed. As long as the interior volumes
of ships, compared with their deadweight capacity, were generous, this
was a viable combination. After the 1966 load line revision even dry
cargo ships with tight and strong steel hatches and segmented cargo
spaces were allowed deeper loading. Subsequently, earlier ore-oil carriers
were replaced by the OBOs (ore-bulk-oil) which could fill all holds with
one medium-heavy bulk commodity, or one another (or each third)
with heavy ore. They were often the biggest bulkers afloat but, as long
as the oil boom continued, most of these only carried crude oil, gaining
little from their built-in flexibility.15
Lloyd's statistics only started to make a distinction between bulk car-
riers and other dry cargo ships in the late 1950s. It is obvious that their
‘ore-bulk', as well as the later ‘ore-bulk-oil' categories only included
new ships, identified by size and certain technical features. Therefore
Lloyd's figures on bulker tonnage do not give a true overall picture of
bulk trades because traditional ‘tramp steamers' are missing; on the
other hand, they present a rather perfect summary of the diffusion of
this specific new innovation. Table 5.3 indicates a growth which was
at least as impressive as the one within tankers, although average sizes
grew more slowly. This difference is also suggested by the fact that aver-
ages for OBO ships (mainly occupied in oil trade) were much higher
than those of dry cargo OBs (ore-bulk).
The oil crises of the 1970s stopped the growth of the global tanker
tonnage, shifting to decline in the early 1980s. In the short term, dry
bulk tonnage still increased, mainly because most of the OBO fleet
moved to dry cargo trades. In the longer run, the gradual transition
from oil to coal increased demand for big dry cargo ships. From about

1985 onwards, the tonnages of oil and dry bulk fleets have been roughly
equal but, interestingly, in terms of average size, the latter have gradu-
ally passed tankers by a fairly clear margin (see Figure 5.1). Even more
interesting is that both groups have grown much more slowly than in
the 1960s and 1970s. Tanker tonnage first dropped by almost a third
and only regained the 1980 level by 2004; subsequently it has increased
by some 30 per cent. Dry bulk tonnage grew by no less than 75 per cent
between 1980 and 2008, but still average growth was only two per cent
a year - a far cry from the boom of the 1970s.16 The stagnation was even
more pronounced in the sense that no new size records for tankers were
set after the end of the 1970s - indeed, three of the five tankers excee-
ding 550,000 dwt were scrapped in the 1980s, and no new ones above
400,000 tons were built between 1980 and 1995. Neither have any bul-
kers bigger than theBerge Stahl (1986; 364,800 dwt) been constructed. It
seems as if the tanker and bulker revolutions have come of age.

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 71
At the same time as the two bulk revolutions were in full swing,
another was just brewing. Containers appeared in American coastal
transport at the end of the 1950s and, after international standards
were issued for ‘boxes' (1965), they started to transform liner shipping
all over the world. At first, the strongest impact of this innovation was
felt in ports: it became much faster, in particular if efficient cranes were
available, to load and unload big standard-size steel containers instead
of a large variety of boxes, crates, rolls, bags, bales, and all kinds of par-
cels in which finished goods had so far been brought on board. It has
been estimated that, ton for ton, the handling of container cargo only
required between five and seven per cent of the labour demanded by
traditional general cargo.17
The idea of specialized container ships was, however, only beginning
to develop in the early 1960s. At first, containers were carried as deck
cargo on ordinary dry cargo vessels, Ro-Ro vessels, converted liners and
even tankers. Often, a few container bays were built in existing holds
or a ship was lengthened (‘jumbo-ized') by adding new container sec-
tions. Such semi-container ships suffered from slow turnaround times
in ports because conventional cargoes still had to be handled by the
old methods. Moreover, their sizes remained modest, seldom exceeding
15,000 tons deadweight.18
The first generation of newly built full (‘cellular') container ships in
the late 1960s already had full-width cargo hatches, enabling contain-
ers to be lowered directly to their ‘cells' and stacked one on top of
another. They were also equipped to carry a moderate cargo on deck
(or rather on hatch covers). In terms of size, these first steps were
still fairly conservative: typically, the pre-1970 ‘cellular' ships car-
ried between 800 and 1,400 standard twenty-foot containers (known
as TEUs). Of course, scale economies were also recognized by liner
companies - exactly as they were being exploited in tanker and dry
bulk shipping - and, in the early 1970s, the next generation of ships
with TEU capacities approaching 3,000 were added to the fleets. As the
biggest ones measured over 45,000 tons deadweight they were exceed-
ing the tonnages of traditional liners by a vast margin. Technological
optimism, in connection with cheap oil, also encouraged liner compa-
nies to seek improved efficiency by building faster vessels. This trend
culminated in a series of gas turbine ships, built by the American
company Sea-Land, which could carry full shiploads of over 2,000
TEU at a speed of 33 knots. They, of course, ran foul of the oil crises,
and were subsequently laid up or converted to more economical diesel
power. Nor did other enterprises with fast container ships succeed as

72 The Advantages of Water Carriage
bunker costs rose rapidly; moderate cruising speeds became the rule
for a couple of decades.19
The backlash caused by the oil crises even seems to have dampened
the enthusiasm for increasingly big ships. Even in 1980, only two ves-
sels had a capacity slightly exceeding 3,000 TEU (that is, their dwt was
almost 50,000).20 Subsequently another factor, limits posed by the locks
of the Panama Canal, moderated the size race. It was only in 1988 that
the American President Line deployed the very first ‘Post-Panamax'
ships (4,340 TEU) in their Pacific service.21 This became a turning point:
the example was followed by other lines, and the growth of demand
for container transport picked up in the 1990s. A new wave of invest-
ments followed. In 1996, Maersk Line launched a new series of 6,000
TEU ships which, only one year later, were followed by another of 6,600
TEU. Between 2006 and 2008, the same company pushed the limits
further with ships of over 11,000 TEU (with a nominal capacity of over
15,000 TEU and dwt of almost 160,000). These can still be regarded as
the biggest container ships in the world, but nothing suggests that they
would remain so. Other lines have built vessels over 10,000 TEU, and
Maersk have already announced the ‘Triple E' series with a capacity of
(at least) 18,000 TEU.22 Thus it seems that the container revolution - or
at least the escalation of container ship sizes - is still in full swing.
The biggest ships always catch the headlines, but a general escala-
tion has taken place across the whole size scale. In the 1950s ‘small
tonnage' was a term connected with ships smaller than, say, 500 dwt;
nowadays these words are commonly associated with vessels below
6,000 dwt. The same phenomenon can also be seen in medium-size
shipping. According to data in the American publication Chartering
Annual, average cargoes of coal-carrying vessels grew from approxi-
mately 8,950 metric tons in the mid-1950s, to 52,200 around 1980
and 105,700 by the turn of the millennium. The corresponding figures
for grain were 8,950, 28,800 and 42,100 respectively.23 The figures
indicate that, in less than five decades, average grain cargoes grew over
four- and coal cargoes over ten-fold.
Figure 5.1 above presents the development of average sizes in the three
‘revolutionary' tonnage categories, as well as for the world merchant
fleet. Despite certain statistical inconsistencies, the curves sketch a fairly
logical picture. First, the tanker and bulker revolutions were chronologi-
cally closely connected, while the container revolution lagged behind
by at least twenty years. It is also evident that the bulk revolutions cul-
minated, or at least were moderated, in the early 1980s. Although a new
growth spurt has been experienced in the early twenty-first century, it

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 73
may not be compared with the rapid and escalating proliferation of big
container ships.
These three specific waves have produced the modern shipping
revolution. As a result, the overall weight of the respective shipping
categories was naturally increased: as early as 1986 - even excluding
gas and chemical tankers and oil bulk vessels - they comprised 66 per
cent of the world total gross tonnage, and this increased to no less
than 76 per cent in 2008. In contrast, general cargo ships (traditional
liners and tramp ships) which still around 1970 represented a third of
the tonnage, now only amount to some 7 per cent. Moreover, there
has been hardly any growth of average size in this category. On the
other hand, two special groups, liquid gas tankers and passenger ves-
sels (both conventional and Ro-Ro, and in particular cruising ships)
have recently experienced rapid growth in both total tonnages and
average sizes. Yet these still represent less than 10 per cent of total
global tonnage.24
In addition to a sheer multiplication of ship sizes, shipping efficiency
has also been improved by another crucial long-term development,
increasing specialization. As terms like tanker, bulk carrier and cellu-
lar container ship indicate, ships are presently being designed for an
increasingly narrow range of cargoes. In the late nineteenth century,
most cargo-carrying ships were of very similar general configuration.
For example, if we divide their deadweight tonnage by the volume of
cargo holds in cubic metres, the ratio (corresponding with the specific
gravity of cargo when all cargo spaces are full) generally fluctuated at
around 0.6-0.7, a relationship which, apart from the weight of the
empty ship and basic hydrostatic laws, resulted from contemporary
load line (freeboard) regulations.25 In practice, standard cargo ships
were optimal for carriage of medium-weight goods, such as grain or rice,
which filled the cargo hold without sinking the ship too deep. When
carrying heavy cargo like iron or ores, the ship sank to the ‘marks' long
before the cargo space was full, while with a light cargo the deadweight
capacity could not be fully exploited.
In the late nineteenth century there already existed one special type
of ship, the passenger liner, for the carriage of very light cargo. However,
for ordinary cargo vessels, the standard practice when carrying light
goods, such as sawn wood or timber, was to load some of it on deck. In
the early twentieth century a special type of light cargo vessel, known
as shelterdecker, was developed. The concept was based on certain loop-
holes in old measurement rules: by accepting an unusually high free-
board (and low deadweight tonnage) a two-deck ship could deduct the

volumes of tweendeck spaces from its register tonnage, as if the cargo in
them were a deck cargo - albeit covered by a weatherdeck.26
Table 5.4 suggests that the differential between light and heavy cargo
carriers increased a little in the 1920s and 1930s, but even in this respect
the real ‘revolution' took place after the 1950s. For heavy cargoes, the
load line revision of 1966 changed one basic variable, and the resulting
increase of deadweight tonnages was particularly important in the bulk
sector. Moreover, the sheer increase in size made it possible to develop
hull forms with more payload for a given length. Typically, modern
tankers and bulk carriers are built with bows so blunt that similar vessels
have not been seen since the demise of wooden ships of the line.27
At the opposite end of the scale, however, the container revolution
produced even more dramatic results. Steel boxes being weatherproof,
the cargo could easily be carried on deck and, since typical container
goods are quite light, it became possible to carry higher deck cargo than
even imagined before. In the late 1980s, three stacks on deck were still
regarded as a norm, and the stowage ratios (volumes per deadweight)
of container ships were about the same as those of old shelterdeck lin-
ers. However - as Table 5.5 indicates - the ships gradually became more
efficient. Deck cargoes increased as post-Panamax ships were built with
