more beam and stability, and better lashing practices were developed;
between 1986 and 2008, the average dwt/TEU ratio improved by over
40 per cent. Nowadays, the biggest oceangoing ships are designed for
nine stacks on deck, which means that they may carry over half of the
lading above hatches; in small and medium-sized feeders this propor-
tion can rise up to 70 per cent.28 Compared with shelterdeck liners,
modern container ships are, at best, 50 per cent more efficient than
carriers of light cargo. Almost the same can be said of modern Ro-Ro
vessels - although in one respect they resemble old shelterdeckers: most
of their cargo is stowed below decks.29
Table 5.5 also shows that the overall volume (gross tonnage) of con-
tainer carriers has decreased compared with their deadweight capacity.
In practice this means that their hulls, in particular freeboards, have
become lower. The early cellular ships were built with high sides to
guarantee adequate longitudinal stiffness. With more efficiently framed
double hulls, ships could be made lower and slightly lighter, which also
made it possible to carry more cargo on deck. While the average (gt) size
of container ships between 1986 and 2008 only increased by a factor of
1.59, the average TEU-capacity grew by a factor of 2.46. This means that
the advances in cargo-carrying efficiency resulted almost as much from
improved stowage ratios as from bigger hulls.
These advantages clearly exceeded those that were attained in the
shipping of heavy bulk cargoes after the 1966 load line revisions and the
adaption of fuller hull forms. In the long run, the deadweight capaci-
ties of all ships, of course, benefited from the development of engines
and other elements of maritime propulsion which produced weight
saving on both machinery installations and bunkers.30 On the other
hand, in terms of cargo hold volumes - which are more important for

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 77
1800
1600
1400
1200
1000
800
The drop appears to be particularly steep up to about 50,000 dwt, but
even after that the declining trend is clear enough. Thus, the growth
of ship sizes was based on strong economic incentives. Yet it is equally
clear that not all shipowners have been aiming at the biggest available
vessels - in spite of a continuous growth of maximum as well as aver-
age sizes in the ‘revolutionary' fleets, ships in the smallest classes still
abound. In 1995 about 35 per cent of all ships in the global container
fleet measured below 20,000 dwt and between 1995 and 2008 their
number almost trebled, growing slightly faster than the overall figure
(see Figure 5.3). In the oil tanker fleet, the number of ships below
10,000 tons declined after 1995 but in 2008 it still amounted to 38 per
cent of all tankers.32 Thus, while the general trends of the modern ship-
ping revolutions are in line with scale economies, the latter should not
be understood as a deterministic law: there was more than one scale
across the different sectors of shipping.
One of the most obvious constraints on ship sizes is the depth and
width of navigable waters and ports. Coastal shipping has always
depended on small vessels and will continue to do so as long as they
enjoy scale economies vis-à-vis road transport. For bigger ships, the

78 The Advantages of Water Carriage
continuous development of existing ports, and the founding of new
deepwater ones, has, of course, been a necessary precondition for the
increasingly big vessels. Nevertheless, the number of ports which can
receive the maxi-size bulk or container carriers is bound to remain lim-
ited. Another case of man-made geographical constraints are the main
inter-oceanic canals. The size of the Panama Canal locks retarded the
spread of big container ships, and it still limits the popularity of ships
just above the ‘Panamax' size (50,000-60,000 dwt; see Figure 5.3), at
least until the proposed wider locks are built. On the other hand, the
closures of the Suez Canal in the 1950s and 1960s removed one barrier
from the growth of oil tankers and bulk carriers. Since its 1975 reopen-
ing the canal has been continuously widened and can presently be tran-
sited by ‘Suezmax' tankers of about 180,000 deadweight tons. This, in
turn, has decreased the popularity of bigger crude carriers on the routes
from the Persian Gulf to Europe and the USA. On the other hand, the
popularity of the ‘Aframax' tankers (which in practice are the biggest
that can exit fully loaded from the Baltic) has increased greatly with the
growth of Russian oil exports via the Gulf of Finland.
In addition to geographical constraints, ship sizes are also limited in
practice by economic considerations. In the early 1970s, a theory of
optimal ship size was developed to explain such factors, based on the
observation that positive scale economies only applied to ships at sea.
While hauling costs per ton decreased with ship size, costs per ton in
port (loading, unloading, port charges, warehousing, etc.) increased.
Accordingly, the total costs-per-ton function seemed to be U-shaped,
with the relevant optimum size being indicated by its lowest level.33
Subsequently, however, it was observed that cargo handling costs per
ton became less affected by ship size as loading and unloading rates
improved in modern ports. Still, it is difficult to imagine that handling
a ton (or TEU) of cargo could ever be cheaper for a big ship than for a
smaller one. Only in one limited area do bigger ships enjoy modest posi-
tive scale advantages in ports: since long ships can be worked with more
cranes than shorter ones, turnaround times and day costs per cargo ton
(or per TEU) will be lower for the former. Thus it seems that at least in
the dry cargo sector the diseconomies of scale in ports have decreased to
such a degree that theoretical optimum sizes tend to exceed the actual
maximum sizes (as limited by, for example, the depth of water in the
relevant ports) by a substantial margin.34 Finally, it must also be remem-
bered that there is at least one sector of shipping which enjoys full scale
advantages even in port: in tankers the capacity of pipelines and pumps
can be upgraded according to carrying capacity. Big crude carriers can

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 79
be loaded and unloaded in the same time - nowadays usually twelve
hours - as smaller product tankers. In this case, it will be impossible to
determine the optimal ship size in the traditional way.
It is obvious that actual ship sizes cannot be explained by economic
functions that only consider the costs of producers (shipowners); the
costs of users (shippers) must also be taken into account. The fact that
different commodities are traded in widely varying quantities is a tan-
gible consequence of the constraints imposed by trade volumes and
the economic interests of shippers. Raw materials and fuels, such as
crude oil, coal and ores, are frequently conveyed by shiploads exceed-
ing 100,000 tons, while the typical parcel sizes for grain or sugar fluc-
tuate around 50,000 and 80,000 tons, those for steel and semi-finished
metal products seldom exceed 10,000 tons, and sawn wood from
Scandinavia is typically sold by shiploads of 4,000 to 5,000 tons.35 In
all cases the volumes are a function of storage and capital costs of the
shippers - not surprisingly, they are higher for cheap than more valu-
able commodities. On the other hand, parcel sizes are also affected
by hauling distances: if these are long enough, the savings in sea
freight (achieved by using bigger vessels) may outweigh the increase
in inventory costs.36 However - remembering that the price elasticities
of many typical bulk products are fairly low - it may be claimed that
the maximum stock size that importers are willing to accept would
be a more important factor than the actual price development of
the relevant commodities. The importance of storage and inventory
costs has recently been increasingly stressed by the ‘just-in/on-time'
principles in industrial logistics. Instead of replenishing stores with
a few big shiploads, many buyers prefer a more continuous flow of
smaller consignments. Such strategies are quite effective in checking
the growth of parcel sizes.
The primary role of sea freight has also been diminished by the decline
of real-term freight levels during the last half-century or so. Figures
5.4 and 5.5 clearly indicate the magnitude of this long-term develop-
ment in dry bulk trades. Although there had already been a hefty drop
between the 1870s and World War I, an even steeper decline followed
during the bulk-shipping revolution: between the mid-1950s and the
turn of the millennium, average grain freights sank by three-quarters
and coal freights even more. (The figures also show that, because of
bigger ship sizes after the 1960s and more efficient cargo handling in
ports, the freights of coal have been lower than those of grain.) In liner
freights (container shipping) the decline started later, in the 1980s, and
so far it has been more modest.37

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 81

corresponding rebate of ‘blue water' freights and reinforced the impres-
sion that hauling costs were declining faster than they actually were.
In any case, the long-term development of bulk freights (Figures 5.4
and 5.5) tells us that journey costs (that is, the rate at which freights
increase with distance) have quite systematically - albeit fluctuating
with economic cycles - declined at least since the 1870s: at the end
of the twentieth century, a ton of grain could be hauled over a given
distance at only one-tenth of the cost of 120 to 130 years earlier. The
decline seems to have been somewhat slower before 1940 than after
1950.39 This suggests that the development was boosted by economies
of scale during the bulk revolution, but even earlier the figures speak of
a fairly steady improvement of shipping efficiency.
The bulk freight curves also demonstrate that even port or terminal
costs (freights at zero distance in the figures) have declined since the
1870s. If, however, we compensate for the removal of stevedore costs
from sea freights, and add the relevant costs to the FD and FIO rates
according to the ‘gross rate' principle, the decline after the 1950s
becomes much more moderate. As Table 5.6 demonstrates, terminal
costs clearly reduced more slowly than voyage costs. In the mid-
1950s the former roughly equalled the cost of moving goods for some

3,000 nautical miles but, at the turn of the millennium, the rate had
increased to almost 8,000 miles. Still it must be pointed out that the
estimation did not account for all terminal costs: in addition to those
included, exporters and importers in many ports have to pay port
charges based on the volume or value of the merchandise. Thus we
may conclude that while the impact of scale advantages has been sub-
stantial, it has mainly affected costs at sea - exactly as the theories of
optimum ship size predicted. However, by achieving such reductions
in cost, the economies of scale, paradoxically, have exhausted much of
their further potential: if voyage costs in short-sea traffic only amount
to some 10 to 15 per cent of the total transport bill, the investments
aiming at a further reduction of that component would incur fairly
high opportunity costs.
Summing up, it seems that the scope and impact of scale economies
have effectively been restricted - in addition to the more or less inevi-
table geographical factors - by both the relative diseconomies of cargo
handling and inventory costs. Thus, the optimum ship size is closely
related to the actual trade and, in particular, the typical parcel sizes in
the specific sectors. Many shipping companies are therefore faced with
the fact that their customers are demanding ships which, in terms of
voyage costs, would be sub-optimal and, because employing a more
‘size-optimal' ship half empty would involve much worse diseconomies
of scale, they also have to build ships which fit the relevant markets.
Thus, it is no wonder that ‘maxi-size' tankers and bulk carriers still
remain less popular than some thirty or forty years ago. The slowly
growing average sizes in both fleets seem to reflect the fact that scale
economies are still working within a number of niches with medium-
sized ships. On the other hand, at least one major sector, general cargo
carriers, does not present any such trend.

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 83
However, the logistic formula of inverse relationships between ship
sizes and the value of cargo no longer holds when we come to the most
valuable end of the commodity scale. While it is true that finished con-
sumer goods are traded in small parcel sizes, rather by containerloads
than shiploads, such ‘general cargo' will normally be loaded on board a
common carrier like (the earlier) cargo liners or modern container ships.
In this case, the size of a single parcel is not important at all because a
vast number of these can be combined on one big ship, thus enjoying
all the scale economies that can be attained within the scope of actual
geographic constraints. Therefore it seems obvious that the rapid esca-
lation of ship sizes which really started in the 1990s will continue for
quite a while. As container ships are carrying light goods this progress
will not, in the short term, be much hampered by draft restrictions in
major ports. The main limiting factor will be the overall size and growth
of markets, which determine how well the increasingly big container
carriers can be filled with paying cargo. However, as the scope of con-
tainerizable goods is still widening, the growth may exceed the general
growth of maritime trade.
Even this coin has a reverse side. Containers are not only carried
from port to port but even from door to door. The overall logistics thus
involve a high number of collection and distribution terminals. In order
to harvest the best profits of size economies on long intercontinental
routes the big ships sailing to ‘hubs' need to be supplemented by feeder
ships cruising to and from the smaller ports. The growth of ocean traffic
therefore results in a proportional increase in short-sea container capac-
ity (see Figure 5.3). The size of feeders, however, may not automatically
rise because ports visited do not always allow that. Moreover, the opti-
mum feeder size has regularly been affected by two other constraints:
the overall size of the respective local markets and the frequency of
services required by customers. Although bigger ships could handle all
cargo by one trip a month a smaller one sailing each week usually sells
better. With the growth of traffic the latter factor will, however, be of
temporary importance, and at least on the major short-sea routes the
size of feeders has already started to grow.
Modern shipping revolutions have resulted from a complex interplay
of technological and economic development. For practical reasons, the
present discussion has focused on the macro-, or industry level, in the
economies of scale of ship sizes, not of firm sizes. Even the develop-
ment of port technology has been dealt with as a backdrop to shipping
technology, rather than as an independent variable affecting the over-
all efficiency of shipping and its different cost components. The basic

84 The Advantages of Water Carriage
assumption was that the economies of scale were an underlyingmodus
vivendi of shipping - as well as of many other industries - but this is
a rule which needs a few major qualifications. It seems that different
sectors (and sub-sectors) of shipping represent different scales, with
optimum sizes depending - at least in the short term - not only on the
technical efficiency of ships at sea and in port but also on the specific
demands of shippers. The actual balance between these elements, how-
ever, does not seem to have been stable: at least one major variable, the
freight level, has declined compared with many other producer costs.
Since longer-term data for certain relevant variables, such as port costs
and cargo handling rates, are not available a more precise estimation
would require quite a lot of additional research.
In spite of such qualifications, it is still reasonable to claim that, in
the long run, the most central feature of modern shipping has been the
growth of ship sizes. Around 1880, an average cargo vessel measured
about 500 gross register tons, or could load some 700 tons; in 2008 the
respective averages amounted to 16,500 (new) gross tons and 24,200
tons deadweight, which means that both tonnages had grown by a
factor of about 30. This has bestowed on shipping all the typical advan-
tages of large-scale production and contributed substantially to the low-
ering of maritime transport costs - and to maintaining ‘the advantages
of water carriage'. Of course, the increase in hauling efficiency has not
only been a function of mere size but also of a more efficient use of the
potential capacity. Still, having fewer and bigger units also brings sub-
stantial logistic advantages - at least as long as ship sizes comply with
other infrastructure. Supposing - if some counterfactual speculation
is allowed - that the average ship size were still some 500 gross tons,
it would require about 1.5 million vessels (instead of some 45,000) to
maintain the present global maritime transports, and in ports a fleet of
small ships of this kind would require ten times more quayside length
than the present world tonnage.

stacked. Accordingly, they carry more air in their cargo holds.
30. Unfortunately, as there are no systematic measurement data on the light
displacement of ships, the resulting improvements in deadweight capacities
cannot be estimated.
31. As early as the steamers of the 1880s, the volume of cargo spaces amounted
to c. 70% of their gross tonnage (the overall volume of the ship), a ratio
which actually exceeds that on many modern bulk carriers with their self-
trimming holds and big ballast water tanks. Cf. the data in Kaukiainen, ‘Tons
and Tonnages', Appendix tables.
32. ISL, Shipping Statistics Yearbook, special fleets.

## Yrjö Kaukiainen 87

Economics and Policy,6, pp. 128-135.
34. For subsequent discussion, see J. O. Jansson and D. Shneerson (1982) ‘The
Optimal Ship Size', Journal of Transport Economics and Policy,16, pp. 217-38;
P. Garrod and W. Miklius (1985) ‘The Optimal Ship Size: A Comment',
Journal of Transport Economics and Policy, 19, pp. 83-90. These studies, like
even the one by Kendall, only concern dry bulk shipping. Economies of scale
in liner shipping were discussed by J.O. Jansson and D. Shneerson (1985)
‘Economies of Trade Density in Liner Shipping and Optimal Pricing', Journal
of Transport Economics and Policy, 19, pp. 7-22.

http://www.marinetraffic.com/ (Swedish and Finnish ports), accessed 20
April 2011.
36. Cf. for instance Garrod and Miklius, ‘The Optimal Ship Size', pp. 86-89;

that big ships enjoyed extra large scale advantages on long routes (p. 130).
That, however, was a misinterpretation which resulted from the author sum-
ming up terminal and voyage costs, although only the latter of them is a
function of distance. Had only voyage costs been taken into account the scale
advantage of big ships on long trips would almost have vanished, as can be
seen from the flat freight-per-distance curves in Figure 5.4.

## 6
Building the Networks of Trade:
Perspectives on Twentieth-Century
Maritime History
Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok
The development of maritime history as a specialized field of research is
an impressive testament to the founders of the International Maritime
Economic History Association.1 Skip Fischer's own publications, together
with his relentless efforts as editor of theInternational Journal of Maritime
History, have contributed significantly to placing seaborne transport
at the centre of the international economic history of the nineteenth
century. As a result of the collective efforts made in the field since the
1970s, most economists, economic historians and general historians
now recognize the pivotal role played by the maritime sector in foster-
ing a truly global economy before World War One (WWI).
Much of the corresponding literature for the twentieth century,
however, points to a rather different conclusion. In a recent work of
synthesis, Ronald Findlay and Kevin O'Rourke argued that the defining
difference between nineteenth- and twentieth-century globalizations is
a shift in driving forces. According to Findlay and O'Rourke, the glo-
balization process of the nineteenth century had an ‘overwhelmingly
technological ... origin, with falling transportation costs driving com-
modity markets closer together', while the process during the twentieth
century was ‘much more political in origin'.2 Their conclusion - widely
shared - is that the maritime sector had little impact on the globaliza-
tion process characterizing the latter half of the twentieth century. This
view has not been seriously questioned by maritime historians, who
either have ignored the period or been reluctant to link their findings
to broader trends within the international economy of the period.3 The
consequence is that maritime history has been marginalized in ongoing
debates on the factors behind the expansion of the post-1950 interna-
tional economy.
88

Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok 89
This chapter discusses the role of the maritime sector in the interna-
tional economy during the latter half of the twentieth century. By doing
this we seek to accomplish two things. First, we wish to raise awareness
of the actual importance played by the maritime industry in shaping
the development of the international economy during this period.
Second, we also seek to encourage maritime historians to deal more
eagerly with post-1945 maritime history. Our argument also falls into
two parts. First, we present and discuss recent work in economic history
on the development of ocean transport and its role in the growth of the
international economy after World War Two (WWII). A key issue here
is the adequacy of existing measures of freight costs and their ability
to capture the influence of the maritime sector on the international
economy. While it has been repeatedly claimed that real freight costs
did not decline, we will argue that this interpretation hinges on some
technical assumptions that are debatable.
The second part of the chapter suggests an alternative approach. The
role of maritime transport in the development of the international
economy, we argue, cannot be evaluated on the basis of the price of
ocean transport alone. In this period, the maritime sector went through
dramatic technological, organizational and institutional shifts that
allowed for both rapid expansion of capacity and dramatic improve-
ments in the quality of services provided. To our knowledge, there
exist no studies that attempt to adjust for improvements in the qual-
ity of services in existing freight series. Inference from aggregate data
series may therefore be misleading. To find out how the technological,
organizational and institutional changes in the shipping industry have
affected international trade patterns, we think it is necessary to explore
in more detail how these preconditions for trade came into being. In
such a process, the study of the maritime sector and specifically the
actions of shipping entrepreneurs may provide substantial new insights.
We try to show the fruitfulness of such an approach by way of two case
studies on the post-WWII growth of trade in raw materials, more specifi-
cally coking coal and chemicals.
Shipping and post-WWII freight rate developments
According to the prominent maritime economist Martin Stopford,
the shipping industry of the post-1950 period went through a process
of unparalleled change. By 1980, Stopford argues, ‘there was noth-
ing left of the proud, conservative shipping industry which sailed
confidently into the 1950s'.4 While this is perhaps an overstatement,

90 Building the Networks of Trade
fundamental changes had indeed taken place: The small general cargo
carriers and tramp ships that still dominated merchant shipping in 1950
had gradually been replaced by large bulk carriers, container ships and
ships designed to carry specialized cargoes such as cars, chemicals and
liquefied gas. Scale economies, automation, standardization and spe-
cialization were applied to increase the cargo handling capacity and
the efficiency of the fleet. In addition, institutional changes such as
the increasing use of flags of convenience were introduced to cut costs.
In later years, organizational innovations in the operation of shipping
companies also caused a ‘decomposition of the value chain' within the
industry as a whole. Shipping companies increasingly tended to out-
source many of the operations that traditionally had been a natural part
of their day-to-day operations.5
Despite the compelling organizational, technological and institu-
tional evidence indicating radical changes in the transportation system,
economic historians have been reluctant to ascribe importance to the
maritime sector in the development of the twentieth- century interna-
tional economy. The primary evidence supporting such a stance is the
apparent stability in the cost of ocean transport. The Chicago-based
economist David Hummels has published what is probably the most
referenced analysis of the development of transportation costs in the
latter half of the twentieth century.6 Hummels' conclusion is, simply
put, that ocean freights did not in fact decline in real terms and hence
that the many obvious innovations in shipping during this period did
not play any fundamental role in the growth of world trade. Instead, he
points to the developments in air freight as a main driving force:
Ocean shipping, which constitute 99 percent of world trade by
weight... also experienced a technological revolution... but dramatic
price declines are not in evidence... Economic historians have argued
that technological change in ocean shipping was the critical input to
growing trade in the first era of globalization during the latter half of
the nineteenth century. I would argue that technological change in
air shipping and the declining cost of rapid transportation has been a
critical input into a second era of globalisation during the latter half
of the twentieth century.7
It has to be noted that Hummels does not deny that massive
productivity-enhancing transformations, both technological and insti-
tutional, occurred within the shipping sector during the second half of
the twentieth century. However, while not providing much detail, he

Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok 91
contends that most cost improvements provided by these transforma-
tions were countered by the fact that ‘input costs, including fuel, ship
prices, and port costs, were skyrocketing'.8
The views of Hummels have resonated both in the specialized lit-
erature and in more general works on the development of the inter-
national economy. In a much-cited article on British tramp shipping,
Mohammed and Williamson state that ‘the fall in freight rates in the
age of steam, has not been matched in the century since'.9 Along similar
lines Karl Gunnar Persson argues that ‘transport costs fell in the nine-
teenth century, but it is not clear that they have fallen since'.10 Focusing
specifically on the development of world trade, Findlay and O'Rourke
are also convinced that the real cost of shipping goods did not decline
in real terms in the post-war period. Rather, they argue the freights
were ‘remarkably stable... in sharp contrast to the sustained decline in
freight rates before 1914'.11 Consequently, they contend that the mas-
sive growth in international trade during the last part of the twentieth
century cannot be explained by reference to technological changes but
rather needs to be explained primarily by political factors.
Not all researchers of freight rate developments, however, agree with
these conclusions. In several articles Yrjö Kaukiainen has investigated
the development of post-WWII ocean freights. In contrast to works
cited above, his conclusion is that the decline in freight rates that char-
acterized the nineteenth century ‘continued in the twentieth century'.
Presenting figures on the development of freight ratios from 1820 to
the 1990s, Kaukiainen asserts that these ratios have declined substan-
tially and that ‘a decline of such proportions must have had a powerful
impact on global trade'.12 Looking more specifically at the post-WWII
freight rate development, Kaukiainen argues that the decline was par-
ticularly substantial for bulk freights. Hence, ‘between the late 1950s
and the turn of the millennium, average grain freights fell by seventy
per cent and coal freights by eighty per cent, which was much more
than between the 1870s and the 1910s'.13 Overall, then, his conclusion
is that ‘for the dry-bulk market, the period since the 1950s has been
characterized by a decline in freight rates comparable to what occurred
in the late nineteenth century'.14
At first sight these differing conclusions seem puzzling. They become
even more puzzling as Kaukiainen and Hummels base their tramp
freight estimates on the same data. Post-WWII tramp freight rates are
taken by both authors from the tramp trip charter price index origi-
nally constructed byNorwegian Shipping News and continued byLloyd's
Shipping Economist. The reason for the differing conclusions, however,

92 Building the Networks of Trade
is fairly simple: disagreement on the appropriate deflator. To convert
the freight rate figures from nominal toreal values, Kaukiainen uses the
British wholesale price index (up to 1965) and the US producer price
index. Using the same evidence, Hummels applies two alternative defla-
tors, a US GDP deflator as well as ‘a price index for bulk commodities
typically shipped via tramps'.15 Looking at Hummels' figures deflated
using a conventional GDP deflator, the story is much the same as
Kaukiainen's story: real term reduction in tramp freight rates to almost
a third of its 1950 level. It is by using a deflator similar to the ‘freight
factor' estimate first developed by Douglass North that Hummels finds
that real freight rates did not decline in the post-WW II period.16
The disagreements about the interpretation of freight rates after
WWII, then, boils down to a question of choice of deflators, and more
precisely how one should interpret the freight factor. Compared with
the more traditional GDP deflator, the freight factor, or commodity
deflator, is claimed to be more suitable when estimating the barriers
to trade posed by seaborne transport as the GDP deflator ‘includes
non-tradables and many tradables not carried on all routes'.17 The
basic idea is that when transport costs fall relative to the price of a
given product, the fall will stimulate trade in that product, similarly
to the way a fall inad valorem tariffs stimulates trade. If, on the other
hand, the relative price of transport is stable or increases, it hinders
trade growth. In fact, as Persson argues, ‘transport costs can be seen
as an implicit ad valorem tariff'.18 It is on this basis that Hummels
provides the following overall conclusion: ‘While the cost of ship-
ping a ton of wheat or iron ore has steadily declined, the cost of
shipping a dollar value of wheat or iron ore has not.'19 Based on the
same figures, his conclusion in an earlier paper was similar: ‘the price
of bulk commodities has fallen faster than the unit cost of tramp
shipping,yielding no change or even increase in the barrier to trade posed
by international transport'.20
The use of commodity deflators seem to be accepted in the general lit-
erature as the most appropriate when studying the relationship between
trade growth and ocean transport. Based on this measure, there also
seems to be general agreement on the conclusion that real freight costs
did not decline during the post-war period, despite the many obvious
technological, organizational and institutional transformations in ship-
ping. Finally, when explaining why the real freight cost did not decline
despite these obvious improvements, the overall claim is that any cost
saving achieved was countered by rising input costs. These assertions
have some weaknesses, however.

Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok 93
First, macro-level estimates of the development of transport costs as
a proportion of the total value of international trade - the overall ‘freight
factor' - during the twentieth century are not fully conclusive when it
comes to the overall trend. Looking at the century as a whole there is
little doubt that the freight factor has actually declined. Available data
from UNCTAD shows that the cost of transport represented between
three and five per cent of import value in most developed countries at
the beginning of the twenty-first century, while data from North show
the percentage in 1900 to be around ten.21 Clearly, the entire decline did
not occur before WWII. According to the commodity deflated freight
rate indices presented by Mohammed and Williamson, a continued
downward trend is clearly detectable from the late 1930s and until
the mid-1980s, before there is a slight increase until the figures end in
1994. Hence the conclusion that the freight factors show ‘no downward
trend' is at least disputable.22
A second challenge relates to the actual appropriateness of commod-
ity deflated indices when estimating freight costs. As pointed out by
Kaukiainen, the price of the cargoes that are shipped via tramps and the
price of ocean transport are often interrelated. When freight rate levels
fall, the supply of a given product - and hence the price competition -
typically increases, as more distant sources can be effectively brought
to the market.23 Hence, declining freight rates can cause a decline in
commodity prices. In other instances, the cycles of freights and com-
modity prices are not harmonized at all, causing random fluctuations
in the freight factor. An obvious example is the oil price shock of the
mid-1970s. With drastic increases in oil prices, the ratio of oil price to
oil freights increased dramatically, and hence it would seem as if the
real cost of transporting oil decreased much more dramatically than it
actually did.24
The claim that real freight costs - or the freight ratios - did not decline
after WWII because the price of ‘key inputs, such as fuel, wages and
shipbuilding costs' increased, is also problematic.25 Again, the most com-
prehensible investigation of these factors has been done by Kaukiainen.
Investigating developments in capital and operating costs for grain and
coal bulk carriers, he shows that the actual building price per ton has
decreased substantially. Between 1955 and 1980 the price decreased
by 0.2 per cent per annum and from 1980 to 2000 the annual decline
reached 4.9 per cent.26 As for operating costs, these have also declined
substantially. The most important element in these costs is of course
wages. It is clear that absolute wage levels in the traditional western
European shipping nations increased substantially during the post-war

94 Building the Networks of Trade
years. The parallel growth in the size of the ships decreased man-hours in
relation to tons, but it could not prevent an upward movement in labour
costs. However, gradually most industrial countries lost their fleets to
low-cost registries, and expensive labour was replaced by cheaper labour.
The creation of alternative, national registries such as the Norwegian and
Danish International Shipping Registers also contributed to this trend.
Overall, then, as Kaukiainen puts it, as regards labour costs there were
probably ‘substantial decreases in these costs after 1980'.27 Fuel costs also
‘diminished with increasing ship size'. Of course, the price shocks of the
1970s and also the price increases of the early twenty-first century caused
an increase in costs. But overall, by 2000 bunkers per dwt had decreased
to a third of their 1950 level for coal carriers and to 60 per cent for grain
carriers.28 Finally, while the trend in port costs is difficult to estimate
and hence less clear, the assumption that these have been ‘skyrocketing'
is not backed by convincing evidence.29 Rather, most indicators would
suggest a decline in these costs as well. It is evident that port technology
has improved substantially. Estimates of labour productivity in ports also
indicate quite substantial improvements. Increasing competition between
ports has also ‘resulted in a wave of privatization and other measures to
increase price efficiency'.30 Hence there seem to be little to support the
claim that key inputs to the shipping industry experienced a substantial
price increase. On the contrary, for many major inputs a rather substan-
tial price decline is in evidence.
While the preceding discussion has focused on various freight rate
estimates and the choice of deflators, the more underlying measure-
ment challenges also warrant attention. Most scholars seem to implic-
itly assume that the quoted freight rates assembled by Norwegian
Shipping News reflect the market price for services that did not change
throughout the period. With a wide array of technological, organiza-
tional and institutional shifts occurring, it seems at least plausible that
some improvements in quality have occurred, introducing measure-
ment biases in the underlying indices.31 The almost exclusive focus
on the global shipping cost for a few selected commodities may also
disguise the fact that technological shifts may have had different con-
sequences for different regions and for different industries.
As a result, we believe that the development of ocean transporta-
tion costs in the twentieth century and the impact on international
trade warrants further scholarly attention. One potential avenue for
understanding these processes would be to focus on innovation proc-
esses that occurred in various sub-markets of the shipping industry in
the post-WWII era. Focusing on such meso-levels of analysis facilitates

Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok 95
a more precise identification of key players as well as a more fine-
grained understanding of the economic consequences of the shifts for
industries relying on transportation services. Maritime entrepreneurs
often played key roles when attempting to introduce new technologies.
They were in a position to link together the various actors and processes
that had to be coordinated in order for the carrying capacity to expand
as fast as it did and in order for the new, drastically enlarged transport
system to function efficiently. Understanding how these processes came
about should be regarded as an essential feature in any broad historical
analysis of the growth of the international economy. In the subsequent
section we try to show the fruitfulness of such an approach by investi-
gating in detail some of the preconditions for the substantial post-war
growth in the trade in raw materials, a fundamental characteristic of the
post-WWII globalization process.
Building the networks of trade
Between 1960 and 1990 total transport of industrial raw materials
almost quadrupled.32 This massive growth of trade in industrial raw
materials could not have been accomplished without groundbreaking
transformations in the existing transport system. To be able to carry
all this trade, the total volume of shipping room had to be drastically
enlarged. And indeed, between 1960 and 1975 alone, the total ton-
nage of dry bulk carriers increased more than fivefold, from 15 to 85
million gross tons.33 Completely new types of ships were developed in
this process, such as specialized dry bulk carriers and various types of
combination carriers. And, of course, the average size of these ships was
substantially enlarged. While Liberty ships at about 10,000 tons dead-
weight (dwt) carried most of the dry bulk shipments in 1950, by the mid
1970s, the size of an average dry bulk carrier was about 30,000 dwt. By
2006 it had reached 56,000 dwt.34
All in all, the growth of the post-war raw materials trade rested on a
series of transformations and improvements in the existing seaborne
transport system. When explaining international trade growth, such
processes are, however, seldom recognized as important and instead
taken as given. But, by neglecting to investigate how technological and
institutional preconditions for trade are in fact developed, one only
gets part of the story of international trade growth. Individuals have to
establish the conditions necessary for that trade to actually take place.
An adequate historical understanding of trade growth should put these
processes at centre stage.

96 Building the Networks of Trade
A good place to start such an enquiry is to study the actions of ship-
ping entrepreneurs, and their role in the growth of a specific trade.
Take as a first example the growth of trade in coking coal. Coking coal
is a major raw material in the steel industry and the increasing trade
reflected the growth of steel production both in Europe and in Japan.
The leading suppliers of coal were the North Americans and later
Australia and Indonesia. In 1950 general purpose carriers such as the
Liberty ships still handled most of the coal trade. In fact, so dominant
was the Liberty ship in this trade that coal (as well as iron ore, grain
and bauxite) was traded in standard ‘Liberty ship loads'. The increasing
demand for long-haul transport of coal implied that much could be
gained by increasing the size of the ships. Steelworks were increasingly
moved to the seaside to eliminate the need for landward transport of
incoming coal and ores. However, the most substantial savings could be
accomplished by making the seaborne leg less costly. Shipping entrepre-
neurs came to play a vital role in this process.
The price of steel was relevant to shipowners. If the price of steel
could be reduced, ships would be cheaper to build. This was the main
idea behind the New York-based Norwegian shipowner Erling Dekke
Næss' initial interest in the coal trade. When Næss became interested
in how coal was transported, he had already developed a substantial
tanker business, with a total of 20 new buildings on order by 1957.35
When developing the tanker fleet, his principal strategy had been
to invest in larger vessels in order to reap economies of scale in ship
design. When first learning about the coal trade, Næss found that there
had been few attempts at introducing similar strategies, and came to
believe that there was an economic potential in building large, bulk
carriers specially designed for transporting coal. In other words, there
was money to be made by replicating the large-scale bulk strategy pre-
viously applied in the tanker trade. By doing so, the cost of steel could
also be lowered, in turn reducing the new-build price of tankers, as
well as other ships. Introducing large ships in the coal trade thus had a
double motivation for shipowners. The operation would be profitable,
and the ultimate consequences in terms of reduced steel prices were also
economically advantageous.
Long before any large coal-carrying bulk ships could be ordered, numer-
ous challenges had to be evaluated and overcome. For Næss, the first
steps were taken in the period 1956 to 1958, when he organized a series
of meetings with American coal exporters.36 The Hampton Roads coal
exporters were at this point still the leading providers of coking coal to the
Japanese steel industry. Shipments had traditionally been made in Liberty

Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok 97
ships, but were now increasingly taken over by slightly larger bulk carriers
of 15,000-17,000 dwt. Næss sought to negotiate contracts of affreight-
ment with the exporters using ships twice this size. The first question was:
Could the exporters produce the amount of coal necessary efficiently to
serve a fleet of such big ships? Second: How could the coal be transported
to the seaside? Traditional coal trucks carried 70 tons of coal. With ships
of 35,000 dwt, 500 trucks were needed to serve one ship, making the
arrangement overly costly and difficult to manage. In order to introduce
larger vessels in the trade, new arrangements had to be made with rail-
road companies establishing landward transportation systems capable of
handling the increased load. Third, new contracts also had to be negoti-
ated and signed with the customers purchasing the coal. Obvious aspects
such as price, quality and duration of the contract were only some of the
problems that had to be dealt with. Could Japanese steel mills handle the
large amounts of coal that Næss wanted to deliver in one load? Did they
have the port facilities necessary to handle the ships? Finally, a number
of other practical matters - such as the size of the Panama Canal toll and
the wages and insurance levels for the crews manning the ships - also had
to be solved. Not until all these unanswered question were settled, could
the final contracts be signed. The time had then come to visits the banks
to arrange finance, before the orders for the ships could be placed with
the shipyards. As Næss himself explains: ‘It all took time, patience and
persistence.'37 In 1961, six years after the first contacts had been made
with the coal exporters, the first bulk carriers were delivered. The expan-
sion of the coal trade could continue at a new pace.
The growth in raw materials trade was, however, not confined to the
traditional commodities such as coal, grain and ores. One of the fast-
est growing commodities in world trade during the second half of the
twentieth century were chemicals.38 These include organic products, the
olefins and the aromatics, as well as inorganic products such as phos-
phoric acid and caustic soda, vegetable oils and molasses.39 Before 1950,
the scale of trade in these products was minuscule. In fact, many of the
products had never been traded overseas, either because they were too
difficult to transport or simply because they were new. Growing use of
plastics, the expansion of the aluminium and fertilizer industries, as
well as a growing market for vegetable oils and molasses, all contributed
to the growth of international chemical trade. Even more so than in the
coal trade, however, the technological and organizational challenges
that had to be overcome to ensure rapid expansion were substantial.
In order for these products to be efficiently traded across long
distances, completely new types of ships had to be envisaged and

98 Building the Networks of Trade
developed. So-called parcel tankers capable of carrying a large variety of
chemicals in bulk were introduced and the ship type gradually devel-
oped to become a major niche in world shipping. By 1975, a total of
341 chemical tankers were registered inLloyd's Register of Shipping. With
a total gross registered tonnage of about 960,000, chemical tankers
made up about 0.3 per cent, in tonnage terms, of the entire world fleet.
By 2008 the number of registered chemical tankers had reached 3397.
By now, about 5 per cent of the entire fleet of merchant vessels were
chemical tankers.40
It is not clear who actually constructed the first chemical parcel tanker.
According to Murphy and Tenold, ‘the world's first purpose built parcel
tanker' was the ship Svanaas, owned by the Norwegian Iver Bugge and
launched in 1949. According to these authors, the ship could take on ‘a
range of products including solvents, lube oils and some benign chemi-
cals'. However, according to the official biography of another Norwegian
chemical tanker owner - Jacob Stolt-Nielsen - the Svanaas and its three
sister ships - were ‘not suitable for the transport of chemicals or solvent'.
Rather they could primarily take on lubricating oils, vegetable oils, tallow
and grease. Accordingly, these ships were apparently known under the
somewhat condescending name of ‘lubricating oil ships'. Instead, this
biography claims that it was Stolt-Nielsen who was ‘the first to develop
and operate a so-called parcel tanker without the cargo being contami-
nated', and that his converted tankerMT Freddy, launched in 1955, ‘made
history by being the world's first parcel tanker'.41 This view resonates
with the general literature. Martin Stopford cites at length Stolt-Nielsen's
own heroic description of how he constructed the first parcel tanker with
‘no drawings, no naval architects, and no price from the yard'.42 By this
point, however, several parcel tankers had already been launched. As early
as 1948 Union Carbide had converted the T2 tanker R.E. Wilson to carry
nine different chemicals in bulk, while in 1954, according to Murphy
and Tenold, ‘Dow pioneered the first large tanker to exclusively carry
chemicals in bulk'. The ship was at least as technologically sophisticated
as Stolt-Nielsen's apparently revolutionary vessel. It could carry eleven
chemical cargoes with different characteristics and was constructed with
‘double bulkhead to separate the tanks … separate transfer systems, pipes
and connections'.43
Irrespective of who actually launched the first ship, the parcel tanker
soon became what Murphy and Tenold describe as ‘the favoured vessel
for the carriage of a wide range of chemicals'. The obvious reason was, as
the same authors write, that ‘this kind of transport gave substantial cost
reductions relative to shipments in individual containers on conventional

Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok 99
ships'.44 Hence the ships clearly contributed to a decline in the cost of
transporting chemicals across long distances and hence stimulated the
trade in these products. This fact was pointed out by Stolt-Nielsen him-
self who, in an interview with the New York Times in 1963, noted that
‘Before we started exporters here were paying $35 to $45 a ton to Japan,
for example. Our rates today are $16 to $20 a ton.'45
Reduction in the price of overseas chemical transport was not the
only effect of he launching of the parcel tanker. Increasing techno-
logical sophistication in the construction of these ships throughout the
1960s and 1970s meant that products that for safety or other reasons
had been debarred from intercontinental trade now became tradable.
By developing parcel tankers with stainless steel tanks, the Norwegian
shipping company Odfjell for the first time made possible efficient
and secure trade in products such as sulphuric and phosphoric acid.46
Hence, also within the chemical trade, the innovative abilities of entre-
preneurs played a substantial role in creating the preconditions for this
trade to expand. Without innovations such as the parcel tanker and
the stainless steel tanks, international trade in chemicals would have
developed much more slowly. As Murphy and Tenold conclude, ‘The
technological innovations and transport cost reductions associated with
parcel tankers are important explanations for the growth of the interna-
tional trade in chemicals'.47
Increasing the actual shipping room and developing ships capable of
carrying new and advanced types of products were not the only techno-
logical improvements needed for the international raw materials trade
to increase as fast and efficiently as it did. Land-based facilities also had
to be improved. For the new and enlarged bulk carriers this involved
the construction of larger and deeper ports, the building of berths with
loading and unloading facilities and also the development of efficient
land-based transport facilities capable of serving the port with raw
materials at a pace and scale congruent with the capacity of the bulk
ships. The efficient operation of overseas chemical transport required
the development of specially designed port terminals capable of storing
difficult and potentially dangerous chemical cargoes.
These technological improvements in turn depended on a series of
organizational and institutional transformations. For the bulk carriers to
be operated efficiently, their development had to be coordinated with
the producers of raw materials, who needed to be able to deliver, on
schedule, the increased amount of cargo the bulk carriers could carry.
The same was true of the chemical plants. New solutions also had to be
developed in order to finance the building of the increasingly large and

100 Building the Networks of Trade
hence also increasingly expensive bulk carriers. While the price per ton
of such carriers decreased, their radically increased size meant that the
amount of money that needed to be raised to finance a single ship was
becoming substantial. And increasingly, such funds had to be obtained
from banks or trough yard credit. According to a recent study, the replace-
ment ratio - that is, the value of an old vessel relative to the cost of a
new build - decreased substantially during the 1960s and 1970s. This
meant simply that in order to finance the building of a new bulk carrier,
less and less equity could be raised by selling off old tonnage and more
and more equity had to be obtained from other sources.48 This was also
true for the much smaller chemical tankers, which became increasingly
expensive due to their technological sophistication and use of expensive
building material. In fact, building a 10,000 dwt chemical tanker in the
late 1960s would cost six times as much per ton as building a 200,000
dwt crude oil tanker.49 These increased financial requirements implied
the development of new financial arrangements. An important element
was the acceptance of long-term freight contracts as collateral. This again
transformed the dominant relationship between the shipper and the
shipowner operating within the traditional tramp sector. From an open
market based structure, more and more of the traditional tramp trades
became organized within a closed contract based structure.
The simple point to be made from these examples is that the expansion
of international trade - in our examples the coal and the chemical trades -
was not an automatic process. It required people to take action and trans-
form the whole transport system on which the trade was based. Shipowners
stood at the centre of these processes. They were central in developing new
ship designs, in linking together the producers and receivers of raw materi-
als, and in organizing new and innovative ways of financing and building
the ships needed to take care of the growing trade. As Næss puts it: ‘The
job was to tie up the links in the chain. The steel companies would buy
the coking coal to produce the steel which the shipyards would use to
build the ships which I would order to carry the coal.'50 Historical analysis
of these trends in the development of shipping provides us with important
new insights into how the expansion of the international economy during
the post-war years unfolded and was accomplished.
Conclusion: maritime history as international
economic history
Despite convincing evidence for a dramatic technological, organiza-
tional and institutional revolution within the shipping sector after

Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok 101
WWII, few scholars outside the field are prepared to assign to the ship-
ping sector any importance in fostering a more widespread interna-
tional economic integration in the period. General economic historians
stick to the conclusion that as long as the commodity-deflated price of
seaborne transport apparently remained stable during the second half
of the twentieth century, the contribution from the maritime sector
in increasing the level of international trade must have been limited.
Maritime historians have only shown limited interest in post-1945
development trends, and to the extent that they have dealt with such
issues, they have largely refrained from linking their findings to more
general aspects of the international economy. As a consequence, the
prevalent interpretation remains that while the shipping sector pro-
vided a crucial stimulus for the integration of the world economy before
1914, the same role is ascribed after 1945 to trade liberalization, and
shipping is largely left out of the equation.
In this chapter we have questioned the viability of this interpretation
and, as an attempt to bypass it, argued the need for maritime historians
to engage more eagerly with twentieth-century development trends.
First we discussed existing interpretations of freight rate developments.
While data supporting a substantial decline in freight rates for crucial
commodities during the post-war period have been widely published,
the usual practice of deflating the freight rate with the price of the
transported commodity has led scholars to conclude that real freight
rates have not declined. As suggested above, we believe that it is equally
reasonable to argue that the fall in prices of industrial raw materials
and freight rates were related processes, as lowered transportation costs
increased availability and price competition for key industrial raw mate-
rials. Using such a deflator may therefore yield misleading results.
The dual process of decline in freight rates and the falling price of key
industrial raw materials had substantial consequences. The location of
industrial production had, since the industrial revolution, been heavily
determined by the availability of industrial raw materials, and this was
still very much the case in the early 1950s. In the following decades,
however, new industrial centres relying on importation of such materi-
als emerged (most notably Japan), and industrial production economies
spread to new geographical destinations. The establishment of an inter-
national market for key industrial raw materials thus removed a critical
limitation for emerging industrial centres and served as a preamble to
later developments of geographically dispersed production regimes.
The emergence of truly global production chains, where raw materials,
intermediary goods, and final commodities are traded between nations

102 Building the Networks of Trade
and geographically scattered industrial centres, is, of course, highly
dependent on the development of modern transportation systems. The
demand for increasingly complex shipping services was met by a mas-
sive expansion of capacity as well as crucial changes in quality, not eas-
ily captured in existing data on freight rates. Put simply, it is extremely
difficult to envisage that the transportation needs of the industrial sec-
tor at the outset of the twenty-first century could have been served by
Liberty ships and manual loading of goods. In the absence of massive
technological and organizational improvements, it is more realistic to
expect increased transportation costs, and severe difficulties in sustain-
ing the global industrial economy that has emerged since WWII.
The adoption of new organizational models and technologies is often
described in a manner that conveys the impression that these strategies
faced few challenges. As an alternative hypothesis, we would suggest
that the development and adoption of certain aspects of modern bulk
handling system was a very complicated and challenging process for
the participants. While the advantages of adopting new technologies
are obvious with the benefit of hindsight, participants were faced with
several risks when committing resources to such projects. First of all,
the development of specialized bulk handling vessels demanded invest-
ments in new ships where the cost could not be recouped by the ship-
owner if the deal went sour. Second, the development of new loading
arrangements demanded coordinated commitment of investments in
specialized equipment, and represented high risks for participants not
knowing who would reap the economic gains of such investments. The
technologies adopted in the shipping sector thus shared characteristics
that tend to predetermine market failure and prompt vertical integra-
tion, but only rarely do we find this pattern adopted in the sector.
Our examples from the coal and chemical trades provide some hints
of how maritime historians can illuminate these processes. In these sec-
tors, shipping entrepreneurs took key initiatives in developing new tech-
nologies, but succeeded only because of their ability to mobilize resources
from participants outside the shipping industry. It is exactly this ability
to transgress the shipping sector - in coordinating the interests of par-
ties across the value chain - that gave the shipping entrepreneurs in
our examples their central position. Identifying such transnational and
trans-sectoral entrepreneurial networks may allow us to understand more
fully the innovation processes at work in the international transporta-
tion sector after WWII. Placing shipping entrepreneurs at the centre of
such frameworks may also serve as a complement to research focusing
on individual shipowners or on the rise and fall of merchant fleets. This,

Espen Ekberg, Even Lange and Eivind Merok 103
in turn, may also help us to identify more precisely how changes in
the transportation industry came about, and what impact they had on
global economic development. Maritime historians should feel a special
obligation to take on this task. We need to reaffirm one of the main aims
formulated by Skip Fischer and Helge Nordvik in their editorial to the first
issue of theInternational Journal of Maritime History: to ‘encourage authors
to place their studies within the context of the broader questions being
posed by historians in general'.51
