## 9
Why They are Tall and We are
Small! Competition between
Antwerp and Rotterdam in the
Twentieth Century
Stephan Vanfraechem
This chapter considers the results of micro-economic and political-in-
stitutional research that has been carried out into the ports of Antwerp
and Rotterdam and investigates whether it can help to understand
why the two ports have made quite different choices as regards labour
organization on the docks.1 Despite the fact that ports like Antwerp,
Hamburg, Bremen, Le Havre and Rotterdam all share the same hin-
terland and therefore compete with each other for market share, com-
petition between specifically Antwerp and Rotterdam has become an
important focus of attention in previous research projects.2
Citius, altius, fortius: from sail to steam
From the mid-nineteenth century the steamship gradually came into
service. The steam engine and the iron ships were stronger, more pow-
erful and more flexible. More power led to higher speed and greater
capacity. The considerably higher running-cost of those steamships
forced shipping companies, port authorities and cargo handlers to adapt
and change the organization both on board the ships and also on shore.
Because of the higher running costs of the steam vessels, the ‘dead'
time in the port had to be reduced to more realistic, economic terms, so
the loading and unloading of cargo needed to be speeded up. Because
of the volumes and the need for a faster turnaround, cargo handling
became a more specialized business.
In Antwerp cargo handling and especially the transport and storage
of the goods had traditionally been dominated by the naties. The crew
of the ship took care of the actual loading or unloading of the ship and
delivered or received the goods to and from the naties who brought the
142

## Stephan Vanfraechem 143
goods under the crane or transported the goods from under the crane to a
warehouse or shed.Naties had been active in the port since the thirteenth
century. Originally they were no more than a group of labourers working
together in a co-operative structure, and initially this meant little more
than working together and afterwards sharing profits. Gradually, the
expansion of the port led to a more formal structure of naties with their
own rules and procedures. As a consequence of this formalization, from
the fifteenth century onwards people who wanted to enter this business
had to buy a share of the natiee and thus became formal shareholders of
their company. As from then various companies of naties emerged and
the members of the naties gradually started buying the necessary equip-
ment as a group. As the port and hence the cargo handling business grew
further, this core group of natiee companies with ‘natie-men' became too
rigid to cope with all the work, so from the seventeenth century the
naties started hiring casual labourers. Unlike the natie-men, these casual
workers were not formal shareholders of the company but were hired on
a daily basis and were paid a formal wage for the service they rendered
that day. They could work for variousnatiee companies.3
In Rotterdam the vemen offered more or less the same services as the
naties did in Antwerp.4 The vemen were active in transport, distribution
and warehousing. As in Antwerp - and in other ports - they worked
together with the crew to load and unload ships.
With the introduction of the steam vessel, the need for a faster turna-
round of the vessel, combined with the larger volumes, transformed cargo
handling in most (European) ports into a more specialized business. The
ship's crew were no longer able to load or unload their vessel themselves.
In both Antwerp and in Rotterdam specialized stevedoring companies
developed during the last quarter of the nineteenth century, in Antwerp
at least often founded and owned by former captains.5 These stevedores
made exclusive use of casual labour. An ‘army' of casual labourers was
formed in both ports. In 1899 the Arbeidsbladd - the official bulletin of the
Belgian Ministry of Labour - estimated the number of dockers in Antwerp
at around 25,000.6 In Rotterdam an equal figure is mentioned.7
In Rotterdam, the difference between the traditional vemen and the
new stevedoring companies was to fade very soon. Veem and steve-
doring activities were combined in vertical integrated companies. In
Antwerp, however, a clear distinction between the traditional naties and
the stevedoring companies remained. Both types of companies stayed
within their own territory: the naties remained active in transport, dis-
tribution and warehousing, whereas the actual loading and unloading
of the vessels was the sole terrain of the stevedores.8

144 The Evolution of Antwerp and Rotterdam
This clear division of labour between thenaties and the stevedores in
the Antwerp port had both long- and short-term effects. In the short
term, shipping companies complained about this unnecessary compli-
cation of the cargo handling and also the lack of transparency: it led to
situations where the natie had to wait for the stevedore to unload the
ship, or vice versa where the stevedore had to wait for thenatie to bring
the goods under the crane.
In the longer term, and unlike the port of Rotterdam where vertical
integration led to bigger (and more substantial) companies, the small
and medium-sized companies also remained dominant in Antwerp.
The naties in particular remained very small companies, mostly family
businesses and most of the time undercapitalized. Most of the naties
were still organized on a co-operative basis: people who wanted to
enter the companies had to buy shares and thus became co-owners of
the company. In practice most naties became family businesses where
the son inherited or took over the shares of his father. These com-
panies did not have a broad or solid financial base: at the end of the
year the profits were split up and distributed amongst the sharehold-
ers rather than re-invested in the company. Since capital flowed back
to the shareholders there was no surplus that could be re-invested.
There was also little need for investment. For instance, investment
in the port infrastructure was the sole responsibility of the city: the
city authorities were responsible for and financed the construction
of the docks, quays, canals, cranes, warehouses, etc.9 The stevedores
and naties hired quays, warehouses and cranes from the authorities
and did not have to invest themselves in their infrastructure. The
city council was also very reluctant to grant long-term concessions to
private operators for fear of monopolies. This fear of monopoly was
shared by maritime circles also. Furthermore, the city council, in a
bid to avoid specialization, opted for a general cargo port where all
facilities (warehouses, cranes, etc.) were as universal as possible. The
exception to this rule was the grain and oil business where the city
council, though unwilling to invest in specialized warehouses and
tanks, was forced to do so by trading companies. It was forced, for
example, to grant a long-term concession to the Magasins à Grains
d'Anvers (SAMGA) in 1891 to build a grain silo at their own expense.
Long-term concessions, however, were very rare prior to World War I.
As a consequence the majority of naties and stevedores invested very
little in infrastructure and depended on the investments made by the
city council; they all seemed happy with this arrangement. For the
naties and stevedores the hiring of infrastructure such as warehouses

## Stephan Vanfraechem 145
and cranes from the city council seemed the best guarantee of
keeping large foreign companies out of the port of Antwerp.10
This hanseatic tradition, where the local municipality played a very
active role in the development of the port - mainly as a financer of
infrastructure - would remain until World War II. In the long term this
aloofness or absence of the cargo handlers from investment in port
infrastructure led to conservatism. Thenatieswere not aware of techno-
logical innovations and were not willing to invest in new technologies
and specialized terminals. The same was true of the stevedoring com-
panies: though not organized on a co-operative basis, they too were
mostly under-capitalized and were slow to invest in their own installa-
tions for specific cargo.
Nor did the organization of labour and dominant use of casual labour
force thenaties and the stevedores into investments. Although the turn
of the century confronted the employers with trade unions and strikes
and subsequently the demand for permanent employment instead
of casual labour, naties and stevedores remained assured of a stable,
large army of casual labourers.11 The introduction of a National Joint
Committee established a more or less formal procedure of collective
bargaining, wage agreements and, at the beginning of the 1920s, even
a link with the consumer prices index. The employers' permanent wage
bill remained very low: if they needed dock workers they hired them on
a daily basis without any further financial obligation for the future.
In Rotterdam we see a different picture. In the first place the division
of labour was not as strict as in Antwerp. Thevemen and stevedores were
more integrated, looking after the whole supply chain from the berthing
of the ship to the storage of the goods in the warehouses and vice versa.
This prevented loss of time and also made things more transparent.
Secondly, the cargo handlers took a more proactive approach towards
technological innovation and were supported in this - but also de facto
forced - by the city council.12 Unlike their colleagues in Antwerp, the
Rotterdam city council and the Rotterdam port authority opted for spe-
cialization. Dedicated facilities and technology received much greater
stimulation than in Antwerp. There was also a bigger incentive or
necessity for private companies to be more proactive. In Rotterdam the
national government invested in the maritime and continental access
to the port while the city council focused on maritime infrastructure
within the port, such as docks and quays. Investment in infrastructure
was left entirely to the private sector as from the beginning of the twen-
tieth century. The city stopped investing in cranes, warehouses, sheds,
etc., so private companies had no choice but to invest in their own

146 The Evolution of Antwerp and Rotterdam
infrastructure. In Rotterdam companies were also given the opportunity
to take long-term leases. Stevedoring companies obtained their own
terminals. The city invested in the docks and quay walls but all super-
structure was built and paid for by private companies. In Antwerp, until
World War II, the naties and stevedores could not - but also seemed
reluctant to - get long-term leases; they made use of public superstruc-
ture which was equipped mainly for general cargo.
In Rotterdam the trend towards specialization led, from the end of
the nineteenth century, to the introduction of modern technology like
floating grain elevators and floating/shore based grab cranes. Rotterdam
now gained a clear technological advantage over Antwerp in bulk han-
dling technology. Rotterdam cargo handling companies were much
more flexible, service-oriented and innovative than their Antwerp col-
leagues. This gave the Rotterdam port a competitive advantage.
The fact that in Rotterdam the port authority and the municipal
and national authorities - stepped aside much earlier, compared to
Antwerp, where investments in superstructure were concerned, led to
different kinds of companies in the two ports. Since the Rotterdam
stevedores were - at least to a much larger extent than in Antwerp -
financially involved in the development of their ‘own' port and had
to invest in their own cranes, warehouses and terminals, they needed
a much sounder financial basis. In Rotterdam profits were re-invested
in the company rather than simply flowing back to the shareholders
as seems to have been the case in Antwerp with the naties. In contrast
to Rotterdam, Antwerp cargo handlers were small, profit-oriented and
uninterested in vertical or horizontal integration. This would have long-
term effects, as we shall see further on.
World War II: short- and long-term effects on port
infrastructure, labour costs and labour organization
The effects of World War II on North Sea ports were enormous.
Both Antwerp and Rotterdam were used by the German occupying
forces to supply their troops.13 Once the Allied troops had landed in
Normandy and the Allied victory seemed inevitable, German troops
started preparing to destroy port infrastructure in Belgium and the
Netherlands to prevent the use of both ports as a supply centre by
the Allied troops. The swift Allied advance and the Belgian resistance
surprised the German troops in the port of Antwerp: they had to flee
before they could finish their preparations, leaving the port almost
untouched.

## Stephan Vanfraechem 147
In Rotterdam, however, the destruction of the port was almost com-
plete. Quays had been bombed, cranes and other superstructure had
been transported to Germany, the Nieuwe Waterweg been blocked by
the sinking of ships. Once the Allied troops had arrived in the port
they could hardly fail to see the damage done: 42 per cent of the total
quay length had been blown away, 35 per cent of the warehouses were
destroyed and the storage capacity for bulk-goods was reduced by 90
per cent.14
Antwerp benefited in the short term: since its main competitor lay
in ruins, the Allies had to use Antwerp as the main supply centre for
their troops. Very shortly after liberation the Antwerp port was used to
almost full capacity by the Allied forces. Meanwhile the Dutch govern-
ment had been re-installed and quickly understood that the reconstruc-
tion of the Rotterdam port was of national importance, essential for the
full economic recovery of the whole country, hence a financial priority.
With the help of the Marshall funds a completely new port rose out of
its ashes. The Rotterdam port became partially operational from 1946,
reaching almost full capacity in 1948.
Antwerp was now confronted with the downside of escaping the war
almost intact. It would struggle until the mid-1960s to overcome the
long-term effects of this immediate post-war period. In sharp contrast
with the short-term effects, the long-term effects of the fact that the
port was relatively intact after the war were very negative: the port of
Antwerp entered the post-war period with exactly the same port infra-
and super-structure as during the interwar period. In 1950 most cranes
in Antwerp were over 30 years old.15 Now that Rotterdam had been
completely rebuilt and was equipped with the most modern cranes
and other equipment it became painfully clear that the Antwerp port
was simply outdated: the superstructure was no match for Rotterdam.
In 1948 Rotterdam overtook Antwerp and would leave it (far) behind
forever.
For solutions the port companies looked directly to the municipal
and national authorities. The local authorities however lacked financial
means and budgetary scope to invest in the port. The large investments
that were made by the city council in the 1930s in order to make the
port more competitive and ready for the expected economic recovery
had clipped the council's wings. After World War II the council was still
paying for the investments of the 1930s and was simply not able to
launch a new wave of investments. This was an irreversible evolution:
the city council would no longer be able to play the active role of the
port builder or port architect that they had played since the beginning

148 The Evolution of Antwerp and Rotterdam
of the ‘modern' port in the 1870s and 1880s. The council would now
put more responsibility in the hands of the cargo handlers themselves.
The city did continue, together with the national government, to invest
in the docks and the construction of the quays but left everything
concerning superstructure to the companies themselves. They were
expected to invest in cranes, specialized handling equipment and the
construction of warehouses.
Most likely because of their conservatism and small scale, and lacking
good forward-looking management, it was quite some time before the
cargo handlers actually realized that they would have to play a more active
role and could no longer wait until the ripe fruits would come falling off
the trees. The overall figures of inward- and outward-bound goods for the
period 1948-55 show that Antwerp was simply outplayed by Rotterdam.
The gap between the two ports became wider every year.
It was ten years before action was finally taken. The Belgian govern-
ment gave financial support to the Antwerp port authority's Ten Year
Plan, which anticipated a doubling of the Antwerp port capacity. This
multi-million dollar project was finished in 1965. In the 1960s the
world fleet almost doubled in size from 172 million dwt (deadweight
tons) to 338 million dwt. During that same period seagoing trade grew
from 1080 million tons to 2480 million tons. Antwerp now started to
regain its position in the North Sea region and was now - at least as far
as port infrastructure was concerned - competitive again. While the gap
between Antwerp and Rotterdam did not increase further, Antwerp was
still quite vulnerable in some areas. Port expenses had been a constant
worry for both Antwerp and Rotterdam since World War I. Piloting
costs, port taxes and handling costs were constantly compared by ship-
ping companies trying to cut costs.16
The biggest port expense, which also influences freight rates to a
considerable extent, is the actual cost of handling. Both ports and
their employers' federations always kept their tariffs somewhat obscure
and were reluctant to publish official tariffs since that might encour-
age the competitor to take another look at his own tariffs in order to
present a better - that is cheaper - tariff to a potential client. It is and
was also common practice to give considerable reductions on the offi-
cial tariffs in order to entice shipping companies to come to a certain
port.17Handling costs - especially for labour-intensive cargo like general
cargo - are mostly determined by the cost of labour. This meant that
port authorities and port employers were extremely sensitive to a rise
in labour costs because this would normally have a negative effect on
handling and terminal costs and could harm the position of the port.

## Stephan Vanfraechem 149
Before World War II the port of Antwerp had little to fear from
Rotterdam as far as labour costs were concerned. Dockers' wages were
considerably lower in Antwerp than in Rotterdam, resulting in lower
handling tariffs. The Antwerp port promoted itself during the interwar
period as the ‘quickest and cheapest port of Europe'.18 The Rotterdam
port authority could not compete with this low cost of labour. Rotterdam
tried to make itself more competitive by offering reductions on official
port tariffs but these efforts could not compensate for the considerably
higher labour costs, compared with Antwerp.
The period 1944-48 was of enormous and decisive importance for
the way in which labour costs evolved in both ports. After liberation
both the Dutch and Belgian governments came up with a plan to keep
prices and wages under control in order not to create an unstoppable
spiral of wage rises leading to price increases. Both governments devel-
oped similar solutions: the free (that is, free from government interfer-
ence) initiative between trade unions and employers' organizations
was temporarily shelved - although this system would not be officially
abolished in the Netherlands until 1964 - and replaced by a central,
government-led incomes policy. The government would decide how
prices and wages evolved in order to prevent massive inflation.19
Although the medicine was the same, the patients reacted in different
ways. In the Netherlands the central incomes policy was very successful.
Several institutions saw to it that the different sectors and industries
did not exceed the margins that were set by the government. This led
to wage and price movement that was in almost perfect balance and
control. The Rotterdam port profited from this situation: although there
was some friction between trade unions over the control over the port,
generally speaking wages in the Rotterdam port increased in a moderate
way which had positive consequences for the handling costs.
In Belgium, on the other hand, wages and prices were at no point
under the control of the national government. The central incomes
policy was a disaster and had already been abandoned by 1946. After
only a few months the different sectors and industries started exceeding
the margins imposed by the government and institutions such as the
National Labour Council where government, trade union and employ-
ers sat around the table. The Antwerp port reflected this situation: after
liberation one wage increase followed another, which made Antwerp
the (much) more expensive adversary of the Rotterdam port. The com-
plete failure of the central incomes policy, combined with the presence
of a communist action committee which forced the ‘conventional' trade
unions to support excessive wage demands by their own rank and file,

150 The Evolution of Antwerp and Rotterdam
put Antwerp in 1948 - so only 4 years after liberation - in an extremely
uncompetitive position regarding labour costs.20 In 1948 the Antwerp
standard dockers' wages were more than twice as high as in Rotterdam.
Combined with the hopeless sight of the outdated port infrastructure,
this placed the Antwerp port until the mid-1950s in an extremely vul-
nerable position. From the mid-1950s onwards the conventional or offi-
cial trade unions got a grip on the communist nucleus in the port and
were now able to follow a more realistic course where wages and work-
ing conditions were concerned. In order to make the port more compet-
itive the trade unions now even supported the employers' federations
in their demand for more flexibility and especially more realism and
pragmatism. The wage gap between Antwerp and Rotterdam, however,
was only gradually to diminish: only after the central incomes policy
was completely abandoned in the Netherlands in 1964 did labour costs
in Antwerp and Rotterdam evolve at the same pace.
In the post-war period the port of Rotterdam and its employers also
took decisive steps towards a further decasualization of dock labour.21 As
shown above, the Rotterdam port and its employers mainly made use of
casual labour at the beginning of the twentieth century. Trade unions,
however, quickly began opposing the practice of hiring labourers on
a daily basis which left these casual dockers with almost no financial
security. As from the beginning of the twentieth century a new category
of dockers appeared. With agrarian roots and Orthodox-Protestant back-
ground these dockers showed ambitions to become respectable and dis-
ciplined workers. They showed little or no affinity with the stereotypical
docker who favoured the freedom and flexibility of choosing for himself
which employer he would work for that day. This new category of dock-
ers wanted a steady job with some formal rules and regulations. This
tendency was also seen among some enlightened employers who were
influenced by social issues and hoped to stabilize the social climate in
the port by introducing proper rules and better working conditions.
This combination had led to the introduction in 1918 of a Labour
Pool which guaranteed work (and income) to some 2,000 dockers.
Fluctuating with the business cycle this number could rise to 6,000. A
large proportion of the Rotterdam dockers however remained casual
labourers during the interwar period.
In the immediate post-war period (1945-46), the unemployed dockers
were active in the rebuilding of their port and were mobilized to restore
the cargo handling activities in the port. From 1947 on, however, there
was a shortage of casual labour. Dockers who used to work in the port
now found employment in construction or metallurgy. From 1959

## Stephan Vanfraechem 151
employers in the port of Rotterdam were confronted with a structural
shortage of casual labour. The port tried to attract commuters from
other parts of the country and in the 1960s a bonus was given to dock-
ers who could persuade workers to become dockworkers. In 1961 the
Rotterdam employers launched an offical sociological survey to find out
why they were not successful in attracting new labourers for the port.
The conclusion showed that dock work was perceived as a temporary,
dirty job and not as a job for the long term.22
Rotterdam now took a step forward by consciously choosing the path
of decasualization. All companies in the port raised the numbers of
regular workers at the expense of casual labourers. In 1965 the number
of regulars reached 80 per cent. The remaining casuals were all offered
a contract by the Labour Pool and were granted holiday benefits, health
insurance, formal rules of dismissal, and so on. No longer hired on
a daily basis, these casual workers were in practice hired for a consider-
able period of time and worked longer periods for the same employer.
In Antwerp quite the opposite happened and casual labour remained
the cornerstone of labour organization in the docks. A system of unem-
ployment insurance was introduced - the ‘Compensatiefonds voor
bestaanszekerheid' which guaranteed dockers 66 per cent of their nor-
mal income in case of involuntary unemployment. The majority of the
Antwerp dockers however remained casual labourers who were hired on
a daily basis. In 1964 a formal proposition by the employers to decasual-
ize and introduce a guaranteed weekly income was rejected, with most
dockers referring to the loss of independence and freedom.23 So unlike
their Rotterdam comrades the Antwerp dockers did not see casual dock
labour as an ‘improper' occupation.
Of boxes and men: how did Antwerp and Rotterdam
adapt to the introduction of containers?
Towards the end of the 1960s the Antwerp port had finally undergone
the necessary re-structuring. The official inauguration of the last docks
and lock in 1965 marked the end of the Ten Year Plan, doubling the
port's capacity with new docks and locks. With containerization in the
offing, however, this would not prove to be the correct or complete
remedy for the Antwerp port.
In 1966 both Antwerp and Rotterdam welcomed their first container
vessel.24 At the end of that year Antwerp had received 43,000 TEU while
Rotterdam did slightly better with 60,000 TEU. In the following years
however the Antwerp port was once again no match for the Dutch

152 The Evolution of Antwerp and Rotterdam
competitor. In 1971 the Rotterdam port was already handling 1 million
TEU annually. The Antwerp port would only reach that level in 1978.
In 1980 Rotterdam broke the barrier of 2 million TEU. Antwerp would
reach that level 13 years later. So after a somewhat comparable start in
1966, the Rotterdam port had once again outclassed its major competi-
tor. Why did Rotterdam once again adjust better to the challenges of
containerization? The fact is that the standardization of the cargo - with
the container as the symbol for this evolution - made speed the key
factor in the cargo handling business. The eventual loading and unload-
ing of the cargo had to be speeded up because of the capital-intensive
nature of container ships. Since container ships were three or four times
more expensive - both in building and in exploitation - than a regular
liner, the shipping companies wanted to further reduce dead time in the
ports. Where the sea:land ratio for the average liner was about 1:4, this
changed to 1:1 for the container ships. This quest for more speed could
not be met exclusively by an additional labour force.
Given the physical challenges of container handling, the only way to
really increase speed was clearly to invest in new technologies specific
to the container handling business. The shipping companies required
specialized terminals with specialized large capacity cranes. Antwerp
and Rotterdam responded in totally different ways to these market
demands. In Rotterdam the large stevedore companies, correctly inter-
preting the implications of those boxes, started investing in specialized
terminals constructed solely for container handling, encouraged by
the Rotterdam port authority.25 The result was that after a few years
Rotterdam was the undisputed leader in container handling in the
triangle Antwerp-Rotterdam-Hamburg, with Antwerp remaining in
second and sometimes third place. Antwerp responded much too late
to the challenges of containerization. In the 1960s the overall picture
of cargo handling in the port of Antwerp was remarkably similar to
the situation in the interwar years, with the clear division between ste-
vedores and naties continuing to affect the actual handling. Although
some firms had disappeared, there were still several dozen small cargo
handling companies. The cornerstone of the naties remained their
co-operative structure. This limited the investment options for too
long. Compared with their Rotterdam opponents, the Antwerp port
companies were simply unable to meet the financial challenges of con-
tainerization; with their co-operative structure, they could not generate
the necessary capital for large investments. The Antwerp port also had
no tradition of specialized terminals. As long as the port authority had
been responsible for the construction of the docks, warehouses and

## Stephan Vanfraechem 153
quays, it had been mainly a multifunctional general cargo part, so that
all kinds of general cargo could be handled in most of the installations.
After the port authority's withdrawal, the stevedores andnaties had fol-
lowed that same policy: the quays were installed for general cargo and
had to be multifunctional.
It took the Antwerp cargo handling companies too long to realize
that they needed only specialized cargo terminals if they wanted to
play an important role in container handling. More than just a change
of mentality was required, however. The cargo handling companies also
needed to change their organization and structures if they wanted to
cope successfully with the challenges of containerization. In the 1970s
and 1980s the Antwerp cargo handling companies abandoned their co-
operative structures and reorganized as normal enterprises with a sound
and transparent financial base and structure. A process of vertical and
horizontal integration started: naties offered stevedoring services and
vice versa. Fewer but larger companies invested in specialized terminals
from the early to mid-1980s.26 The real breakthrough of containers
and increased market share for the Antwerp port did not, however,
arrive until 1992. In that year the first specialized container terminal
upstream was opened. Previously all container terminals had been situ-
ated downstream of the locks, resulting in considerable loss of time for
the container vessels. In Rotterdam, however, container terminals had
long since been situated upstream.
Some conclusions
At the end of the nineteenth century both Antwerp and Rotterdam
were confronted with the need for a faster turnaround for the vessels
that docked in their ports. The higher running costs of the steam ves-
sel made speed more important for ship-owners. In both ports we have
seen that specialized stevedoring companies started offering their serv-
ices and made use of mainly casual labour, with dockers being hired on
a daily basis.
In Antwerp, however, we have seen that these stevedoring companies
worked separately from the traditionalnaties. Stevedores were in charge
of the actual loading and unloading, while thenaties took care of distri-
bution, transport and warehousing. In Antwerp the small, undercapital-
ized company remained dominant. In Rotterdam the traditionalvemen
started offering stevedoring services. By the end of the nineteenth
century, a process of vertical integration had already set in. This led to
bigger and fewer companies compared to Antwerp.

154 The Evolution of Antwerp and Rotterdam
Because of the limited role of the city council in the port's
superstructure, the Rotterdam companies were pushed into a more
pro-active role than their Antwerp colleagues. In Antwerp the com-
panies could rely on warehouses, cranes, etc. that were built and
financed by the city council. In Rotterdam the city council limited
itself to the building of the maritime infrastructure (docks, quays).
All superstructure needed was financed by the private companies.
This pushed the Rotterdam companies into technological innova-
tion in a bid to increase the return on their investments. In Antwerp
this factor was absent: the city paid for all superstructure, and the
financial risks, consequently the potential benefits, for the private
companies were limited. The Antwerp naties and stevedores seemed
happy with this. In Antwerp the city council and the private com-
panies opted for an ‘all-round' port. All facilities were designed for
the broadest possible types of cargo. In Rotterdam the city council
encouraged specialized terminals and facilities.
How has all this had an impact on labour organization? The choice of
a general cargo port combined with the limited financial involvement
in their port meant that an ‘all-round' labour organization with a large
pool of casual labourers seemed the best deal for the Antwerp employ-
ers. Supported by the fact that, apart from some labour shortages in
the 1960s, there was an abundant supply of casual labour and thus no
pressure to take steps towards decasualization, cheap casual labour and
a pool of all-round dockers remained the cornerstone of dock labour in
the port of Antwerp. The financial risks for the Antwerp employers were
limited: if they needed dockers, the supply was more than adequate. If
not, they had no financial obligations towards this large army of casual
labourers. Permanent labour costs for the Antwerp employers remained
limited. For the majority of small companies casual labour and leasing
superstructure was the only way of doing business. In Rotterdam, on the
contrary, from the end of the 1940s the supply of casual labourers will-
ing to work in the port dried up. The Rotterdam employers were forced
to rethink their labour organization and took decisive steps towards
decasualization from the 1950s. I believe that in the long run this
forward-looking approach definitely proved to be the best strategy. To
protect their own large investments but also to make labour organiza-
tion on their own terminals as tailor-made or specialized as they wanted
to, the Rotterdam employers fell back on their own core of permanent
labourers. Although further research needs to be done, I believe that this
company rather than port-wide approach (as was the case in Antwerp)
made the Rotterdam employers better prepared for the challenges of the

## Stephan Vanfraechem 155
future. Once it became apparent that the introduction of the container,
for instance, would fundamentally change cargo handling (techniques
but also the composition of the gangs),the Rotterdam employers were
faster to adapt. In Antwerp, for example, it took difficult negotiations
with a pool of several thousand casual dockers before there was any
movement towards a reduction of the gangs. For too long the Antwerp
port and its employers maintained a strategy of ‘one size fits all' with a
large pool of all-round dockers. The fact that the Rotterdam stevedores
were pushed towards a more substantial financial involvement in their
own superstructure and made definite choices of certain types of cargo
also made them more aware of the most appropriate labour organiza-
tion for their own terminal. In Antwerp, the employers had been think-
ing that they needed to do everything and therefore needed a pool of
labourers who were as all-round as possible. Choosing to maintain a
very large pool of casual labourers at community level (in 2011 there are
still 10,000 labourers in the pool, 5,000 of whom remain casual) made
it very difficult to adapt to market changes. In Rotterdam the choice of
permanent labourers made this easier.

## 10
Institutional Path Dependence
in Port Regulation: A Comparison
of New Zealand and Australia
James Reveley and Malcolm Tull
Introduction
The Australian and New Zealand port industries in the post-World
War II period exhibit strong elements of path dependence. Simply
put, economic and political actors' purposive decisions pushed the
development of port institutions down a pathway which became hard
to step off: institutional lock-ins impeded strategic flexibility and
the growth of port productivity; inefficient macro- and micro-level
institutional arrangements reduced Australian and New Zealand port
efficiency. Given this experience, our chapter uses path dependence
as a method to explain institutional stability and change within
New Zealand's and Australia's respective port systems. While port
institutions in both countries were slow to adapt to shifts in the wider
industry environment, they eventually succumbed to reformist change.
As we explain, this occurred more strongly in New Zealand through
pathbreaking institutional transformation in 1989, the pressures for
which mounted over several decades. In Australia, by contrast, there
were many false starts and change was slower even after the ports
were caught up in the federal government's late 1980s economy-wide
programme of microeconomic reform.
Applying path dependence to port history
Despite its cross-disciplinary appeal, the path dependence concept has
only recently been utilized by historians who study ports.1 In line with a
social science trend, moreover, they use path dependence not to explain
how technical progress occurs, but rather to investigate institutional
and organizational stability and change.2 This approach follows
158

160 Institutional Path Dependence in Port Regulation
The trigger effect of ‘backlash processes' is especially useful for analysing
port regulators' decision-making reversals.15
Regulating New Zealand's port labour markets16
Not infrequently, New Zealand's ports were chokepoints that slowed
the movement of the staple commodity exports on which its economic
development relied. Waterside workers repeatedly demonstrated their
capacity to hold up stevedoring work. Countrywide strikes shut ports
down in 1890 and 1913.17 A thorn in the side of governments of various
political stripes thereafter, port-level labour disputes impeded trade
flows.18 Spurred by a crisis moment in 1940, as the country prepared
for war, the government finally assumed control of labour hiring and
work at all major ports. By establishing legally constituted internal
labour markets (ILMs), the wartime scheme created a class of labour
market ‘insiders' no longer exposed to casual hiring, and who had
the exclusive right to perform port work. Instead of operating at the
firm level, the ILMs were organized at the port level around the legally
defined occupation of ‘waterside work'.19 Each port had its own ILM.
This scheme regularized port hiring and provided greater employment
and income security to waterside workers.20 Worker selection and
job allocation was centralized through the port-based labour bureaus
administered by a specialized government agency, the Waterfront
Control Commission.
Trying to prevent costly hold-ups at the country's ports, legislators
amended the institutional rules of New Zealand's port ILMs no fewer
than four times between 1946 and 1948.21 During this time the labour
bureau control agency, renamed the Waterfront Industry Commission
(WIC), maintained the port-based ILMs. Irritated by militant port
unionists, a conservative national government's leaders in 1950
endorsed the decision by port employers to confront the national
Waterside Workers' Union (WWU).22 In 1951, during a landmark 151-
day national dispute, the Nationals deregistered the WWU and replaced
it with twenty-six new port unions. Path dependence was initiated by
this government's answer to the logical next question: should port-level
ILMs and the WIC be abandoned in favour of a different labour market
arrangement, such as a return to firm-based hiring?
Entrenching self-reinforcing path dependence
Self-reinforcing path dependence in New Zealand port labour
market regulation stemmed from a well-known institutional lock-in

James Reveley and Malcolm Tull 161
seedbed: a ‘critical juncture' occurred during which ‘a particular
institutional arrangement' was chosen from two mutually exclusive
options.23 To keep or not to keep the WIC - that was the question.
Appointed in 1950 to consider alternatives, a Royal Commission of
Inquiry gave lawmakers the answer. When the Commissioners' findings
were finally tabled in parliament in 1952 the government accepted their
majority recommendation to retain the WIC, implemented through
the Waterfront Industry Act 1953.24 The die was cast: for nearly twenty
years increasing returns then reproduced this institution, rendering
the ILMs resistant to change even after containerization lessened their
tenability.
The returns to this port labour market institutional arrangement
increased throughout the 1950s as the fortunes of both the unions
and many firms became entwined with it. First and foremost, the
ILM institutions empowered the new port unions, which quickly
organized themselves into a North Island and a South Island Federation.
By providing job security and regular pay to registered waterside
workers and giving them a legal monopoly over stevedoring, the WIC
inadvertently supplied a stable base for union organizing. Principally,
it maintained an exclusive port-level register of legally licensed
waterside workers (known in local parlance as watersiders) available
to be allocated to shipping and stevedoring companies. All registered
watersiders were required by law to join their local port union, with
casual hiring permitted only in cases of extreme labour shortage. While
it was never the policymakers' intention, the WIC quickly became the
cornerstone of post-1951 union strength at the country's ports.25
Institutional lock-in also stemmed from the ILMs' advantages for
firms. Principally, firms were guaranteed a supply of skilled labour with
which to meet their fluctuating labour needs. A national levy spread
the cost of maintaining the stevedoring workforce across the industry;
firms contributed in direct proportion to hours worked by the registered
watersiders they drew from the Commission. Not having to employ a
permanent workforce sustained an array of independent stevedoring
firms operating on short-term contracts with shipping companies; they
held around 20 per cent market share until the late 1960s.26 But it was
not just the independents that benefited from this set-up. Given that
firms did not need to permanently hire a workforce, relying instead
on the WIC's labour supply, the use of ‘cost-plus' contracts meant
that there was money to be made from stevedoring. This contracting
system displaced labour costs elsewhere in the transport chain, as the
stevedoring operator simply charged the shipper a percentage on top of

162 Institutional Path Dependence in Port Regulation
the costs, labour included, incurred when handling cargo. Undoubtedly
it was the prospect of making a quick dollar that spurred the Union
Steam Ship Company - the largest coastal shipping firm - to engage
in contract stevedoring in the early 1960s.27 Major shipping lines with
stevedoring company subsidiaries, notably the British-owned New
Zealand Overseas (Conference) Lines, used an equivalent of cost-plus
contracting: they simply built into their freight rates a high margin for
anticipated stevedoring - especially labour - costs.28
Some of the collectivized labour pool's benefits accrued to all firms
engaged in stevedoring. In marked contrast to the opportunism-prone
spot contracting system of casual employment prevalent before the
war, the WIC's guaranteed labour supply lowered transaction costs by
decreasing uncertainty about the quality of labour inputs and worker
motivation. Although firms controlled neither hiring nor dismissal,
they elicited sustained work effort by tapping into elaborate informal
routines that evolved within this institutional environment to govern
bargaining on the job. Instead of invigilating waterside workers through
detailed supervision, monetary incentives substituted for expensive and
resistance-prone monitoring.29 While incentivizing watersiders lowered
labour contract negotiation and enforcement costs, it increased labour-
based transformation costs.30 Time series on the cost structure of New
Zealand stevedoring do not exist, but anecdotal evidence presented in
previous research suggests shippers perceived labour costs to be rising
throughout the 1960s due to cost-plus worker-placating arrangements
which, in turn, were causing freight rates to escalate.31
Clearly the WIC promoted interlinked and inefficient maximizing
behaviours by firms and unions alike. Over time these organizations
became tied into an institutional framework that impeded economic
efficiency; the additional costs flowing to shippers undoubtedly
lowered the rate of return to society of tying up resources in ports.
This arrangement was sustainable while New Zealand's national
income was rising due to export-led extensive economic growth,
supported by a protectionist import substitution policy with the
ability ‘to accommodate high labour costs'.32 The entrenched port ILM
institutions became less tenable, however, when this economic regime
faltered in the late 1960s.
Shifting to reactive path dependence
While the shift to reactive path dependence within the ports industry
did not occur overnight, the turning point is easily locatable in the
early 1970s. Britain's entry to the European Economic Community

employment. This meant that, even for container operations eminently
suited to being staffed by an organization-specific and specially trained
workforce, port employers were still forced to draw workers on a short-
term basis from the WIC's general labour pool. Further limiting industry
adjustment, a 1973 bipartite agreement meant that all redundancies
had to be voluntary. The WWF progressively tightened its labour
market control, limiting firms' use of casual labour to the point of
its elimination in 1980.37 When labour shortages arose, registered
watersiders were shuttled - not inexpensively - between ports, under a
system of short-term transfer. This transformed the port ILMs into one
nationwide, hermetically sealed and union-controlled ILM.

166 Institutional Path Dependence in Port Regulation
The government's port reform process quickly moved from policy
formulation to implementation (H). Stakeholder consultation was
expedited by the appointment of an official representative Ports
Industry Review Committee, whose members included two union
representatives. When the Committee's 1986 report presented a split
opinion about the WIC's future, the Business Roundtable responded
with its own report arguing that this body should simply be scrapped.42
The die was cast when, in 1988, the government's Treasury Department
advised that high shipping and port labour costs deprived the economy
of 1.5 per cent of GDP.43 The WIC was the lightning rod for this criticism.
While the retention of some type of ILM arrangement was possible
even if the WIC were abolished, policy debates over port reform were
truncated by tying the two together. In an effort to uncouple them and
retain collectivized labour within an ILM, the WWF made a - futile -
last-minute plea to the parliamentary select committee considering the
Waterfront Industry Reform Bill. The Roundtable's influence over the
policy agenda meant that, when the Bill became law in March 1989,
the government simply implemented its recommendation regarding
waterfront employment, namely to create ‘a permissive framework'
by ‘removal of the statutory bodies'.44 The WIC was abolished in
September 1989, ending a path dependent process that stretched back
nearly 40 years.
Institutional path dependence in Australia
As an island nation with a coastline of 36,735 km and widely separated
centres of population, Australia has always depended on shipping and
ports to handle its trade. Australia's 42 major ports include specialized
bulk ports and mixed cargo ports handling both bulk and container
cargoes. In 2009-10 total trade at Australian ports was about 922 million
tonnes, 94 per cent of which was accounted for by bulk cargoes. This
reflects Australia's longstanding comparative advantage as an exporter
of primary products to world markets. Australia has been a major
beneficiary of industrialization in Asia: in the 1960s Japan's ‘growth
miracle' led to a major increase in demand for mineral exports and saw
it replace the UK as Australia's major trading partner. Since the 1980s
double-digit growth of the Chinese economy has led to unprecedented
growth in demand for Australia's raw materials and energy supplies.45 In
2009 China replaced Japan as Australia's largest export market.46
Seaports are vital interfaces and must function efficiently if Australia is
to survive as a trading nation in an increasingly globalized and competitive

James Reveley and Malcolm Tull 167
world economy. However, as in New Zealand, ports have sometimes been
chokepoints rather than gateways for shipping and trade, especially for
high-value general cargoes. This is mainly because for much of its history
the Australian waterfront has been characterized by rigid institutions that
have been slow to change as the maritime industry has changed. The
primary institutional features are the monopoly power and job control
exercised by the waterfront unions; the concentrated market structure
and high level of vertical integration in the cargo handling industry; and
the management practices of public port authorities. These combined to
produce a classic pattern of self-reinforcing path dependence. Waterfront
crises - ranging from major strikes to cargo bottlenecks - have led to
episodic intervention by both state and federal governments but until the
1980s most governmental attempts to overcome institutional lock-in met
with only limited success.
Regulating the waterfront
Until the 1990s, when a limited number of ports were privatized, all of
Australia's major ports were directly controlled by state governments.
The federal government, however, has the constitutional power to
intervene due to its responsibility for interstate trade and commerce.
Historically its role has been limited to industrial relations (the
Commonwealth Court of Conciliation and Arbitration provided the
first award for the industry in 1914), defence, quarantine and national
security.
Historically, the Australian waterfront industry - like its counterparts
the world over - has been notoriously strike-prone.47 This was originally
due to the system of casual employment, where workers were hired only
when there was work to be done and the employers avoided as far as
possible the costs of maintaining an idle labour force. Irregular earn-
ings made life difficult for waterside workers and their families, fostered
militant unionism and encouraged restrictive practices designed to spin
out work. The Waterside Workers' Federation was established in 1902
and gradually became the main union on the Australian waterfront. In
1928 after a major strike the federal government introduced a licensing
system for waterfront labour which was designed to reduce the union's
monopoly of the labour supply. During the Second World War, despite
opposition from employers, who opposed any interference with their
right to control the employment of their workers, it set up a Stevedoring
Industry Commission to regulate the waterfront. In 1949 the Labor
government of Prime Minister Chifley reconstructed it as the Australian
Stevedoring Industry Board (ASIB). This body was given extensive

168 Institutional Path Dependence in Port Regulation
powers over stevedoring operations. Employers remained critical of its
operation. For instance, the Fremantle Port Authority complained that
it caved in to union demands ‘on the wishful thinking basis that it
would produce peace in the industry'.48 In 1956 a Liberal Country Party
government replaced the ASIB with the Australian Stevedoring Industry
Authority (ASIA) with reduced powers.
The introduction of attendance money and other benefits by the ASIB
and ASIA improved conditions but the icy climate of industrial relations
persisted. Both authorities tried to regulate the number of waterside
workers but this was difficult due to the competing demands of employ-
ers and workers. The agencies had the difficult task of simultaneously
dealing with the Waterside Workers' Federation, a militant Communist-
led union with a high degree of labour solidarity, and employers who
had an ‘anachronistic mind-set' created in the pre-war era of abundant
casual labour.49 A widespread cost-plus system of charging for stevedor-
ing services meant that rather than delay ships in port, shipowners
would grant concessions to unions and pass the cost on in the form of
higher freight rates.50 Strikingly similar to the New Zealand experience,
self-reinforcing path dependence helped produce institutional inertia
and low efficiency on the waterfront.
Technological change is a classic driver of institutional change but
had limited impact on Australia's capital city ports until after the
Second World War. In the 1940s the Americans, frustrated by waterfront
strikes and delays, introduced forklift trucks and pallets to Australian
ports to speed up cargo handling.51 The experience gained with
palletization helped pave the way for the more revolutionary technique
of containerization, which was introduced into international trade in
the late 1960s and spread rapidly.52 Containerization required fewer
but more highly skilled workers and to facilitate training permanent
employment was introduced in 1967. As in New Zealand, however, it
was accompanied by a complex and inefficient centralized system of
labour allocation. The cost of idle time was funded by an industry-wide
levy and there was little incentive for individual employers to minimize
the size of their workforces. As Figure 10.2 shows,53 since the late 1960s
there has been a dramatic and sustained decline in strike activity on
the waterfront but containerization did not bring all of the expected
improvements in labour productivity.54 Just like New Zealand, the rules
and procedures of the internal labour market continued to suppress the
pressures of the outside labour market.
By the 1970s both of the main players - the Waterside Workers'
Federation and the Association of Employers of Waterfront Labour - were

170 Institutional Path Dependence in Port Regulation
were 49 mostly small stevedoring companies operating in Australia; fifty
years later there were only seven companies providing general cargo
handling services.55 The high degree of concentration has contributed to
Australia's container ports being relatively inefficient by world standards.
In the mid-1970s, for example, container handling rates were well below
the 25 per hour then being achieved in ports such as Rotterdam, Tilbury,
Oakland and New York. However, a government inquiry found that the
low productivity of Australian ports was due a multiplicity of factors
including the state of the physical infrastructure and problems with the
workforce, including industrial disputes and restrictive work practices.56
It may be argued that the lack of competition weakens the incentives
for management to minimize labour costs; stevedoring and shipping
companies tolerate restrictive practices because they can readily pass
higher costs on to importers and exporters.
The effects of the concentrated market structure in container
operations has been exacerbated by the operation of ports and port
facilities, as well as regulatory activities untaken by customs and
quarantine authorities. Australian ports were originally modelled on
the British system of publicly owned harbour trusts as exemplified by
the Mersey Docks and Harbour Board of 1857. Beyond requiring ports
to break even, the acts of formation were usually vague about the
objectives to be pursued and gave considerable scope for managerial
discretion. Australian port authorities tended to be inward-looking,
prone to overstaffing and over-investment.57
Most capital city ports aimed to be ‘first class ports' and provide
facilities to service the needs of the world's largest ships. This often
led to building well in excess of demand which had implications for
port charges and budgets. The manager of the Fremantle Harbour Trust
summed up the prevailing view of port managers in 1934:
I feel we are over-efficient mechanically as compared with what we are
called upon to do. You may query the necessity for that condition of
affairs, because that must represent expenditure which is not earning.
The overplus of power is a splendid standby for the farming or
shipping community, in as much as if they were behind in deliveries
on one day, and it were necessary to hasten up on some following
day, the surplus of machinery would enable that to be done.58
The ‘overplus of power' may have been ‘a splendid standby', but at
times it was also an expensive one with major implications for port
charges and budgets.

James Reveley and Malcolm Tull 171
So far we have seen that institutional features of the industry
including (a) the monopoly power exercised by the Maritime Union
of Australia (MUA), (b) the development since the late 1960s of a
concentrated market structure, and (c) the management practices of
port authorities, combined to create a waterfront environment prone
to anti-competitive arrangements. The economic rents generated have
taken many forms but many were non-pecuniary, such as restrictive
work practices, overmanning, regulatory practices and investment
policies. A government report found that in the late 1980s the Australian
waterfront industry was still characterized by:
• ineffective management
• poor workforce training
• introspective industrial attitudes
• poor response to user needs
• poor information flows to users and between links in the transport
chain
• lack of supply and demand balance, often reflected in congestion
and queues or in under-utilization of expensive facilities
• abuse of monopoly power, reflected partially in port authority pric-
ing and investment policies
• a tendency for matters to improve while under investigation but to
lapse once the pressure is removed.59
In many respects the institutional lock-in and inefficiency on the
waterfront mirrored institutional rigidity in the wider economy; indeed,
as we shall see, the driving forces for change came from global forces
and the broader Australian economy, albeit augmented by new industry
leadership.
Shifting to reactive path dependence
In the early years of the twentieth century government policy
was dominated by the creation of ‘protective' strategies to help
preserve living standards: the White Australia Policy (1901); arbitration
(1904) and the basic wage (1907); tariff protection (the first clearly
protectionist tariff was the Lyne tariff of 1908); and the government-
owned Commonwealth Bank (1911).60 While in the first half of the
twentieth century these policies and institutions helped boost Australia's
population and economic progress, by the 1960s there were indications
that institutional rigidity was reducing the competitiveness of the
economy. In the 1970s economic slowdown and globalization forced

172 Institutional Path Dependence in Port Regulation
a change of outlook.With the economy on ‘the hinge of history',61 in
the 1980s the federal government introduced major microeconomic
reforms including tariff reductions, labour market reforms, deregulation
and privatization. It is generally agreed that these reforms of Australia's
economic institutions contributed to a surge in productivity growth
and laid the foundations for over two decades of sustained economic
growth.62
The federal government's drive for microeconomic reform was
designed to combat inefficiency and rigidity in the economy as a whole
but also triggered reactive path dependence on the waterfront. In 1989
employers, unions and the federal government reached an In-Principle
Agreement (IPA) on a three-year programme of waterfront reform. The
waterfront reforms included a change from industry-based to company-
specific employment, improvements in the efficiency of port authorities
and the creation of a more competitive commercial environment. The
size of the workforce was to be reduced by 3,000 voluntary redundancies
but 1,000 younger workers were to be recruited to improve the age
profile of the industry. Awards were to be restructured and the number
of unions reduced.
Port authorities were commercialized, corporatized, or (less commonly)
privatized.63 Both commercialization and corporatization involve
restructuring designed to introduce accountability and economic
efficiency into government commercial activities. The only capital city
ports to be privatized are Adelaide (2001) and Brisbane (2011). The
preferred Australian model remains one of public ownership, with ports
acting as strategic managers subject to statutory and governmental
oversight. Due to the reforms, which included extensive contracting out
of services, between 1993 and 1997 total employment at Australian port
authorities fell by about 52 per cent.64
A key objective of the reforms was to increase productivity on the
waterfront. However, measuring port productivity is a complex matter,
as it is affected by a large variety of factors, including port geography
and differences in the volumes and composition of shipping and
cargoes. Figure 10.3 illustrates a partial measure of productivity, the
average crane rate, for Australia's five container ports between June 1990
and June 2010.65 It shows that there were two major turning points, the
first in the early 1990s and the second in the early 2000s. The crane rate
increased from about 14 TEUs per hour in 1990 to about 20 TEUs per
hour in 1996, an increase of 43 per cent, demonstrating the success of
the first wave of reforms. A 1995 study by the Bureau of Transport and
Communications Economics (BTCE) concluded that ‘waterfront reform

James Reveley and Malcolm Tull 175
(the National Ports Council and the New Zealand Ports Authority) to
coordinate port planning but these were ineffective and eventually
abolished.70 The wheel has now come full circle: in 2010 the federal
government announced Australia's first National Ports Plan which
aims to improve coordination in planning and improve landside
access to congested metropolitan ports.71 Ports are required to produce
30-year plans identifying land required to provide adequate road and
rail access. This will require the cooperation of state governments,
which is not always forthcoming. It is too early to assess if it will have
any more success than the British and New Zealand attempts in the
last century.
Conclusion
The durability of inefficient port regulatory institutions in New Zealand
and Australia is only explicable by reference to path dependence.
Comparing the reform process in each country, clearly the institutional
change trigger points had different timings and effects. In New Zealand
containerization functioned as a trigger event, ushering in a process of
reactive path dependence that ultimately exposed waterside workers to
competition in the external labour market. Once initiated, institutional
change occurred in a lockstep manner. Stemming from a backlash effect,
the change was decisive, transformative and long-lasting. By contrast,
pressures for change in Australian ports were more diffuse, coalescing
only under the wider process of microeconomic reform. Unlike New
Zealand, where the roots of frame-breaking port labour market change
can be dated to containerization spreading across the country's ports
in the early 1970s, the Australian waterfront industry's shift to reactive
path dependence coincides with the economy-wide reforms of the
1980s. New Zealand's reactive path dependence culminated in an earlier
regulatory decision, to lock the WIC in place, being unambiguously
reversed. Arguably in Australia, however, port regulatory reform
was but the first step down a path that is still being travelled. It is
replete with obstacles. The economists' solution to problems of path
dependence and institutional rigidity is usually to recommend a ‘good
dose of competition', but in Australia widespread market failures and
political and social constraints limit the applicability of this approach.
Indeed, the history of the Australian waterfront demonstrates that even
government-initiated reactive path dependent change is not always
able to overcome the inertia of existing governance and institutional
arrangements.
