Adolf K. Y. Ng and Ka-chai Tam 181
(referred to as the ‘early Open Door Policy period'). We will first discuss
the impacts of this new policy on the ports of China and the policies
and mechanisms of the government in reforming port management
and governance. Then we will investigate the details of institutional,
financial and legal reforms within the Chinese port industry, followed
by an analysis of the constraints it faced.
The Open Door Policy and Chinese ports
Since adopting the Open Door Policy as its strategic development
direction in 1978, China has enjoyed decades of substantial economic
growth. By the 1990s, China had already become the second largest
recipient of foreign direct investment (FDI) after the United States.
Throughout the early Open Door Policy period, China persistently
achieved a highly impressive annual GDP growth rate.
Such achievements, however, were the outcomes of considerable
painstaking side effects of reforms. Indeed, during the early Open
Door Policy period, the government struggled to overcome a number
of challenges, notably: (1) to improve the rural economy; (2) to secure
infrastructure and basic industrial development; (3) to acquire the latest
innovations in science and technology; (4) to upgrade key industries;
(5) to improve education and training; and (6) to reform the CCP and its
institutional system, while continuing strictly to control political and
military power. Moreover, economic development could be sustained
only if the country was supported by good transport systems, well-built
infrastructures, technological and managerial innovations. Acting as
the interaction point between land and sea, ports were critical nodes
in the establishment of efficient multimodal supply chains, especially
since maritime transportation was (and still is) the most cost-effective
and popular way to move cargoes and raw materials around the world.3
Nevertheless, in China, construction and upgrading of transport infra-
structure often lagged behind economic growth, thus forming bottle-
necks along the supply chains.
Various authors investigated the problems of developing a good trans-
portation system in China,4 and identified several problems, including:
(1) inadequate physical infrastructure; (2) insufficient deep water ports;
(3) poor bureaucratic structures; (4) an imperfect legal framework,
especially for supporting international trade; (5) the lack of a healthy
competitive environment for the port and shipping industries; and (6)
uncoordinated, if not conflicting, central and local government respon-
sibilities in these industries. Given that these problems would seriously

182 China's Seaport Development
undermine economic and industrial development, it was clear that the
government had no alternative but to reform its existing governance
structure. Indeed, until the late 1970s, under central planning, there
were various deficiencies in the Chinese institutional system, includ-
ing overlapping and unproductive ministries and departments, lack-
ing accountability and transparency, and the lack of a creditable legal
framework.
Successful institutional reform was critical to achieve good port gov-
ernance and development. This meant that market incentives should
be allowed to operate in important economic sectors, with the govern-
ment being present in the form of regulation and macro-management.
With increasing FDI and contacts with the global market, China's port
industry became more complex, with intensified inter-port competition
in the international arena. Inefficient institutional structure was one
of the most problematic obstacles to further economic development.
Therefore, during the early Open Door Policy period, the government
carried out four phases of institutional reform (1982, 1988, 1993 and
1998) to improve the economic structure. The first phase took place
in 1982 after Deng Xiaoping's speech entitled ‘Institutional Reform
is a Revolution'. To achieve the ‘modernization of socialism' and to
complement economic development, the State Council abolished some
overlapping ministries or integrated departments with similar responsi-
bilities.5Moreover, in view of the increase in foreign trade, the Ministry
of Foreign Trade and Economic Cooperation was established through
the merger of several committees and ministries.
This phase succeeded in resolving some urgent economic problems.
However, it mainly focused on the rural economy. With increased FDI
and private sector participation, new problems related to centralized
decision-making and the merging of administration and operations
arose. This exposed the need for another phase of reform. The concept of
separation of government and enterprise was first proposed in the CCP's
On Reform of Economic Structures (1984). This idea was accepted during the
Thirteenth CCP Congress and the Seventh National People's Congress in
1987 and 1988 respectively. The objective was to create an institutional
structure which could complement demands for modernization, includ-
ing the reduction of bureaucratic practices and the strengthening of
departmental coordination within the institutional system under the
so-called ‘three definitions' (sandinggg).6 Under this scheme, a number
of ministries and departments were abolished or merged. At the same
time, some of the powers of the national government were devolved
to local authorities, leading to the gradual establishment of multi-level

Adolf K. Y. Ng and Ka-chai Tam 183
governance. Moreover, to fulfil the goal of separation of operation from
administration, the government also began state-owned and public
enterprise reforms. Policymakers aimed at strengthening the functions
of macro-coordination, information and regulation, while diluting the
government's role in management and governance in various economic
and industrial activities, including ports.
The third phase took place in 1993 after the Eighth National People's
Congress and served largely as a further endorsement of the 1988 reform.
It gave clearer indications of the roles of different levels of government,
the separation between administration and management, decentraliza-
tion and efficiency improvements. Finally, the fourth phase took place in
1998 after the Fifteenth CCP Congress, under circumstances quite differ-
ent from the last three. By then, the Open Door Policy had been in place
for two decades, with the basic form of ‘socialist market economy' already
established. Moreover, since the early 1990s there had been an influx of
FDI into China. Under this phase of reform, the number of ministries and
ministry-led (mostly industry-related) bodies was further reduced, from
40 to 29. Once again, there was emphasis on creating a smaller govern-
ment through decentralization, devolution and separation of administra-
tion and business management. Stressing improved coordination and
efficiency, the fourth phase also brought law enforcement in the govern-
ance of China to the fore, especially recognizing the effects of globaliza-
tion and considerable investment by multinational corporations.
In this respect, we argue that this fourth phase was a watershed for
the genuine separation of administration and management. For the first
time, the responsibilities of government and the private sector were
clearly defined in ways that could be generally understood. The govern-
ment's responsibility was to levy taxes on enterprise owners according
to their investments and profitability, to regulate the operation and
financial arrangements of enterprises, and to appoint major officials of
enterprises. The enterprises were responsible for the autonomy of man-
agement, profits or losses, the maintenance (or increase) of the value of
public assets, as well as defending the rights of enterprise owners. Such
clear-cut definitions aimed at accurately identifying the relationship
between the state and the market, allowing each to fulfil their appropri-
ate roles. Therefore it was clear that the government was attempting to
alter its ruling ideology from ‘multi-faceted government' to ‘govern-
ment with limitations'.7 This reformed system was also an answer to
the changing demands of new economic development. What then are
the implications of these reforms for the Chinese ports during the early
Open Door Policy period?

184 China's Seaport Development
Institutional reforms
Accountability was what was required of officials; they were responsible
for their actions and must explain to the public both reasons and antici-
pated consequences. China's institutional structure faced problems of
overlapping ministries, irresponsible behaviour by officials (in both
senior and junior ranks), poor efficiency and sweeping government
intervention. As a consequence, institutional reforms were urgently
required to allow China to achieve sustainable economic development.
This was especially important for the port industry, which was one of
the frontiers to the outside world.
Public sector management
With a coastline of more than 14,500 km, and nearly 30 ports with
international connections, China has substantial potential in develop-
ing maritime transportation, as can be seen through the long history of
overseas trade in traditional China. Nevertheless, modern port develop-
ment in China got off to a slow start. Although the container revolution
started in the 1960s, the first container did not appear in China until
1973,8 and it was not until 1978 that the first regular container service
between Shanghai and Sydney was launched by COSCO, the country's
flagship container shipping line.
Such backwardness started to change with the implementation of
the Open Door Policy. With intensified economic development, the
demands for shipping services in China boomed. The government
recognized that ports and their infrastructures were the basic tenet of
China's economic development, and thus ports were assigned priority
for further investments.
Article 7 of the Sino-Foreign Equity Joint Venture Law stated that
an equity joint venture might enjoy preferential treatment, notably
tax reduction and tax holidays.9 This law applied to the ports. The
crucial document revealing that port development was given priority
by the government was probably the Interim Regulations of the State
Council of the PRC on Preferential Treatment to Sino-Foreign Joint Ventures
on Harbour and Wharf Construction (hereinafter ‘Interim Regulations').10
Recognizing that modern and efficient ports were necessary for China's
‘socialist modernization' (Article 1), while understanding the fact that
port-related projects were often capital-intensive and time-consuming
with low returns (Article 2), joint ventures in port projects and opera-
tions were given preferential treatment in terms of time period and
financial arrangements. These efforts were reflected in subsequent

186 China's Seaport Development
in 2000.16 During the early Open Door Policy period, several ports flour-
ished and showed their potential to become major regional hubs.
The emergence of China completely altered the nature of inter-
national trade in Asia-Pacific.17 As Waters pointed out, however, the
transportation network continued to be one of the weakest links
within China's booming economy, as transport infrastructures were
unable to keep pace with increasing industrial production.18 Indeed,
during the 1980s and even the early 1990s, most containers from
China needed to be exported through Hong Kong. Chiang and Lau
identified four main problems of Chinese ports during the early Open
Door Policy period: (1) shortage of finance; (2) imbalance of trade; (3)
poorly supported transportation network; and (4) social impacts on
employment. Interestingly, these unfavourable conditions had actu-
ally created a somewhat optimistic perception of the potential of port
development, thus attracting considerable FDI in port operation and
management.19 For example, since the mid-1990s, the Shenzhen ports
of Yantian and Shekou have been operated by Hutchison Port Holdings
(HPH) and Modern Terminals Ltd. (MTL) respectively, two Hong Kong-
based multinational terminal operators. The operation rights at the
port of Dalian were given to Port of Singapore Authority Corporation
(PSA). Rapid development of these Chinese ports was the direct out-
come of the exploitation of professional management methods by
multinational firms. With the first call by Maersk (the largest container
shipping line at the time) in 1994, Yantian had broken Hong Kong's
monopoly on being the sole international container port serving PRD
and Southern China.20 Meanwhile, Shanghai, with more than five mil-
lion TEU in 2000, ranked sixth among the world's top container ports
(Table 11.2).
Port reform and development was given priority by the government.
Therefore during the Ninth Five-Year Plan period (1996-2000), there
was a wave of reform and development. The government invested more
than 40 billion RMB in port infrastructure, with an increase of 848,000
TEU container handling capacity. It also marked a tremendous boom
in the utilization of foreign investments in port development projects,
with a total of US$ 7.6 billion, 25 per cent more than during the Eighth
Five-Year Plan (1991-95). Moreover, there were significant differences
in port policies between these two periods. While the earlier focused on
physical construction, the later emphasized the ‘softer' aspects, includ-
ing overhauling and regulating the economic system, encouraging
healthy competition, enhancing macro-market control and separation
between administration and operation.

188 China's Seaport Development
government had mentioned full privatization, throughout this period
no such arrangements were observed in any Chinese ports.23 In fact,
most countries around the world maintain some form of public pres-
ence in ports, with nationalistic feeling also discouraging full privatiza-
tion.24In China, though the public sector's role was reduced, it was far
from being extinguished.
Public financial management
Finance shortage was a major barrier hindering Chinese port develop-
ment. The government recognized that its budget was inadequate to
finance ambitious port projects, implying that it was impossible to
dominate port affairs completely. Thus some financial concessions were
required.
Sources of port finance
During the early Open Door Policy period, the government still played
a significant role in financing port projects. For example, during the
Ninth Five-Year-Plan, the government invested US$ 6 billion in upgrad-
ing ports and waterways. At the same time, local government was
encouraged to raise funds through the levy of port construction fees.
Another source of income was international financial institutions.
For instance, in 2000, the World Bank had lent US$ 71 million to
China to develop ten container depots in the port of Tianjin. Finally,
further funds came from major domestic companies such as COSCO.
Nevertheless, port construction and improvements were usually gigan-
tic projects demanding considerable capital investment. Loans from
international organizations and domestic fund-raising were simply
insufficient for such ambitious plans.
With inadequate funds, it was necessary to seek help from FDI, which
was indeed critical for Chinese port development. In the early 1990s
investments had been secured from multinational firms through equity
joint ventures so as to build up capacity, while the proportion of gov-
ernment investment gradually declined. In the late 1990s China secured
about 60 per cent of the required funding for port development projects
from multinational firms. This evolution is exemplified by the construc-
tion of the Shanghai International Shipping Centre (SISC). Although
SISC was a strategic element in the government's policy, the authori-
ties made it clear that they would not bear any financial responsibility
for the construction terminals.25 In fact, during this period, with some
notable exceptions like Yantian and Shanghai, most port joint ventures

Adolf K. Y. Ng and Ka-chai Tam 189
did not issue share capital, utilize limited recourse project finance (like
debts), or sell infrastructure bonds, mainly due to the high financial risks
and the political perception that ports were regarded as strategic assets.
Concession fees and tariffs
At the beginning of the Open Door Policy, Chinese ports were operat-
ing under a self-sustaining system (yigangyanggan). Under this system,
a certain fixed amount of income was given by the port administration
to port enterprises to pay for port expenditure. In other words, enterprises
were paying ‘fees' rather than ‘taxes' to the government. Recognizing the
disadvantages of annual rental payment, as well as the need to comple-
ment Chinese economic development, the payment method for conces-
sion fees changed in the 1990s. After financial reforms, port enterprises
no longer needed to pay such fees, but were instead taxed on profits,
like other Chinese enterprises. Royalty charges replaced rent payments
in the Chinese port industry. Before the reforms, tariffs on port services
were under national control, jointly determined by the Ministry of
Communications (MOC), the Ministry of Finance and the State Price
Bureau. Since then, according to Article 8 of theInterim Regulations, it has
been up to the enterprises themselves to determine the prices.
Other financial concessions
Under the Interim Regulations, tax concessions were introduced to
encourage port investment. There were exemptions from custom duties
and other consolidated commercial-industrial taxes on raw material
imports, facilities, transport equipment and other devices used for termi-
nal construction. Moreover, tax exemptions were introduced for newly
established port joint ventures with 15 years or more of operation.
Legal reforms
With shrinking public and national planning and support, the govern-
ment could no longer govern solely through executive means. Indeed,
discussions to improve the Chinese legal system had been taking place
since the 1970s. However, the objective of legal reforms was largely
complementary to the direction of the country's economic develop-
ment. As Zheng pointed out, most of the laws and regulations passed
since 1978 mainly concerned economic development, commercial
activities and foreign investments.26
Being part of the economic development package, maritime trans-
portation also underwent legal reforms. As in other industries, purely

190 China's Seaport Development
executive means of dealing with port affairs were neither adequate nor
effective enough for real-world situations. Indeed, a legal document
dedicated to port operation and development, the Port Law, was not
promulgated until 1 January 2004. Before then, the main legal docu-
ment governing the port sector was theMaritime Code.
TheMaritime Code
China began to construct a legal framework for maritime policy after the
formation of the PRC in 1949. However, the legal structure of the mari-
time sector was not laid down until 1992 when the Open Door Policy
triggered such a need. As mentioned earlier, before 2004, there were
no laws specifically addressing the management of the port industry.
Previously, the Maritime Code, adopted in 1992, had served as the major
legal framework for China's maritime development (including ports).
Within the Code, there were two major port-related points. First, the
MOC was responsible for land (except rail) and maritime transporta-
tion, including maritime strategy and policymaking. For the manage-
ment of ports, MOC granted permits to foreign firms to engage in the
operation of Chinese ports, while the MOC was responsible for national
port planning, strategies and policies. Second, the Code introduced
exceptions in various areas. Before its adoption, no foreign firms were
allowed to engage in any activities within Chinese ports. After the adop-
tion of theMaritime Code, exceptions were granted in four situations: (1)
if there was a joint venture with a foreign partner; (2) if special technol-
ogy and skills not available among domestic companies were needed;
(3) if the principal base of the joint ventures was located in China; and
(4) if the registered capital contributed by the foreign partner(s) did not
exceed 49 per cent. Hence, several points that significantly contributed
to the evolution of Chinese port development during the early Open
Door Policy period were present. First, the Code laid the foundations
of China's strategy in port reform and development during this period.
Second, it endorsed the necessity of introducing foreign capital in port
development projects through the establishment of equity joint ven-
tures with domestic partners. Third, it enabled the transfer of new (usu-
ally high-tech) technologies and skills when such investments posed
significant benefits to the country's general economic development.
Other laws, regulations and guidelines
In addition to the Code, many other maritime-related regulations were
also approved and promulgated during this period. Partly to com-
plement maritime-related laws and regulations, other more general

Adolf K. Y. Ng and Ka-chai Tam 191
commercial laws were also drafted, approved and published. These
aimed to protect the economic rights of both foreign and domestic
investors. For example, Article 2 of the Sino-Foreign Equity Joint
Venture Law prohibited nationalization or requisitioning by the state of
any equity joint ventures under normal circumstances. Even under very
special circumstances where public interest required the requisitioning
of such joint ventures, the document required the state to strictly follow
legal procedures and compensate the affected investors. Under certain
circumstances, the State Council also issued ad hocc regulations and
guidelines facilitating port development whenever necessary. Due to
decentralization, local guidelines were also provided which gave advice
on how local port authorities should implement administrative duties.
All these laws, regulations and guidelines were efforts to standardize and
systematize the development of China's maritime industries, including
ports. In general, the definitions and statements in these documents
were reasonably clear and well-defined. The government clearly showed
its determination to create a market-friendly environment.
Decentralization
The participation of non-government interests was necessary to act as
watchdogs and to provide services which could not be effectively pro-
vided by the public sector. Within the Chinese port industry, the partici-
pation of the private sector was two-fold: first, the national government
decentralized power to local authorities; second, the private sector was
involved through investing terminals and facilities in these decentral-
ized ports. During the Mao era (1949-76), all Chinese ports were under
strict national control. Indeed, even until 2002, the maritime transport
sector was still directly guided by the State Council, together with the
Maritime Bureau under the MOC, which de facto was responsible for
almost every single maritime activity within ports, including human
resource management, financial planning, infrastructure building and
production. The relevant port authorities and local government played
only subordinate roles.
Despite such direct leadership, some changes had been made. The
centrally controlled system had exposed obvious disadvantages for
contemporary port development, including: (1) inconsistency between
port and city development; (2) lack of attention paid by local authori-
ties to port development; and (3) distance from the national authority,
based in Beijing and mainly staffed by people with little understanding
of port operations, often imposing inappropriate policies and strategic

Adolf K. Y. Ng and Ka-chai Tam 193
Port authorities in different cities or regions had different levels of
autonomy and strategic priorities. There were several reasons for this,
including history and government ranking, as well as the port and its
corresponding city's status and importance. With reference to Shanghai
Port Authority and Shenzhen Port Authority, these differences will be
analysed here. First, it was about history. Shanghai had been a major
port even before the establishment of the PRC in 1949, and thus unsur-
prisingly, the city had an established tradition. Shenzhen Port Authority,
by contrast, was created in 1994 after the establishment of Yantian
International Container Terminals (1992). Hence, the port authority was
new and did not experience the (rather painful) re-structuring process
of the public sector and state enterprises which took place in the 1980s.
Also, while there were a number of domestic port enterprises under
the Shanghai Port Authority (for example, Shanghai Port Container
Co. Ltd.), no similar enterprises were affiliated to the Shenzhen Port
Authority.30 The Shanghai Port Authority was therefore often far more
authoritative and insistent when dealing with the private sector.
The second factor affecting local authorities' power and planning
concerned the hierarchies within the Chinese institutional system.
Vertically, Shanghai was a municipal city, that is, a ‘provincial level'
authority. On the other hand, Shenzhen was a city under the Guangdong
Province, that is, a ‘city level' authority. Horizontally, the port author-
ity was a department under its respective government. Under such
an arrangement, the Shanghai Port Authority was de facto one grade
higher than its Shenzhen counterpart within the institutional system.
As illustrated in Figure 11.1, the Shanghai Port Authority and Shenzhen
Port Authorities were situated in Grades III and IV respectively within
the institutional system. Hence their interests could be channelled to
the government much more easily, a notable example being their par-
tial (but also critical) role in the government's decision to accept their
proposal to construct SISC, rather than other alternatives proposed by
nearby Zhejiang and Jiangsu Provinces.
The last factor was the status and importance of the port and its city.
Shanghai had been perceived as China's commercial city since the nine-
teenth century. After the adoption of the Open Door Policy, the govern-
ment still largely regarded Shanghai as the main gateway for foreign
investments. This gave Shanghai a rather special status, which in turn
generated self-esteem for the city and its citizens. Unsurprisingly, the
vision of Shanghai Port Authority, which aimed to restore its previous
role as a core player within the global shipping industry, was far more
ambitious than all other port authorities in China. On the other hand,

194 China's Seaport Development

Figure 11.1 Shanghai and Shenzhen port authorities under the Chinese
institutional system
although also an important port, Shenzhen was largely regarded simply
as an industrial base by the government. Hence towards the end of the
twentieth century, the vision of Shenzhen Port Authority was signifi-
cantly less ambitious, and more or less a ‘complementary' component
to the port of Hong Kong.
While Shanghai regarded itself as the future leader in global shipping
and logistics, Shenzhen tended to identify itself as a local player and a
complement to the port of Hong Kong. During this period, Shenzhen
tended to apply a complementary approach, while Shanghai seems to
have employed a more competitive approach towards its neighbours,
such as Ningbo.
Challenges and discussions
We have analysed how China attempted to reform its port govern-
ance and development during the early Open Door Policy period.
Nevertheless, the existence of a reform framework did not guarantee the
process and its outcomes. We argue that although port governance was

Adolf K. Y. Ng and Ka-chai Tam 195
recognized and the framework of meta-governance already established,
there were still substantial challenges. Indeed, with an improving finan-
cial situation, hardware deficiency was largely resolved through mass
infrastructure, technological improvements and education. But a more
problematic factor that prevented effective governance was the ‘softer'
aspects which could not be settled without painstaking efforts. As Jessop
has noted:
the state reserves to itself the right to open, close, juggle and
re-articulate governance not only in terms of particular functions but
also from the viewpoint of partisan and global political advantage...31
Throughout this period, it was not difficult to see that the government
was still suspicious of investment by multinational terminal operators.
Although ideological issues still existed, the Chinese authorities were
not unwilling to give up certain rights. Indeed, the government was
eager to reform port management systems, but its major problem was
the difficulty of defining the public sector's new responsibilities.32
With the lack of clear vision and allocation of responsibilities, the
Chinese authorities struggled to fulfil accountability in good port
governance during this period. Moreover, despite China being the
developing country which introduced the largest number of laws and
regulations during this period, the guidelines to local authorities did
not impose real constraints on their actions.As Zheng pointed out, the
Chinese legal system had struggled to transit from the ‘rule by law' to
the ‘rule of law' during the early Open Door Policy period.33 The gov-
ernment was often unwilling to improve existing laws and regulations
which further complicated the modes of conduct of investors. Even if
certain laws had been approved and promulgated, it was questionable
whether or not such laws would be comprehensively implemented due
to the country's fragmented legal structure. Possible government inter-
vention so as to protect ‘national' and/or ‘public' interests further com-
plicated matters. As Huchet and Richet argued, during this period, the
application of laws in China was far from being generally respected by
political and economic players.34 The lack of an effective framework to
promote transparency and predictability adversely affected confidence
and hence private participation in ports and other business.
Furthermore, ‘institutional inertia' hindered participation in the
port industry. This problem is evident from the example of the custom
clearance procedure. Until 2002, custom documents in China had to be
circulated among several sections within the government. For example,

196 China's Seaport Development
information provided by HPH indicated that in the port of Shantou, it
was normal practice for 70 per cent of the containers to be opened for
an inspection, usually lasting for four days, compared with the interna-
tional average of around 30 per cent and only two days. Furthermore,
during this period, the Chinese did not observe the international prac-
tice that containers using the ports for transhipment only would not be
examined. Moreover, the inspection system was mainly one of ‘random
selection', rather than a comprehensive review of different commodi-
ties, which wasted time and increased costs.35
In addition, the port, as a strategic asset, was especially vulnerable to
excessive government intervention. Indeed, one of the main difficul-
ties of doing business in China was excessive government interference
in strategies and operations. This was often driven by non-economic
motives and inhibited efforts to create a reliable and trustworthy legal
framework, therefore undermining effective promotion of good transpar-
ent and predictable port governance. Furthermore, the unwelcome hand
of government also inhibited the development of a healthy market envi-
ronment and hindered the participation of private investors in Chinese
ports. Before the 49 per cent ceiling for equity joint ventures was waived
in 2002 most Chinese port companies (usually directly affiliated to port
authorities) had a majority stake and their decisions were not always
made on grounds of economic benefits. As Dickens has argued, states
tend to be nervous about the impact of multinational corporations on
autonomy and stability within their national boundaries, as well as being
concerned about the leakage of tax revenues.36 Such fears were particu-
larly significant in China during the early Open Door Policy period.
A good example can be found in the Catalogue for the Guidance of
Industries for Foreign Investment.37 In it the government tried on the one
hand to create an attractive investment environment, while on the
other imposing restrictions on foreign investors. While foreign invest-
ment was encouraged in the construction and operation of port facili-
ties, in the same document the government also classified ports as an
area in which FDI should be restricted, and emphasized that Chinese
must act as the controlling or leading partner. Throughout this period,
the government continuously emphasized that reforms did not neces-
sarily need to be carried out in an entirely Western style.38 This was a
deep-rooted tradition dating back to the reforms of the late Qing period
in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries.
Nevertheless, since the turn of the century, it has become clear that
China has entered a new phase in port governance and development. In
2002, China was admitted to the World Trade Organization (WTO), thus

Adolf K. Y. Ng and Ka-chai Tam 197
opening a completely new era for the country's economic development,
as well as its economic relationship with the world. Soon afterwards, on
28 June 2003, the Government approved the Port Law (promulgated on
1 January 2004). These two events were the watersheds of Chinese port
development, as they finally ended the rather chaotic, non-legalized,
and very much executive methods that dominated Chinese port gov-
ernance and development during the early Open Door Policy period.
Despite the existence of various challenges, some of which are still rel-
evant, empirical facts indicate that the Chinese, in general, have been
successful in continuing their economic miracle since then. Ports have
flourished along the Chinese coastline; Shanghai and Shenzhen have
become the two busiest ports in China and the Asia-Pacific region, if
not the entire world.39 The successful experience of China in its earlier
stage of development provides a meaningful lesson for other developing
countries and emerging economies considering their own approaches
and the possible constraints of port development. The question of how
to strike a good balance between government intervention and market
initiatives is particularly important.

## 12
Private Companies, Culture and
Place in the Development of
Hull's Maritime Business Sector,
c.1860-1914
Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey
Skip Fischer has suggested that maritime historians often fail to situate
their studies ‘within the broader debates that animate discussion and
research in the larger [historical] profession'.1 While this may be true
of many branches of the history discipline - Fischer cites Atlantic and
gender history as examples - it is not so applicable to business history, a
vibrant field that has long had an important maritime dimension. This
is evident in the contributions of Francis Hyde, Peter Davies and Sheila
Marriner - the Liverpool School of maritime business history - to our
understanding of the role that companies play in the process of eco-
nomic development.2 It is also apparent in works relating to the develop-
ment and regulation of shipowning as a specialist activity in particular
localities during the early stages of industrialization in Britain.3 Building
on, and combining, these two perspectives on the business of shipping,
Gordon Boyce devised a ‘complementary yet alternative framework to
Alfred D. Chandler's transaction cost approach'.4 This alternative lay in
Boyce's focus on British shipping, which introduced a non-American,
service sector element to the literature on patterns and processes of
economic growth, which had hitherto been dominated by discussions
relating to manufacturing industries in the USA. Others have followed
in Boyce's wake, with the work of Gelina Harlaftis and John Theotokas
on family enterprise in the British and Greek shipping industries offer-
ing a notable European insight into the field as it has moved beyond
the Harvard School and Chandler's paradigm.5
This chapter seeks to enhance the maritime contribution to the
business history of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries.
Its parameters are at once narrower and broader than earlier contribu-
tions, for the scope of the study is limited to a single port - Hull, on
200

Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey 201
the east coast of England - while its coverage ranges beyond shipping
to embrace the shipbuilding and fishing industries of one of Britain's
most important maritime centres. Using a range of company records
and British Parliamentary Papers, the chapter examines and contextual-
izes the structure and development of the leading companies in Hull's
maritime business sector. There are three main parts in the analysis.
First, an assessment is made of the ways in which British business lead-
ers responded to legal developments in the mid-nineteenth century
which made it easier to establish joint-stock limited liability companies.
The second strand of the chapter relates to Hull's leading maritime busi-
nesses, all of which registered as companies, but changed very little in
organizational terms as they functioned, in effect, as private limited
companies. Third, consideration is afforded to the reasons underpinning
such structural continuity in Hull and the UK in general. Rather than
simply ‘failing' to implement more modernpractices, the adherence of
British business leaders to long-established modes of operation reflected
the social and locational characteristics of their particular business envi-
ronment. And culture, understood here ‘as a system of values and norms
that are shared among a group of people and that when taken together
constitute a design for living',6 was pivotal in this respect.
The private limited company: a ‘typical
British compromise'
The significance of a favourable legal framework for the development
of shipowning in Britain has been widely recognized. Building on
Ralph Davis's seminal analysis of early modern shipping,7 Simon Ville's
study of Henley and Son, and his broader appraisal of the growth of
specialization in shipping, contend that as shipping was governed by
Admiralty law it was afforded critical advantages in the late eighteenth
and nineteenth centuries when demand for sea transport rose sharply
due to the pressures of war and industrialization.8 Similar contentions are
advanced in Sarah Palmer's overview of British shipping between 1815 and
the repeal of the Navigation Laws in 1850, in Philip Cottrell's discussion
of steamship concerns in Liverpool during the mid-Victorian era, and in
Boyce's analysis of the development of large-scale enterprise in British ship-
ping from the 1860s to 1919.9 The consensus of these and other works is
that Admiralty law enabled investors to own shares in vessels as ‘tenants-
in-common', a system that facilitated shareholding by permitting indi-
viduals to sell their shares without recourse to their fellow shareholders and
without the need for a costly and time-consuming legal process to dissolve

202 Private Companies, Culture and Place in Hull's Maritime Business Sector
and reconstitute the shareholding group. Intrinsic to this legal frame-work,
moreover, was a degree of limited liability, for each ship constituted a self-
contained corporation for the duration of each voyage, and therefore if the
commonly owned property was lost at sea each tenant was liable only to
the extent of his or her initial investment. Such advantages, it is argued,
not only attracted capital to the shipping industry before the 1850s, but
also instilled confidence and network connections into the shipowning
community - factors that combined to stimulate the development of large-
scale shipping firms as metal hulls, steam engines and joint-stock limited
liability companies propelled the industry forwards from the 1850s.
In essence, the literature infers that shipping was blessed with many of
the benefits associated with joint stock limited liability before the forma-
tion of such corporate entities was made easier by reforms in company
law between 1825 and 1862. But this interpretation requires refining, for
a closer appraisal of the evidence indicates that shipowners were neither
especially advantaged before 1850, nor particularly inclined to run their
businesses as joint-stock limited liability companies after 1862. Indeed, in
the former period, the entities that owned and managed vessels were sim-
ilar in many respects to the proprietorships and partnerships that domi-
nated British business in general. Such concerns were characterized by a
strong correlation between ownership and control, with most proprietors
and partners taking an active role in the management of their respective
enterprises. Growth was bounded by the fact that firms were inextricably
linked to the personal assets of those involved, although their generally
limited scale was appropriate to the limited extent of the market in which
they were operating.10 The adoption of an organizational form in which
ownership and management were closely related offered a means of
reducing risk in the absence of limited liability. As Payne explains:
By the unification of ownership and control, the entrepreneur was
able to reduce the real or imagined dangers inherent in trusting his
business - or even part of his business - to a manager, when the
growing size of his firm or his own increasing infirmities necessitated
some delegation of authority. Instead, the more capable and consci-
entious employee who had risen to a position of some managerial
authority within the concern would be offered a partnership, thus
ensuring that his own fortunes were intimately bound up with the
more wealthy, if not the original, partners.
This highlights the ‘inherent adaptability' that these organizational
forms offered British business in general. In other words, it was not

Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey 203
just in the shipping industry that the partnership could be altered with
relative ease in response to changing conditions, such as the loss of a
partner through defection, retirement, infirmity or death.11
Whereas in the early stages of industrialization the scope for company
growth was limited, the broad extension of markets after 1850 encour-
aged business expansion.12 This was accompanied by changes in the
law relating to company formation. The Bubble Act of 1720 had made
the formation of a joint-stock limited liability company prohibitively
time-consuming and expensive for most business enterprises. After
1720, according to John F. Wilson, ‘it would cost at least £600 in legal
expenses to form a joint stock company ... and (even in the early nine-
teenth century) as average fixed investment in industry would probably
not have exceeded a few thousand pounds few were willing to pay so
much for the privilege of incorporation'. Although the Act was repealed
in 1825, this ‘was actually only a nominal breakthrough in extending
the availability of joint stock company status, because firms still required
an Act of Parliament before limited liability could be granted'. However,
increased pressure for further relaxation of the laws governing com-
pany formation - especially from the banking and utility industries -
led to the enactment of increasingly permissive legislation. The 1844
Joint Stock Companies Act enabled firms to incorporate through a
relatively simple registration procedure, and limited liability became
widely accessible following the 1856 Limited Liability Act. This process
of reform culminated in the 1862 Consolidating Act,13 which unleashed
‘a veritable flood of applications' that witnessed 24,002 companies reg-
istered in the United Kingdom between the passage of the Act and 31
December 1885.14 As the company differed from the proprietorship and
partnership in that its equity could be sold to professional shareholders
with no managerial role, it is reasonable to expect that the rise in com-
pany formation would foster the separation of ownership and control.
But this did not occur to any significant degree. Rather, as Peter Payne
observes, ‘in contrast with the expectations of the statesmen respon-
sible for the early company acts, there developed a private company'.15
Distinguishing a private limited company from a public concern was
difficult before 1907, when the former attained legal recognition as a
company in which ‘all equity was retained by those who signed the arti-
cles of association registered with the Board of Trade'.16 This difficulty
was noted by an 1895 Departmental Committee:
An opinion has been expressed by many members of the Committee
in favour of dividing joint stock companies into two classes,

204 Private Companies, Culture and Place in Hull's Maritime Business Sector
consisting of so-called "private" companies and public companies.
Undoubtedly it would be convenient for the purpose of legislation
if such a division could be made. But the difficulty is to draw the
line and to state any satisfactory definition of a so-called "private"
company. Clearly the mere fact of a company not inviting public
subscription does not supply any such differential.17
In seeking to enumerate private limited companies, the Registrar of Joint
Stock Companies estimated that this organizational form accounted for
415 companies, or approximately one-third of the enterprises that regis-
tered during the first six months of 1890. As Shannon asserts, however,
the proportion was undoubtedly much higher, for the Registrar applied
a narrow definition that was ‘almost equivalent to "family business"'.
Shannon advocates a much broader interpretation, in which - as in the
case of the partnership - personal acquaintance between shareholders
may be presumed on the basis of shared traits such as location, occupa-
tion and religious affiliation.18 By such a reckoning, the private limited
company constituted approximately three-quarters of the 63,000 regis-
tered companies in existence by 1914.19
The existence of such a high proportion of firms in which there was
little differentiation between management and ownership indicates
that the structure of British companies had remained largely unchanged
despite the legal reforms of the mid-nineteenth century.20 In essence,
this constancy represented ‘a typical British compromise',21 with busi-
ness leaders simply using the law to gain the benefits of limited liability
without actually altering the organizational form of their concerns. The
experience of Hull's maritime business sector offers a substantial body
of evidence to support this assertion.
Hull's private business mentalité
Hull was a major, multi-faceted maritime centre in the early twentieth
century. As contemporary Hullenesians were keen to point out, theirs
was the UK's third largest port in terms of the volume of cargoes han-
dled by its nine docks. This ‘gateway to the great manufacturing centres
of England' was home to a shipping interest that had turned to steam
power and metal hulls much earlier and more comprehensively than
its counterparts in other major ports, and in 1913 it ranked as the UK's
ninth largest shipowning port.22 Although Hull was not a naval base,
it possessed a shipbuilding industry and was unique among Britain's
principal ports in that it boasted a substantial interest in the fisheries.

Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey 205
In institutional terms, Hull's maritime interests were governed by
various organizational forms. While shipping was dominated by private
limited companies, public companies were seemingly prevalent in the
shipbuilding and fishing industries. In reality, however, organizational
structures do not always assume the form implied by their legal status,
as the covert proliferation of the private limited company demon-
strated. Accordingly, in Britain's third port, the spirit of the ‘typical
British compromise' imbued the maritime business sector to such an
extent that thementalitéé of the private firm was widely prevalent in the
1860-1914 period.
In line with national trends, growth in company size was evident
in Hull's shipping, shipbuilding and fishing industries from the mid-
nineteenth century. With regard to shipping, steam tonnage registered
at Hull increased from 138,684 to 168,474 tons between 1878 and
1913, whereas the number of steamship operators fell from 72 to 19
over the same period. In 1878, moreover, the three leading operators
owned 82,113 tons, 59.2 per cent of the total, as against equivalent fig-
ures of 151,755 tons and 90.2 per cent in 1913.23 This largely reflected
the development of a single private company, Thos. Wilson Sons &
Co. (TWSC). In 1878, when it owned some 50,000 tons of shipping,
approximately one-quarter of Hull's registered tonnage and over one-
third of its steam tonnage, this was Hull's principal shipowning firm by
a large margin.24By 1913, TWSC's local pre-eminence was more marked,
as its fleet of 86 steamers, aggregating 116,011 tons, comprised almost
70 per cent of the steam tonnage registered at Hull. At this point, the
second-ranking firm of W.H. Cockerline & Co. owned 12 vessels total-
ling 27,022 tons, while the Deddington Steamship Company Ltd came
a distant third with a fleet of four vessels measuring 8,722 tons.25
TWSC was unequivocally a family-owned and -managed private lim-
ited company. The business dated back to 1822, when Thomas Wilson
entered into a partnership with John Beckinton with the aim of import-
ing and distributing Swedish iron. In keeping with the ‘kaleidoscopic
nature' of the partnership form,26 the concern underwent numerous
changes in personnel and purpose. It moved into shipowning in 1831,
and ten years later Thomas Wilson established a new partnership under
the name of Thomas Wilson Son & Company. As the senior partner, he
possessed a two-thirds share in the concern, with David Wilson, his eld-
est son, owning one-third. Thomas's two youngest sons, Charles Henry
Wilson and Arthur Wilson, entered the partnership in 1850 and assumed
full managerial control when Thomas died in 1869, a year after David
had left the firm. When their partnership was registered as a company

206 Private Companies, Culture and Place in Hull's Maritime Business Sector
in 1891, the entire authorized capital of £2,000,000, comprising 20,000
shares of £100 denomination, was distributed among seven members of
the Wilson family, with Charles Henry serving as Chairman and Arthur
as Deputy Chairman. Arthur became Chairman on his brother's death
in 1907, and when he died in 1909 he was succeeded by his son, E.
Kenneth Wilson, with Charles Henry Wellesley Wilson (Charles Henry's
son) assuming the role of Deputy Chairman. Theother members of the
Board were Clive H.A. Wilson (Arthur's brother), the Hon. Guy Greville
Wilson MP and Oswald Sanderson. The latter - who had joined the firm
as Manager in 1901, was elevated to the Board in 1902 and appointed
Managing Director in 1905 - was related to the family by marriage.27
The Wilsons had interests in Hull's steam shipbuilding industry, not
least as significant purchasers of the products of local shipyards.28 This
industry was dominated by three firms in the mid-nineteenth century -
Martin Samuelson & Co., Humphreys and Pearson's, and Charles and
William Earle. While the former two concerns ceased operating in
the late 1870s, the business started in 1854 by local brothers Charles
and William Earle - registered as Earle's Shipbuilding and Engineering
Company Ltd [Earles] in 1871 - continued trading until the 1930s.
As a local trade publication observed, ‘[v]arious other firms might be
enumerated as having been largely engaged in shipbuilding in Hull,
but Earle's Company is now the chief representative of the business'.29
Its output stood at 36,125 tons in 1913, a figure that far exceeded that
of other shipbuilders in the locality, with Cochrane & Sons producing
9,985 tons, and the third-ranking concern of Cook, Welton & Gemmell
building a total of 8,459 tons. Earles also dominated marine engineer-
ing, producing a total of 27,980 ihp (indicated horsepower) in 1913,
whereas Amos & Smith Ltd and C.D. Holmes & Co. Ltd - the port's
other principal marine engineers - constructed a combined total of just
23,557 ihp.30 As the change in its name implied, Earles was transformed
from a partnership into a company in 1871, a development that her-
alded fundamental alterations in its ownership and management. The
concern's authorized capital of £300,000 was divided into 6,000 shares
valued at £50 each, with less than ten per cent held by Hull residents,
and none of the five Board members having an obvious connection
with the city. As Joyce Bellamy states: ‘from being owned and managed
completely by local men, the business was … transferred to persons who
had previously had little connection, if any, with the port and only a
small proportion of shares were locally held'.31
The voluntary liquidation of Earles in 1900 was caused by a toxic
mix of operational and financial factors. But many contemporaries

Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey 207
attributed the failure to poor management, with Syren and Shipping
reporting that ‘all that is needed to make shipbuilding as great a suc-
cess at Hull as it has been a failure in the past, is that the undertaking
shall be controlled on sound business like principles'.32 Given such
sentiments, many in Hull would have greeted the reconstitution of
Earles in 1901 as a family-owned and -managed business as a positive
development, especially as this move, which took the business back
to its organizational roots, was arranged by Charles Henry Wilson of
TWSC. Earles was established with a capital of £150,000 divided into
15,000 shares of £10 denomination, 10,000 of which were taken up in
the first issue. Charles Henry Wilson, with 9,747 shares, was the leading
shareholder by an overwhelming margin, while his sons, Charles Henry
Wellesley and Guy Greville, held 50 shares apiece, and his wife, Florence
Jane Helen, owned a single share.All 15,000 shares had been allocated
by 1912, with ownership largely the preserve of the Wilson family.
The 14,697 shares held by Charles Henry were now in the hands of his
executors - Guy Greville and the Earl of Chesterfield - while a further
101 shares were owned by Charles Henry Wellesley, Guy Greville and
Florence Jane Helen. The Board was also dominated by the Wilsons and
their close associates. In 1912, it comprised Charles Henry Wellesley,
Guy Greville, Oswald Sanderson, the Earl of Chesterfield and Harold
James Selbourne Woodhouse. Sanderson was close to the family, while
the Earl of Chesterfield (Edwyn Scudamore-Stanhope) was married to
Enid Edith Wilson, the fourth child of Charles Henry. The Woodhouse
family had been involved in Earles since the Wilson takeover in 1901,
Harold James Selbourne's father having been the agent of Florence Jane
Helen Wilson in respect of her stake in Earles, as well as a shareholder
in his own right.33
Hull's trawl fishery expanded in two broad phases during the period
1850-1914, with sailing smacks superseded by steam trawlers in a
remarkably swift transition during the late 1880s and 1890s. It has
been argued that this technological transformation was facilitated by
a significant organizational development, with the proprietorships and
partnerships of the smack era giving way to joint-stock limited liability
companies. Such concerns, according to this line of reasoning, were
established to secure from beyond the local fishery the additional capi-
tal and managerial talent that was necessary to resource the adoption of
steam engines, steel hulls and a range of ancillary trades.34 But a closer
examination of the business records of Hull's three largest steam trawl
fishing companies - Hull Steam Fishing and Ice Company Ltd [Hull
SFIC], Hellyers Steam Fishing Company Ltd [Hellyers SFC]35 and Great

208 Private Companies, Culture and Place in Hull's Maritime Business Sector
Northern Steamship Fishing Company Ltd [Great Northern] - reveals a
marked degree of organizational continuity, with each firm exhibiting
many of the characteristics of the private limited company.36
While neither Hull SFIC nor Great Northern - both first registered in
1880 - were private limited companies in a strict sense, their sharehold-
ers were largely drawn from Hull's fisheries. In Great Northern's initial
share issue, for example, no less than 27 of the first 30 shareholders,
who together owned 86 shares, 67.2 per cent of the total, were described
as Hull smackowners. The management of the firm, moreover, was
entirely in the hands of local fishing people, for all twelve members
of the Board were Hull smackowners with shares in the company.37
Family ownership and management was a further significant feature
of Great Northern, with Robert Hellyer and his sons, Charles and
John William - who had previously built up a large fleet of smacks at
Hull - being key figures in the new company. Robert was the leading
shareholder, with 38 shares, while Charles owned 10 shares and five
belonged to John William. Accordingly, just over 40 per cent of Great
Northern was owned by the three Hellyers, who were all directors of the
company.38 The family's involvement as owners and managers had con-
tracted significantly by 1913. Indeed, the ownership of the company
had broadened to include shareholders from as far away as Edinburgh,
London and Torquay. Hull remained the primary source of investment,
however, with 12,190 shares, 45.1 per cent of those issued, owned by
city residents, while a further 22.3 per cent belonged to investors in
nearby Cottingham, Beverley and Hessle. Ownership and management
were closely related, with all ten Board members possessing shares, and
there was some continuity in that three of the Directors had served the
company since its foundation in 1880.39
The development of Hull SFIC followed a similar path, with capital,
initiative and managerial expertise drawn from Hull's smack fishery
being instrumental in its foundation in 1880. But the involvement of
the Wilson family - owners of TWSC - was a striking feature of Hull
SFIC. This interest dated from 1890, when the nominal capital of the
company was raised from £30,000 to £200,000. The combined share-
holding of the Wilsons amounted to 6,180 shares, with Charles Henry
Wilson possessing 4,680, Arthur Wilson owning 1,000 and 500 belong-
ing to Florence Jane Helen Wilson, Charles Henry's wife. In sum, the
Wilsons controlled over 30 per cent of the firm's capital. Not surpris-
ingly, both Charles Henry and Arthur sat on the Board of Directors.40
Although no detailed shareholder lists have survived for the 1899-1913
period, it is evident that the Wilsons remained influential figures in

Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey 209
Hull SFIC well into the twentieth century. While a list of company
directors and managers reveals that in 1905 the nine-strong Board
included Arthur, Charles Henry and Charles Henry's son, Charles Henry
Wellesley Wilson,41 the inventory of Arthur's estate indicates that he
owned 900 shares, worth £4,500, in Hull SFIC on his death in October
1909.42
Hellyers SFC was more of a private limited company than Hull SFIC
and Great Northern. While the concern was not completely family-
owned and -managed down to 1913, the founder, Charles Hellyer (son
of Robert), and his heirs dominated it to such an extent that it was
essentially a ‘family firm'. The company was formed with an author-
ized capital of £41,000 divided into 4,100 shares of £10 denomination,
with Charles holding 3,208 shares, some 78.7 percent of the total. A
further 67 shares were owned by his wife, Jane Elizabeth, while two of
their daughters, Henrietta Ann and Jane Wheaton, were allotted one
share apiece.43 The company's authorized capital had been increased
to £160,000 by 1912, with the proportion of shares owned by Charles
falling to 28.2 per cent. However, this was accompanied by an increase
in the shareholdings of other family members, their combined stake ris-
ing from 50.3 to 63.9 per cent between 1897 and 1912. In keeping with
a family enterprise, the roles of Charles's eldest sons, Frank Orlando
Hellyer and Owen Stooks Hellyer, expanded over time. Whereas Frank
Orlando and Owen Stooks had respectively owned just 101 and 100
shares in Hellyers SFC in 1897, by 1912 the former, with 1,423 shares,
had become the second largest shareholder (after his father), and the
latter ranked third with 1,421 shares. At the managerial level, there
were four directors - Charles Hellyer, Charles Hodder, Benjamin West
and Walter Wilson - in 1891. This had increased to six by 1912. While
managerial continuity was evident in that all except Hodder remained
as directors, family dominance of Hellyers SFC was assured by the pres-
ence on the Board of Frank Orlando, Owen Stooks and their brother,
Sydney Hanniford Hellyer.44
With Hull's trawl fishery, like its shipping industry, essentially run
by private owner-managers, only one major firm in the port's sub-
stantial maritime business sector, the shipbuilding concern of Earles,
adopted a ‘modern' form of enterprise before 1914. But even this
did not last, for Earles traded as a private limited company - family-
owned and managed - from 1901 onwards. Such structures were com-
mon in Victorian and Edwardian Britain, whereas in Germany, the
USA and elsewhere there was a pronounced move towards corporate
forms marked by ‘a complete divorce between control and ownership,

210 Private Companies, Culture and Place in Hull's Maritime Business Sector
whereby … management is entirely undertaken by professionals, while
the bulk of the equity is held by investors who take no role in running the
business'. Noting how British business leaders ‘remained firmly attached
to the traditions of their predecessors',45 a significant corpus of scholarly
work has identified the predominance of family-owned and -managed
firms as a retarding influence on the British economy. This negative
verdict has been challenged by numerous scholars, not least because it
takes little account of the common values, beliefs and practices - the
culture - that shapes business activity in particular localities.
Business behaviour and culture in an ‘isolate city'
It has been alleged that Britain's family firms engaged in non-expansion-
ary strategies so that founding families could maintain control over their
enterprises. Chandler, in particular, criticizes British business leaders for
perceiving companies ‘in personal rather than organizational terms, as
family estates to be nurtured and passed on to heirs'.46 This, it is centered,
encouraged founders to remain in power for longer than they were able
to perform satisfactorily, and to bequeath the running of firms to heirs
who lacked the necessary competencies for such a role. But the notion
advanced by Chandler that British business leaders ‘failed' by not follow-
ing the American example of ‘managerial capitalism' has been challenged
on various grounds. Roy Church, for instance, highlights the fact that
family firms deployed contrasting strategies in different countries, while
others argue that family firms and non-family firms engaged in similar
behaviours.47 So, as Wilson concludes, ‘one cannot say that the family
firm per see was a problem'.48 This highlights the limitations inherent in
what Philip Scranton describes as ‘organizational determinism', with
Chandler's focus on the internal workings of the firm yielding ‘a field of
vision so constrained that little other than the desired objects and proc-
esses can be seen'.49 In other words, insufficient attention is afforded to
the impact that factors external to the firm had upon business behaviour.
Locating business behaviour within the environment in which it oper-
ates has therefore emerged as a key research theme. This is especially true
with regard to the cultural context. As Jonathon Brown and Mary B. Rose
assert: ‘[e]asily the most important influence on [business] behaviour is
shown to be "culture", as reflected in the value systems in society, locali-
ties, religious groupings, or firms'.50
Hull's experience emphasizes the weakness of analyses that err
towards ‘organizational determinism'. Strategies routinely associated
with family firms - especially those held to have impacted negatively on

Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey 211
growth - are evident in the development of TWSC. Succession is a case
in point, as Thomas Wilson - according to his sons and heirs - remained
at the helm of TWSC for too long, while 40 years later the progeny of
Charles Henry and Arthur Wilson took over the management of TWSC
(and Earle's) despite doubts about their ability and aptitude for such
positions.51 There is also evidence to support the contention that fam-
ily firms were vulnerable to ‘the extraction of excessive resources from
a firm to support family lifestyles'.52 When, for instance, the nominal
capital of TWSC was halved from £2 million to £1 million in 1895,
£500,000 of this was transferred to the depreciation fund, with the
remainder used to create debentures for the family at an interest rate of
5 per cent per annum, which guaranteed a yearly income of £25,000.53
But there are also many examples of non-family firms engaging in
behaviours that are usually associated with family businesses.
When Earle's operated as a public company, for instance, the pay-
ment of dividends to shareholders often took priority over investment
in the firm, even when profits were low. Accordingly, in 1883, the
net profit amounted to £9,581 2s 4d, a return that was described as ‘a
subject of grievous disappointment' by the Board, which nevertheless
distributed a dividend of 4 per cent to shareholders.54 The Hull trawl
fishery, moreover, serves as an example of personal ties forming the
ownership base of non-family firms. The establishment of Hull SFIC
and Great Northern entailed steps being taken to ensure that each
firm remained in the hands of trusted associates, albeit those engaged
in the trawl fishery and related occupations rather than family mem-
bers. Great Northern was especially restrictive in its stricture that ‘no
person other than the owner of the whole of a fishing vessel shall be
eligible to become a Member of this Company'. Further, it was stated
that ‘persons who are not smackowners, upon whom a share or shares
may devolve, by reason of death of a Member may hold such share or
shares during a period not exceeding 12 months, nevertheless such
persons shall not be entitled to participate in the dividend declared by
the Company'.55
The relationship between business behaviour and culture was deemed
to have had a negative influence on economic performance in Britain,
according to a number of studies published or reprinted in the 1980s.56
As William D. Rubinstein puts it, the ‘cultural critique' embodied in
these works maintains that ‘British culture in its various manifesta-
tions was (and is) anti-industrialist and anti-business', leading to ‘the
culture absorption of the middle classes into a quasi-aristocratic elite,
which nurtured both the rustic and nostalgic myth of an "English way

212 Private Companies, Culture and Place in Hull's Maritime Business Sector
of life" and the transfer of interest and energy away from the creation
of wealth'.57 Evidence can be drawn from Hull's maritime sector to sup-
port this perspective, with the Wilson brothers being the most obvious
examples of the alleged desire of British business leaders to distance
themselves from vulgarities of trade and industry. While Arthur had
a mansion, Tranby Croft, built on the rural fringes of Hull in 1873,
Charles Henry seemingly opted for ‘a rural, preferably historic home'
when in 1878 he acquired Warter Priory, a 400-acre estate near York that
dated from the late seventeenth century. There are also indications that
the two men indulged in ‘the more aristocratic interests of cultivated
style, the pursuits of leisure, and political service'.58
Such evidence notwithstanding, the theory that British business
leaders were afflicted by national cultural traits that seduced them
away from business matters is highly contentious.59 Perhaps the most
obvious flaw in the notion that principals pursue extramural interests
to the detriment of their business is the fact that the ability to pursue
these external, often costly, interests is largely reliant upon their firms
remaining profitable. Moreover, in the case of the Wilsons, like many
other entrepreneurs, it was demonstrably not the case that the pursuit
of ‘aristocratic' and business interests was incompatible. As theNew York
Times observed of Charles Henry and Arthur Wilson in 1891:
Business first and everything else afterwards is the maxim of the
Wilson family. On a recent occasion, when the Duke of Clarence
attended a meet at Tranby Croft, there were two flags to be seen fly-
ing at the tower. One was the union jack; the other was the flag of
the Wilson liners. And the union jack was not the uppermost of the
two. This arrangement, whether accidental or intended, exemplifies
pretty well the relative positions which business and sentiment hold
in the affection of the two brothers.60
Indeed, undertaking seemingly counter-productive activities could
actually prove to be hugely beneficial for the firm. Reputation is a vital
‘intangible asset' in business, and a display of wealth gained by virtue
of successful commercial dealings might enhance the reputation of a
business leader.61 There were other advantages to be gained through
admittance to rarefied social circles, as evidenced by Charles Hellyer's
successful rise from Hull's trawl fishery into the higher echelons of
county society. A reconstitution of Hellyers SFC in 1897 saw the firm
attract major investment from Sir Tatton Sykes, who bought 1,000 shares
(16.3 per cent) in the company. Sykes was the 5th Baronet - a titled and

Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey 213
wealthy landowner - whose family seat was the stately Sledmere House
in East Yorkshire.62
Regardless of the benefits or otherwise of the ‘gentrification' of
business leaders, ‘independence' and ‘self-reliance' are arguably more
important constituents of British culture. The esteem afforded to these
‘national traits' is apparent in the writing of philosophers and politi-
cal economists such as John Stuart Mill and Thomas Malthus, and the
populist works of Samuel Smiles and his ilk. In 1882, for example,
Smiles wrote: ‘[t]he spirit of self-help is the root of all genuine growth
in the individual; and, exhibited in the lives of many, it constitutes the
true source of national vigour and strength'. It might be argued that
self help was a self-serving myth on behalf of entrepreneurs who were
reliant - especially in the early stages of developing their venture - upon
others for the necessary resources. But as Wilson asserts, ‘[t]he key to
understanding this situation is the degree to which contemporary busi-
nessmen would allow outsiders to influence internal activities within
the firm, because while they relied enormously on external networks
for certain factors of production, few would envisage any interference
with management'.63 This occurred in Hellyers SFC when the founder,
Charles Hellyer, asserted his control over the firm in the face of a dimin-
ished shareholding. The nominal capital of the company was increased
from £41,000 to £80,000 between 1891 and 1897, a rise that was
accompanied by a decline in Charles's stake from 3,208 shares to 2,381
shares. However, the founder consolidated his position in the company
by securing the position of Permanent Director, through which he was
‘entitled to retain the office until he resigns the office, or dies, or ceases
to hold shares or Stock of the nominal value of not less than £5,000'.64
Compelling though these national cultural explanations might be, the
local environment was at least as important in shaping business behav-
iour. As Geoffrey Jones and Mary B. Rose state: ‘[t]he focus on national
cultures can be misleading … [the] idea that nineteenth century British
businessmen merely fell prey to a set of nationally determined social
priorities is to deny the importance of locally and sectorally distinctive
values, skills and attitudes'.65 In essence, the national traits of ‘inde-
pendence' and ‘self-reliance' could be enhanced or weakened by the
immediate environment in which a firm operated, as the case of Hull's
maritime industries demonstrates. Location played a pivotal role in
this respect. Cast by Larkin as late as 1986 as an ‘isolate city … lonely
northern daughter … holding through centuries her separate place',66
Hull's remoteness from other centres of population - the Leeds-Bradford
conurbation is about 50 miles to the west, across one of the largest

214 Private Companies, Culture and Place in Hull's Maritime Business Sector
tracts of agricultural land in central England - was exacerbated by the
comparatively poor transport links of the pre-1914 era. Indeed, ‘the
extreme geographical isolation of the town' in the nineteenth century
is invariably highlighted in contemporary and historical appraisals of its
development,67 which generally infer that insularity and introversion
characterized the citizens of a place that ‘glories in standing still'.68
It would be an exaggeration, however, to say that the relative remote-
ness of the locality prevented external influences from penetrating
Hull's maritime business community, which, given the very nature
of sea-related activity, had vital overseas connections. But it is highly
probable that the relative isolation of Hull meant that the fortunes of
individual firms had a greater resonance in this ‘lonely daughter' than
they would have done in other towns and cities of comparable size. This
accounts for the close correlation between ownership and control in
the maritime sector, and its relative immunity from outside influences.
As Brown and Rose observe, ‘depending upon the relative importance
of a business group, the culture of the firms helped to mould that of
the surrounding society'.69 The shipping concern of TWSC was funda-
mental to this process. The monopoly enjoyed by the company in Hull
was not only captured in a local adage - ‘Hull is Wilson's, Wilson's is
Hull' - but also explicitly recognized in a report prepared by a branch
of MI5 regarding the 1914-18 war in which ‘the strength of the Wilson
influence at Hull' was noted.70 The sheer size and commercial power
of this private limited company meant that it exerted an extraordinary
influence on the behaviour of the local maritime business commu-
nity, an influence that was enhanced by the diversification of Wilson
family members from shipping into other sea-related industries. By
such means, Hull's maritime business people were both directly and
imperceptibly encultured by TWSC's ‘set of values, norms and beliefs',71
which was imbued with the conviction that family ownership and man-
agement was inextricably linked to success in business.
Culture matters
Three threads are interwoven to form the fabric of this chapter. First,
examination of the legal framework within which the organization of
British firms evolved indicates that shipowners were not especially priv-
ileged with regard to limited liability before 1850, a finding that tem-
pers the conclusions of earlier works on shipowning. This discussion
further suggests that a large proportion of companies remained largely
unchanged even after they incorporated under the terms of legislation

Michaela G. Barnard and David J. Starkey 215
passed in 1862, with many continuing, in effect, to operate as private
limited companies. The second thread offers empirical evidence relat-
ing to Hull's maritime businesses to assess the extent to which a private
mentalité prevailed in the port's business community, an assessment
that revises conventional wisdom regarding the significance of joint-
stock organization in the local maritime sector, notably the steam trawl
fishery. The importance of people and place in shaping commercial
strategies and organizational structures is appraised in the third element
of the chapter. Here, it is evident that business behaviour is not merely
dictated by profit levels, with culture paramount in conditioning the
way entrepreneurs organize, develop and conduct their firms. As Wilson
observes, ‘one must state boldly that Culture matters'.72 The national
culture system is important in this respect but, as evidenced by Hull's
maritime business sector, it is at the local level, or within the context of
‘place', that culture is honed.
This tripartite approach sheds lights on wider themes. For instance,
in condemning the ‘cultural critique', Rubenstein notes that one of the
most objectionable features of the criticism levelled at ‘Britain's economic
performance during the past generation is its obsession for manufactur-
ing industry, its manufacturing fetishism. Tacitly, only manufacturing
counts as "legitimate" business activity.'73 Comprising a range of service
and extractive activities, as well as manufacturing, the maritime business
sector should be afforded particular attention if a comprehensive under-
standing of the relationship between culture and business behaviour is to
be attained. As this chapter demonstrates, moreover, invaluable insights
into business evolution can be gained from comparative analyses of mari-
time industries in particular spatial contexts. Such analyses, in turn, have
the capacity to inform broader debates about economic performance over
the long term. Accordingly, to address the criticism voiced by Skip Fischer
that opens this chapter, further research into sea-related enterprise will
enable maritime historians to contribute effectively to scholarly debates
in business-facing fields of historical enquiry.
