## 13
Risks and Rewards: The Business
of Norwegian Shipping
Stig Tenold
Due to the volatility of freight rates and vessel values, shipping is gen-
erally considered an industry in which the risks of doing business are
substantial.1 One reflection of this riskiness is the fact that fortunes can
be made, and lost, extremely quickly in the shipping sector. This chap-
ter discusses how shipowners in Norway, which for more than a century
has been one of the world's leading maritime nations, have dealt with
the question of business risk in a historical perspective.
Four time-specific cases are presented. The first is the traditional
business model that facilitated the expansion of Norwegian shipping
in the sailing ship era. Second, the advent of tanker shipping in the
interwar period saw high levels of Norwegian participation, and the
owners' willingness to take on new types of risks bore fruit. In the third
case, the first postwar decades, the most common Norwegian strate-
gies involved a substantial overall increase of risk. The hazards of this
approach became evident when shipping markets collapsed in the early
1970s. Finally, Norwegian owners' retreat towards less volatile market
segments during the last few decades is briefly presented.
The chapter deals specifically with the two most important aspects
of business risk within shipping: what I have decided to call ‘the activ-
ity risk' and ‘the asset risk'. The activity risk refers to the volatility of
profits from the sale of transport services, while the asset risk refers to
the effects of fluctuations in asset values on the companies' balance
sheets. Through a very simple analytical framework, it is shown how
the manner in which Norwegian shipowners have been exposed to, and
dealt with, these risks has changed over time. Some of these changes
have been related to the transformation of the shipping market, while
others have been the result of conscious or unconscious adaptation
by Norwegian owners. While these risks, at least to some extent, are
220

## Stig Tenold 221
general - that is, they are applicable to all shipping companies, regard-
less of nationality - differences in institutions and factor prices among
countries are likely to give a variety of arrangements and adaptations.
An introduction to business risk: the shipowners'
perspective
Shipping is often considered a high-risk industry. According to the
leading textbook in maritime economics, shipping sounds ‘more like a
gambling game than a sober transport business. Itisa gambling game.'2
In their book on risk management in shipping, Kavussanos and Visvikis
refer to eight broad risk categories, before pointing out that their list ‘is
certainly not exhaustive'.3 Today, shipping companies are well aware
of the different types of risks, and of the importance of communicat-
ing the manner in which these risks are managed. For instance, the
Norwegian shipping company Odfjell presents their approaches to
six different risks in their annual report, while the Danish company
Norden discusses their handling of seven specific risks.4
From a financial and economic point of view, the most important
risks are the ones related to the two tasks at the core of the shipping
companies' business; indeed, the two tasks that determine success or
failure in the shipping industry. The first task is the production and
sale of transport services. The second task is the investment in the
ships necessary to produce such services. Thus, it might be convenient
to use an uncomplicated analytical framework to isolate these parts of
the shipowners' business. Figure 13.1 presents this framework graphi-
cally. The left hand side - representing supply - refers to the factors
that are largely controlled by the shipowner, while the right-hand
side - depicting demand - refers to elements over which the owner
has less influence.
In the case of an individual ship, the activity risk is directly related
to the day-to-day operation of the vessel. Transport activities generate
profits; the freight income that the owner is able to obtain in the mar-
ket, minus the costs of providing the service. The costs refer not only
to operating costs, but also voyage and cargo handling costs, periodic
maintenance costs and capital costs. Dividends - or other forms of
remuneration to the owners - can be considered part of the capital cost
element. However, one can also think of the remuneration to the own-
ers as the ‘surplus' that is left when all other costs have been met.
The asset risk refers to the potential changes in the value of the ves-
sels, which are the main component on the company's balance sheet.

222 Risks and Rewards: The Business of Norwegian Shipping
Supply Demand
Transport costs Activity risk Freight rates
Investment choice Asset risk Vessel values
Figure 13.1 The main business risks faced by shipowners
Variations in vessel values lead to a gain or loss (relative to the book
value of the vessel), which is only realized when the ship is sold. The
very strong correlation between freight rates and vessel values implies
that the activity element and the asset element tend to move in the
same direction. High freight rates strengthen not only profits, but also
the balance sheet, while a depressed market results in a reduction of
both incomes and vessel values.
There are three advantages to the simple framework presented above.
First, it deals with the two factors that are most important for the health
and sustainability of the shipping company. Shipping fortunes have
been made both through the profits from the sale of transport services
and through ‘asset play' - strategies related to purchase and sale of
ships. Second, the framework covers the two significant parameters of a
‘going concern': income versus costs, on the one hand, and net assets,
on the other. Finally, by adopting a basic structure - where the core is
factors and mechanisms that have remained both important and more
or less unchanged over centuries - it is possible to compare the risks
faced by shipowners in different time periods.
Before I introduce the historical development into this framework,
however, two disclaimers are in order. First, there has never been such
a thing as a ‘common' Norwegian strategy. There is thus a substantial
element of generalization in the historical overview. Nevertheless,
I emphasize strategies and developments that pertain to the majority of
the companies, or a specific group of companies, in the various periods.
Second, several institutions and arrangements, what one might collec-
tively label ‘the maritime insurance industry', have been established to
reduce the shipowners' risk. Such measures are not the main focus here.
Insurance will be touched upon briefly where it is relevant to discuss
certain points. In general, however, maritime insurance is an important
and under-researched aspect of the shipping business that will remain
uncovered here as well.

## Stig Tenold 223
Case 1: The sailing ship era - limited capital, limited risk
In the second half of the nineteenth century, Norwegian ship-
ping expanded rapidly; according to calculations by the statistician
A.N. Kiær, the value of the Norwegian merchant marine multiplied by
a factor of more than four from 1850 to 1870.5 The basis of the growth
was a combination of beneficial domestic and international conditions.
In 1860 revenues from third-country shipping - transport between for-
eign ports - for the first time exceeded the revenues from transporting
Norwegian imports and exports.6
The main form of organization in Norwegian shipping was the part own-
ership system, as in many other maritime nations.7 This was a system that
facilitated capital accumulation, but also enabled investors to spread risks.
Ship investments were organized on what would, in current ‘management
speak', be called ‘a project basis'. Individual investors bought shares of a
vessel and received remuneration if the ship traded profitably. When the
ship was sold, or for other reasons ended its trading life, remaining funds
were paid out to the investors, and the partnership was dissolved.
Although profits from shipping in the sailing ship era fluctuated in
close relationship with general business cycles, the activity risk appears
to have been limited. One of the most thorough analyses of a ship-
ping business in the last part of the nineteenth century is Fischer and
Nordvik's account of the investments of the shipowner Peter Jebsen.
The activity risk, in Jebsen's case, was clearly limited. Close scrutiny of
the accounts proves that ‘it was indeed hard to lose money operating
sailing vessels in the early 1870s' and Jebsen's ‘high rates of return cast
doubts on the reliability of the traditional arguments about the [low]
profitability of sail' in the late 1880s.8
The low activity risks were matched by relatively limited asset risk.
Due to the part-ownership system, the financial resources that an inves-
tor needed to enter the shipping industry were small; the size of the
balance sheet was limited. The initial expansion in the first half of the
nineteenth century was mainly based on cheap resources (wood and
labour) used to build ships locally. However, even when Norwegian
owners expanded through investments in second-hand sailing vessels,
‘[t]he risk was minimal, since purchase prices were close to wreck or
hulk values, and sometimes lower'.9
The apparently low activity and asset risks in the sailing ship era have
one important caveat: it is primarily valid for ships that sailed without seri-
ous incidents. This was not the case for all ships, however: in the ten years
after 1890, more than 2,000 Norwegian ships were wrecked.10 On average,

224 Risks and Rewards: The Business of Norwegian Shipping
more than 4.75 per cent of the Norwegian sailing fleet was wrecked annu-
ally in the period from 1851 to 1914. Before 1880 around 3.1 per cent of
the sailing fleet suffered this fate every year. However, from 1881 to 1914,
with the average age of Norwegian sailing vessels getting markedly higher,
the share increased to more than 5.6 per cent annually.11
The wreckage figures illustrate that though shipping investments
appeared to be low-risk, they were far from risk-free. In fact, marine
accidents created a two-way trade-off between activity and asset risk.
Some owners refrained from insuring their vessels; this led to lower
costs and higher profits (lower activity risk), but implied the total loss of
the investment if the ship were wrecked (higher asset risk). Other own-
ers chose to over-insure the vessels, particularly in years with a bad mar-
ket (and correspondingly low vessel values). One Norwegian shipowner,
Fredrik Smith Petersen, claimed that this could explain the increasing
losses in bad years; owners speculated on the ship being lost, the crew
being saved and the insurance premium being paid out.12
Even with the transformation towards more expensive steamships,
the Norwegian ownership system enabled the limitation of asset risks.
According to Fischer and Fon's analysis of the investments of the bro-
ker and shipowner, Thomas Fearnley, ‘we see little evidence of classical
Schumpeterian risk-taking in his activities'. Like most Norwegian ship-
owners, Fearnley had a conservative investment pattern, preferring to
purchase shares rather than entire vessels: ‘This behaviour, of course,
had the advantage of spreading risk in an industry in which the peril of
watching helplessly as a valuable asset was lost was always very real.'13
The strategies of Thomas Fearnley conform to the general view of the
cautious Norwegian shipowners. However, as the value of ships increased,
the possibility of making money in the sale and purchase market grew.
In his analysis of the business of the shipowner Lauritz Kloster, Helge
Nordvik emphasizes that ‘[t]here were, however, some notable exceptions
to the relative lethargy and aversion to risk that characterised many of the
established shipowners'. From 1911 onwards, Kloster followed a strategy
of ‘speculative investments in vessels with a view to securing profits not
through operations but through "asset play"'. Indeed, ‘[w]hen the chance
to alter his strategy from low to high-risk, increasingly dependent on asset
play, arose as a result of the war, Kloster rapidly pursued it vigorously'.14
Kloster was not alone in following a speculative asset play strategy
during World War I. Many established owners became increasingly
bullish, and there was a steady influx of risk-seeking newcomers to the
industry. However, several spectacular bankruptcies revealed the perils of
a too ambitious and expansive strategy. For many Norwegian shipping

## Stig Tenold 225
investors, the 1920s was a time to lick their wounds. Others struck black
gold in a rapidly growing, but relatively new, segment of the shipping
industry. The success story of Norwegian shipping in the interwar period
is closely intertwined with new investments in oil tankers, investments
that presented the owners with new and different types of risks.
Case 2: Interwar tanker shipping - high rewards
for new risks
The transformation of Norwegian shipping in the interwar period has
been succinctly characterized by Haakon With-Andersen as the develop-
ment from laggards to leaders.15 The basis was the rapid modernization
of the fleet, specifically the focus on modern motorships and tankers.
The Norwegian foray into the tanker segment stood out in an interna-
tional perspective. This new strategy enabled many owners to weather
the storms in what was generally a difficult period for shipping.
The tanker ventures also involved a new way of conducting business.
While many Norwegian owners still operated along traditional lines,
the tanker investments provide a good illustration of an ‘investment
choice' that totally transformed the approach to and question of risk.
The purchase of a series of second-hand tankers from Anglo-Saxon
Petroleum, the transport arm of Royal Dutch Shell, has often been
described as the breakthrough for Norwegian owners in the oil tanker
industry. While the direct long-term importance of these purchases is
debatable, the ‘Anglo-Saxon business model' became the fundament for
most tanker owners.16 The oil company chartered the ships for a period
of ten years and also arranged long-term financing for 75 to 80 per
cent of the purchase price. This business model - with a steady income
stream from long charters and substantial external financing - became
a blueprint for most expanding Norwegian tanker owners.17
What characterized the risk aspect of this business model? The long-
term time-charters with the oil companies reduced the activity risk.
The owners knew what their annual income would be throughout the
charter period, and the activity risk would then be reduced to the ques-
tion of the ability to keep costs down and the effects of currency fluc-
tuations. Moreover, in the case of the Anglo-Saxon ships, the financing
provided by the oil company implied that the demand for equity from
the owner was relatively low, varying between £10,000 and £20,000.
Funds from external shareholders, investments from brokers, ship
chandlers and insurance brokers, further alleviated the owners' need for
cash, and thus reduced the asset risk.

226 Risks and Rewards: The Business of Norwegian Shipping
The Anglo-Saxon purchases were but a small part of the Norwegian
tanker expansion. From the mid-1920s onwards, a number of owners
began to order new-built tankers, particularly from yards in Sweden and
Denmark. Usually, but not always, these ships were committed to long-
term charters. The depressed world economy and the massive competition
in the shipbuilding industry meant that the yards established a system of
relatively generous financing for new-buildings, on a scale similar to the
80 per cent offered by Anglo-Saxon. Both the activity risk and the asset risk
therefore remained low in the initial period of the ships' economic life,
enabling entrepreneurs with limited capital to amass funds for expansion.
Again, however, there is a caveat. The Norwegian tanker tale ended well
at the outbreak of World War II, and the tankers played a crucial role dur-
ing the war. This happy ending reflected the fact that oil transports were
the only rapidly increasing segment of international seaborne transports
in the interwar period. The investments were undoubtedly a gamble, but
a gamble that paid off because the stakes were placed on the right horse.
Indeed, if tanker demand had developed differently, the fact that tankers
were inflexible and purpose-built for one market would have changed the
outcome significantly. Indeed, as Sturmey has pointed out: ‘For the inde-
pendent shipowner tanker ownership entails some special risks in that
he is completely dependent for his livelihood on a few large companies
whose residual requirements only he satisfies'.18
Investments in tanker tonnage, financially backed by long-term char-
ters to the oil companies, became an important business model for many
Norwegian and Greek shipowners in the decades after World War II.
However, the remuneration from the time-charters was lower than that
which the owners in good years could reap in the spot market, where
transport services are sold on a voyage-by-voyage basis. Shipowners paid
a premium, in the case of foregone earnings, to get the security of a long-
term charter. Consequently, just as risk-prone owners in the sailing ship
era sailed without insurance in order to improve profits, an increasing
share of the tanker community sought the added incomes available in
the spot market. As long as the market was good, this was a beneficial
choice. When the tanker market turned sour, it proved to be disastrous.
Case 3: The first post-war decades - becoming ‘the
gamblers among the gamblers'
Michael Porter refers to the Scandinavian tanker owners in the early
1970s as ‘gamblers among the gamblers of the shipping industry'.19
While this impression is partly a result of misinterpretation and based

## Stig Tenold 227
on faulty empirics, there is an element of truth in it. By the early 1970s
there is little doubt that the Norwegian shipowners in general, and
tanker owners in particular, were more exposed in the event of a market
downturn than their foreign competitors.20 Thus, when the shipping
market collapsed, few nations were as hard hit by the crisis as Norway.
How can we account for the massive vulnerability - or risk exposure - of
Norwegian owners?
The demand for seaborne transport increased rapidly from 1950
to 1973. A number of factors worked together to push up shipping
demand: transport-dependent industrialization in Europe and Japan,
the continuing growth of oil consumption, which was largely filled by
supply from the Middle East, and changes in sea lanes, in particularly
during the two closures of the Suez canal. As a result of these beneficial
demand developments, the world fleet grew rapidly, with economies of
scale as an important ingredient of the expansion (see Chapter 5 by Yrjö
Kaukiainen in this volume).
From the early 1960s the Norwegian fleet developed differently from
the fleets of most other countries. There was still a predilection for tanker
investments, but the ships now became both larger and, as a result of fre-
quent renewal, younger than the world average. This development, which
can be explained by the relative price of capital and labour in Norway,
implied that the asset risk increased substantially. While external financing
was still extremely important, the sheer size of the units led to a problem
of divisibility. The owners put all their eggs in one basket, not only by
operating relatively inflexible ships that could transport a limited number
of cargoes, but by operating fleets of few large vessels, rather than many
small vessels. In gambling terms, they went ‘all in' on a number of large
positions, rather than hedging their bets. The asset risk became substantial.
However, in a market characterized by demand growth this was not prob-
lematic; as a result of the development of ship values, good timing of sales
and purchases meant that the value reduction of the vessels was limited.
At the same time, many shipowners purposely increased their activ-
ity risk. The duration of the time-charters was reduced, and an increas-
ing proportion of the fleet was operated in the spot market. This led
to phenomenal revenues when the market was good, but exposed the
owners to inactivity and reduced incomes when demand failed to live
up to expectations. A series of close freight rate peaks in the late 1960s
and early 1970s convinced many tanker owners that the spot market
strategy was superior.
The question of the risk inherent in the different chartering strategies
was frequently debated. Hilmar Reksten was a proponent of the spot

228 Risks and Rewards: The Business of Norwegian Shipping
market strategy, and not without reason; in 1973 he recouped more
than 40 per cent of the building price of one of his tankers during a
voyage that lasted only 68 days. His main opponent, and vying with
Reksten for the position as Norway's largest tanker owner, was Sigval
Bergesen, who generally operated in the traditional manner, foregoing
the freight rate peaks but securing a steady stream of income from long-
term charters.
Reksten claimed that Bergesen was in fact the risk-prone one, as
cost inflation and currency developments eroded the income from
Bergesen's charters with the oil companies.21 In the early 1970s Reksten
appeared to be correct. In the period 1967-73 a spot market strategy
gave an average annual return of 39.2 per cent of the new-building
price, compared with 17.2 per cent for a strategy relying on one- to
three-year charters and 12.8 per cent for three- to seven-year charters.22
Such differences were, however, premised upon the existence of fre-
quent periods of undercapacity with concomitant freight rate peaks.
When the tanker market from 1974 onwards entered an almost fifteen-
year-long period of depressed freight rates, history became a merciless
judge in the dispute between Reksten and Bergesen. The former was
responsible for Norway's until then largest bankruptcy, while the latter
could thank the long-term contracts for his position as one of Norway's
very few shipowners running a profitable company.
The discussion of risk within shipping also received academic atten-
tion. In 1970, two young economists, Peter Lorange and Victor Norman,
presented seventeen Scandinavian tanker owners with a number of
hypothetical investment alternatives in order to gauge their attitudes
towards risk. The owners systematically disregarded the safe alterna-
tives, preferring instead risky investments with identical expected
returns.23 Indeed as the expected return in real life was higher in the rel-
atively risky spot market, it should come as no surprise that Norwegian
shipowners, if this risk-prone group were representative, would shun
long-term charters.
Furthermore, the joint movement of freight rates and vessel values
put the owners in a very difficult position. Revenues dissipated as a
result of the freight rate reduction, and the overcapacity in the shipping
market reduced the value of some ship types by as much as two-thirds.
The significant external financing implied that this was a problem the
shipowners shared with the banks. However, the mortgage conditions
usually included a ‘minimum value clause'; if the value of the ship fell
below a certain threshold, the owner was forced to put up additional
collateral or sell the vessel. As a result, the banks would often force the

## Stig Tenold 229
disposal of vessels in a depressed market - ‘selling chicken in the rain',
as it became known in Norwegian shipping circles - securing some pay-
back on the mortgage, but leaving the owners without assets.24
When the market collapsed, the perils of the ‘typical' Norwegian
strategy became obvious. Vessels in the spot market were laid up, while
those trading on short time-charters failed to find profitable employ-
ment when the charters expired. The strategy of frequent renewal of
ships also implied that Norwegian owners had large amounts of new-
buildings on order. Some relented and paid heavy cancellation fees,
while others took delivery of vessels that were worth only a fraction of
the original investment. In one way there is a parallel to the situation in
the sailing ship era; just as the Norwegian strategy then was beneficial as
long as the ships sailed without accident, the post-war decade strategy
was eminently suited to a market with growing demand. When vessels
were wrecked, and when the market collapsed, the results were cata-
strophic. It is tempting to reduce the most extreme strategies to a binary
or ‘black-and-white' outcome. When things went well, all was good.
When disaster struck, all was lost. For most Norwegian owners there was
no alternative strategy if the vessels were wrecked or the market growth
declined - such incidents led to an exit from shipping.
The shipping crisis led to haemorrhaging of the sector in Norway. The
number of companies and the size of the fleet were drastically reduced.
Too many shipowners had assumed activity and asset risks that were
unsustainable when the market collapsed. The problems were of course
a result of the demand side of the business risk framework presented in
Figure 13.1: the low freight rates and vessel values. However, increas-
ingly uncompetitive Norwegian costs and extremely unfortunate invest-
ment choices made the situation even worse. Indeed, the fact that some
Norwegian companies survived indicates that the basis for the problems
was not only exogenous. There were successful companies, those that
had chosen a strategy with less spectacular short-term profits than the
spot tanker market, but which proved sustainable in the longer term.
Case 4: After the crisis - once bitten…
The majority of the companies that survived the shipping crisis had
diversified their investments, primarily through investment in new and
innovative specialized vessel types such as car carriers, chemical tankers
or gas carriers. To follow Sturmey's argument above, these ships were
extremely specialized, usually suitable only for a specific cargo, and as
such apparently increased both the activity and the asset risk. However,

230 Risks and Rewards: The Business of Norwegian Shipping
the specialized market segments are characterized by long-term rela-
tionships between shipping companies and their customers. Specialized
markets, often referred to as ‘industrial shipping', are not characterized
by the same cut-throat competition and flexible price-setting as the
main dry and liquid bulk markets. This feature improved the predict-
ability of the revenues, which again was helped by institutional changes
that reduced the costs of providing transport.
The 1987 introduction of the Norwegian International Ship Register
led to a substantial reduction of operating costs for vessels flying the
Norwegian flag. The strong growth of the Norwegian fleet in subse-
quent years was partly a result of asset play strategies by companies
speculating on an improvement in vessel values. In the longer term,
however, the importance of such strategies has been reduced. The
majority of the current Norwegian shipping companies are involved
in the highly specialized markets of industrial shipping, where one of
their competitive advantages is a reputation for solidity and competent
handling of risks.
The Norwegian expansion within specialized shipping was far from
risk-free. The case was rather the opposite; it involved the launching
and nurturing of non-existent markets and also hinged upon new, and
often untried, technologies. However, the pioneering role gave the
Norwegians a head start, and though the revenues have been less spec-
tacular than what the tanker market could offer during the peaks, as a
long-term strategy it appears more viable. Moreover, a number of the
companies involved in specialized shipping have diversified their activi-
ties by investing in related activities, such as for instance terminals that
both improve the service they can offer their customers and provide
a diversification of the business.
Risks and rewards in Norwegian shipping - a long-term
synthesis
The Norwegian exposure to business risk in shipping has changed over
time. The scheme presented initially, dividing the business risk into an
‘activity risk' and an ‘asset risk', enables the evaluation of the relative
importance of various types of risks, as seen in Figure 13.2.
In Case 1, referring to the sailing ship era, the most important risk
was related to the perils of the sea: would the vessel be able to safely sail
from one destination to the next? The capital investment was relatively
meagre, and through the part ownership system, investors were able
to spread their risks by holding minority shares in a number of vessels.

## Stig Tenold 231
Case 3
Case 4
Case 2
Case 1
Low Activity risk High
In principle, both the activity risk and the asset risk were relatively low.
However, in cases where the ships were wrecked, it was a total loss. The
risks could, however, be mitigated by insurance of the individual owner-
ship parts of the ships.
The farewell to the old sailing ships was accompanied by the intro-
duction of new business models - and new types of risk. The shift from
sail to steam and the entry of extremely risk-seeking investors during
World War One have only been briefly touched upon here. A more
long-term illustration of the changing risks is the Norwegian foray into
the tanker sector in the interwar period, presented as Case 2. For ship-
owners operating on long-term charters to the oil companies, revenues
were secure and external financing reduced the amount of the owners'
own funds that were invested. The risk was therefore primarily related
to cost developments and the state of the ship and the shipping market
when the charter expanded.
The Norwegian fleet expanded considerably in the first post-war dec-
ades, driven by rapidly increasing demand for seaborne trade and growing
use of economies of scale. From the early 1960s onwards, the investment
pattern changed towards increasing asset risk. Although external financ-
ing was still important, the high cost of the new vessels raised the stakes.
This was compounded by an increase in the activity risk, as a relatively
high share of the Norwegian ships was operated in the spot market or on
short charters. The high asset and activity risks in Case 3 can explain why
Norway was hit particularly hard by the shipping crisis.
hgiH

Figure 13.2 An evaluation of activity and asset risk in Norwegian shipping

232 Risks and Rewards: The Business of Norwegian Shipping
The negative experiences of the 1970s and early 1980s most likely
affected the strategic adaptation when Norwegian shipping broke out
from the doldrums around 1990. Moreover, the majority of the compa-
nies that survived the crisis had introduced innovations and followed a
relatively low-risk, industrial shipping strategy, presented asCase 4, and
continued in this vein into the new millennium.
Concluding remarks
The simple framework presented in this chapter, combined with domes-
tic elements such as factor costs and institutional arrangements, may be
used to evaluate the changing nature of risks and rewards in shipping
in a long-term perspective. Throughout the four periods described, the
two tasks at the core of the shipping companies' business - the pro-
duction and sale of transport services and the investment in the ships
necessary to produce these services - have remained relatively constant.
However, both the manner in which the companies have solved these
tasks and the resources necessary to do this, have changed. This chapter
has looked at the development with Norwegian shipping companies as
the unit of analysis. A natural extension would be to apply the same
framework to the shipping industries of other countries.
Fischer and Nordvik conclude their analysis of Peter Jebsen's nine-
teenth-century business strategy by pointing out the importance of
‘innovation and calculated risk. It is by pursuing this path that dynamic
capitalists are made and prosperous modern economies established.'25
Innovation and calculated risk characterize Norwegian shipping today.
Still, a lot of shipowners lost sight of the path and embarked on a long
and risky detour, leading to gamblers' ruin rather than prosperity.
