## 14
Business Groups and
Entrepreneurial Families in
Southern Europe: Comparing
Greek and Spanish Shipowners
in the Nineteenth and
Twentieth Centuries1
Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso
Introduction
This chapter aims at comparing and examining the origins and evolu-
tion of business groups in southern Europe, focusing on Spanish and
Greek business groups originating from shipping from the late nine-
teenth century to the beginning of the twenty-firstcentury. Within the
European context Greece and Spain were late industrializing countries
that experienced remarkable economic growth during the second half
of the twentieth century. A modern shipping industry had emerged in
both countries since the last quarter of the nineteenth century, with
quite similar characteristics until the 1930s: Greeks and Spaniards
became low cost tramp operators in the international market. However,
after the mid-1930s, Spain became a minor player in the international
shipping business while Greece became the leading shipping nation
of the world.2 Notwithstanding these divergent paths of development,
business groups and entrepreneurial families pervaded the shipping
industry in both countries throughout the nineteenth and twentieth
centuries. In fact, some of these have proved particularly resilient to
changes in markets, technologies, governments and even political
regimes.
This chapter compares and examines: 1) the origins of business
groups in the two countries; 2) their strategy, structure and evolution,
and 3) the factors that account for their long-term survival. As Khana
and Yafeh reckoned, ‘Historical and dynamic … perspectives of business
235

236 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
groups can enrich our understanding of this institution'.3 Our empirical
base consists of a multiple case study, based on our selection of the most
important shipping business groups in both countries for which in-
depth historical analysis is available. This qualitative research approach
is particularly well-suited to an exploratory study such as this, where
questions are complex and some of the theoretical propositions are
grounded in and emerge from the data and analysis that follow, rather
than being exclusively derived from prior theory.4
The structure of the chapter is as follows. First we offer a brief over-
view of the literature on business groups and entrepreneurial families;
second, we present our main theoretical propositions, built upon the
theoretical literature and the analysis of our empirical base; then we
make a brief presentation of our case studies and discuss the main find-
ings derived from these; finally, some preliminary conclusions and a
research agenda close this study.
A review of the literature
Literature on business or economic groups shows that these appeared as
a very common type of business organization in developing economies
such as those of Latin America and Asia in the twentieth century. Further
research reveals that groups pervaded the business world of Europe, Japan
and even the United States during the first stages of their industrialization
process in the nineteenth century, and in some countries, notably those
of southern Europe and Japan, have persisted throughout the twentieth
century.5 Business groups ‘typically consist of legally independent firms
operating in multiple (often unrelated) industries, bound together by
persistent formal (e.g. equity) and informal (e.g. family ties)'.6 Thegrupos
económicosin Latin America, the merchant houses of India and Pakistan,
the Japanese zaibatsu or the Koreanchaebol, would be examples of such
business organizations. All of these are family-owned business groups,
that is, controlled and managed by one or more entrepreneurial families.7
Families exert control by means of several mechanisms, holding compa-
nies or family offices, equity sharing and interlocking directorships, and
the alliance with, or even creation of, a bank. Banks have usually played
a pivotal role in the origins and evolution of business groups.
The economic literature has provided two types of explanations for
business groups' formation; exogenous and endogenous. The former sees
business groups as a response to market imperfections and high transaction
costs in economies with high political and economic risks and uncertainty.
The strategy of vertical integration and economic diversification allowed

Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso 237
groups to reduce transaction costs and overcome market difficulties. Their
availability of human resources, skills and capital gave them an advantage
entering new markets and new businesses.8 Endogenous explanations,
on the other hand, argue that business groups have particular skills and
capabilities that provide them with a competitive advantage when enter-
ing new businesses and undertaking new projects: ‘project execution
capability'9and the capability to combine resources for repeated industry
entry.10 This capability includes both intangibles such as contacts and
know-how, and tangibles such as the factors, services and products of the
existing firms. For this capability to become a sustained advantage, certain
limits to competition (national and/or foreign) are needed.
Contrary to multidivisional firms, business groups rarely developed
very formal structures of governance. Frequently families control the
group's companies by means of holding firms or family offices, and by
their presence on the executive boards of every company. Another com-
mon trait is their strong relationship with governments, which has given
them several advantages over competitors. Business groups have thrived
within very different political regimes and economic policies.11 But as
Schneider has stressed, ‘business groups vary over time, across countries,
and within countries in terms of what could be called degrees of politi-
cal intimacy'.12 The higher the degree of state intervention in a given
economy, the more valuable are the contact capabilities of the group.13
But the stronger the political intimacy between a business group and the
government, the more volatile the fortune of that group can be.14
In spite of the growing interest in business groups, there is still a lot
of research to be done in this field. Khana and Yafeh argued that ‘many
more historical studies with explicit hypotheses in mind' are needed.15
Our main propositions
The reasons that account for the origins of business groups have to do
with the characteristics of the business environment, as the exogenous
theories argue, but also with the particular characteristics of the forma-
tion of the groups, as the endogenous explanations support. Business
groups create the organizational structures of the companies and net-
works that make their production systems. Their main strength stems
from their organizational sources and human resources along with the
reproduction of entrepreneurship.16
Proposition 1: Business groups are more common in economies with less
developed market institutions - the greater the market imperfections, the

238 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
greater the importance of business groups in a given economy. Business groups
develop institutions based on the organizational capabilities and human
resources on which their competitive advantage rests.
We posit that both Spain's and Greece's economies during the period
from the late nineteenth century until their entry to the European
Economic Community (EEC), regardless of changing political regimes,
shared four distinctive traits: the existence of imperfections in markets
with regard to factors (particularly capital) and information; a high
degree of state intervention and discretion in its decisions; the exist-
ence of limits to competition; and the existence of an asymmetric
commercial policy that protected the national market and supported
exports.17
As to the structure and growth strategies, we assume that the extent
of the group's strategies of vertical integration and diversification is
strongly correlated with the levels of internal and external competition.
In other words, as competition gets fiercer, groups tend to diminish
the degree of vertical integration and unrelated diversification, con-
centrating instead on sectors where they have a distinctive competitive
advantage. With regard to structure, the need for coordination and
control of the group's firms would increase with the level of competi-
tion and selection in the market.18 In the case of business groups that
stem from shipping, it is noted that in times of economic crisis they
revert to shipping, the sector in which they had developed a competi-
tive advantage.
Proposition 2: The business group structure is related to the development
of capital markets and the skills of entrepreneurial families to maintain their
control. Its strategy is related to the main characteristics of the business envi-
ronment in which the group operates: the extent of vertical integration and
economic diversification should be higher in economies with high barriers to
competition and low levels of economic openness. As competition increases,
groups tend to specialize, that is, to concentrate on the sectors where they keep
their main competitive advantages over their competitors.
Following Kock and Guillén we assume the existence of a nega-
tive correlation between the degree of government support and the
group's base of resources and capabilities: for instance, the higher the
government support, the weaker (or less distinctive or less sustainable)
the group's resources and capabilities may be.19 Part of the survival
technique of multi-generational business groups is the close relation-
ship with main political parties and governments. As business groups'
leaders form the most powerful capitalists in developing countries, all
governments seek alliance with them.

Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso 239
Proposition 3: Members of family business groups always try to keep close
relations with governments. There is usually a policy of promoting members
of the family to be involved in politics and to acquire and develop as many
‘contacts' as possible. But the higher the government support or the stronger
the political intimacy between the group and the existing government, the
weaker the group's base of resources and capabilities tends to be and the more
its long-term survival is threatened.
Family-based business groups show higher rates of survival because
family firms usually have ‘dynastic motivations': they want to transmit
the business to the next generation.20 In order to ensure succession
over generations, entrepreneurial families need first, qualified and
capable male heirs to continue the business, and second, structures to
maintain their equity ownership together, such as holding companies,
private partnerships, or family offices. The second reason for multi-
generation survival is the tendency to fragmentation of family members
of business groups who start their own companies in similar fields such
as in shipping.21 The tendency to fragmentation of business groups
has proved pivotal for longevity in shipping business as it renews
entrepreneurship.22
Proposition 4: Family considerations have a strong influence on the sub-
sequent development path of family-based business groups. Their long-term
survival depends on: an effective and non-traumatic succession (availability
of male heirs, able and capable); the availability of mechanisms to keep fam-
ily stock grouped; the flexibility to cope with changes in markets, technologies
and governments; the tendency to fragmentation of business families and
start-ups of new companies in the same field of business that renews entrepre-
neurship and contributes to the longevity of business families.
The shipping business takes place in a highly volatile international
environment, and the firms and groups that have survived over a
long time have accumulated skills of flexibility and adaptability in an
international environment that have allowed them to survive in times
of economic crisis at home and abroad.23 Shipping businesses are net-
work firms oriented towards international activities - thus not wholly
dependent on national economies. Central to the survival of family
business groups in traditional maritime countries is the development of
a common business culture and the creation of international networks
that internalize as many transactions as possible; these networks are
based on organizational and human resources and allow the entrance
of new firms allowing for the further renewal of the entrepreneur-
ship of the shipping business.24 Through a process of flexibility and
adjustment, culture and networks become embedded in the group's

240 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
and region's base of resources and capabilities, as a sort of ‘routine' or
‘memory bank' that tends to exhibit a path-dependent trajectory.25 In
times of crisis this ‘bank' helps business strategies by re-implementing
successful entrepreneurial practices of the past. When these practices do
not offer satisfactory results, the businesses search for new practices that
are subsequently copied, internalized and ‘routinized'.26
Proposition 5: Business groups have created a supportive environment at
home and abroad through the development of a common business culture and
the creation of international business network, allowing for the reproduction
and renewal of their production system by permitting the entry of new firms
and the development of new groups. This supportive environment has facili-
tated their long-term survival.
Our case studies
Our empirical evidence is composed of three in-depth case studies of
Spanish and Greek shipping business groups - the Aznar group on the
Spanish side, and the Embiricos and Andreadis groups on the Greek
one - that have persisted for three or more generations over a time-
span of more than a century. All three are fair examples of the most
important and influential shipping groups of their time.27 Moreover,
we have additional empirical support from other important business
groups such as the Comillas, Ybarra, and Fierro groups for Spain,28 and
the top business groups in Greece, including the Goulandris, Livanos,
Vardinoyannis, Latsis, or Alafouzos.29
Rich case studies, in spite of their limited applicability, have the
advantage of providing detailed descriptions that allow for a rigorous
contrasting of all the theoretical hypotheses presented and can also
offer new findings that may modify and improve them.30
The Aznar business group (1861 to the present)
The founder of the company, a self-made man, was Eduardo Aznar de
la Sota (1830-1902) who got involved in the international maritime
trade and shipping by the 1880s, when he was an important ship-
owner and exporter in Bilbao, the most dynamic port of Spain in the
last third of the nineteenth century. He first established, with Juan
B. Astigarraga, a shipbroking firm in 1861, and both promoted the
creation of the Compañía Bilbaína de Navegación (a public firm) in
1882. With his cousin Ramón de la Sota, he created in 1881 a limited
partnership, Sota & Aznar, the core of a business group which, initially

Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso 241
involved in mining and iron ore trade, thriving businesses in the
industrializing Basque country at that time, ended up as a giant group
of companies involved in shipping, shipbuilding, banking and insur-
ance, iron and steel and mining. Following the changes brought on
by the decline of the liner trade with America at the end of the nine-
teenth century and the steady increase of European tramp trades (coal,
iron ore, timber and grain), Eduardo Aznar was one of the shipown-
ers that transformed Bilbao into a main shipping centre where more
than half of the Spanish merchant tonnage was registered in 1900.31
He collaborated with agencies in London, kept direct contact with
the Baltic Exchange and became fully involved in investment in the
new technology of steam in the 1880s, keeping pace with the British,
the pioneers in steam technology and the world's leading maritime
nation. In the 1890s the house of Sota & Aznar created twenty-five
single-ship companies in a manner similar to that used by British or
Greek shipowners.32
The second generation were Eduardo Aznar's three sons. The Aznar
brothers, Eduardo (1860-1908), Luis Maria (1862-1929) and Alberto
(1865-1923) Aznar Tutor, were involved in the business from the
1880s to the end of the 1920s and were all educated in England where
they spent quite a number of years. The first-born, Eduardo, became
involved in the administration of the firm Aznar & Astigarraga and
all the shipping companies of the group, while the second-born,
Luis, joined the house Sota & Aznar. In the 1890s the maritime busi-
ness witnessed spectacular growth, and the Aznar family, through its
various companies (Naviera Sota & Aznar, Bilbaína de Navegación,
Remolcadores Ibaizábal and others), owned more than 60 ships and
formed the largest shipowning group in Spain in 1900.33 The Aznar
house, led by Eduardo and Alberto, over-invested in shipping that
did not yield the expected results after 1901, when freight rates plum-
meted. However, all the brothers invested in Euskalduna Shipyards at
the beginning of the twentieth century, and in the mining and iron
industries as well as in new technology industries like electricity. On
the deaths of the founder in 1902, and his eldest son Eduardo in 1908,
Luis Maria became the clear leader of the Aznar family, with the Sota
& Aznar group becoming one of the most powerful business groups
in Spain. Luis participated regularly in Spanish politics, becoming an
MP and senator in several legislatures, and was the representative of
the Bilbao Shipowners' Association in Madrid. After his death in 1929,
disputes between his heirs and the Sota family about the control and
management of the businesses emerged.34

242 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
The third and fourth generations of the Aznars led the family from the
1930s to the early 1980s. The third generation consisted of Luis Maria's
four sons: José Luis (1896-1951), Ignacio (1898-1953), Juan Antonio
(1901-72) and Javier Aznar Zavala (1904-64). José Luis, being the first-
born, managed the group from the late 1920s to the early 1950s. All four
brothers belonged to the Spanish business elite. The Sota & Aznar group
in the interwar period expanded in the service sector, shipping, railways
and banking, as well as in industry, mining and iron works. However,
increasing divergences between the two families, due to business and
political reasons, appeared in the 1920s and 1930s. The Sota family sup-
ported the Basque nationalist party, while the Aznar brothers followed
the family tradition of adherence to the Conservative party.35
During the Civil War the house Sota & Aznar was split in two. The
Franco government expropriated the Sota family's assets in the group,
which were bought by the Aznar family with the support of another
entrepreneurial family, the Gandarias Urquijo, and of the powerful
banks Urquijo and Hispano Americano. In this way, José Luis Aznar was
able to get control of the former Sota & Aznar group, now called Aznar.
He then devoted his efforts in the 1940s to reorganizing and develop-
ing his business and transforming the Aznar group into one of the most
powerful in the country with investments in shipping, shipbuilding and
related industries, banking, industry and real estate (see Appendix II). In
the 1950s the Aznar group of shipping companies was the biggest in the
country, owning one-fifth of the Spanish merchant tonnage.36
The early death of José Luis in 1951 brought the fourth generation to
the fore, with his first-born, Eduardo Aznar Coste (1920-81), becoming
the leader of the Aznar group at the age of 31. Eduardo continued the
development strategy that his father had set in the 1940s in maritime
transport and related industries along with the other companies in
industry, mining and real estate. In the 1960s, without abandoning
the previous businesses, he started new ones. In maritime transport, in
addition to the existing involvement of the Aznars in tramp shipping,
fruit transport, liner passenger shipping and coastal maritime transport,
Eduardo expanded into cruises to the Canaries, as well as other tourism
business such as tourist offices, air charters and hotels, and into the
tanker business. He pursued a risky strategy that led to over-investment
in bulk carriers, tankers and high-class ferries in the early 1970s. He also
invested in several other businesses, including the Coca Cola factories
in Spain. Eduardo, who belonged to the inner circle of Franco family
friends, was the leading Spanish shipowner in those years, becom-
ing a member of the Spanish parliament in the Francoist regime. The

Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso 243
problems of the group started in the late 1960s when Eduardo lost con-
trol of Euskalduna Shipyards and broke off relations with the Hispano
Americano bank. After the death of Franco in 1975, Eduardo Aznar, like
many other businessmen of the old establishment, had to cope with a
completely new political and economic scenario, with less influence,
more uncertainty and a deep economic crisis. The Naviera Aznar, highly
leveraged as a result of risky business strategies in the early 1970s, had
to suspend payments in 1979. Shortly after Eduardo's death in 1981
came the final bankruptcy of this company and its preferred partner,
the Banco Urquijo. This precipitated, as a result of the equity sharing
between the group's firms, the liquidation of many other companies of
the Aznar group. Its demolition was fairly representative of the period
of crisis and marked the new age in the Spanish entrepreneurial system
that followed incorporation to the EU.37
Another problem that the Aznar group had to face was related to the
succession, one of the main challenges of family-based groups. Eduardo
had attempted to educate his eldest son, Eduardo Aznar Sáinz, to become
the next leader of the group. But the heir's main interests were far away
from the businesses and, as soon as he had liquidated the bankruptcy of
Naviera Aznar, he withdrew from the picture. Contrary to what had hap-
pened in the previous generation, it was the youngest heir, Alejandro,
who resumed business activities in the companies that had been able to
survive, Remolcadores Ibaizabal in shipping services (tug boats) and the
vineyard Vinos de los Herederos del Marques de Riscal being the two
most important. In the late 1990s, he created Ibaizabal Management
Services, a company that has since heavily invested in bulk carriers and
tankers and now owns one of the largest fleets in Spain. However, both
the size of the group and the extent of its activities are substantially
smaller than during his father's leadership. Although Alejandro has
maintained a low public profile, he is also actively involved in the arena
of government-business relations, having been President of the Basque
Shipowners' Association and Vice-president of the Spanish Shipowners'
Association. In recent years, the sixth generation of Aznars, represented
by Aitor Aznar (Alejandro's older nephew) has been brought into mana-
gerial posts in the group.
The Embiricos business group (1860s to the present)
The founder of the shipping business group of the Embiricos was
Captain Leonardos Embiricos, born in 1765 on the Aegean island of
Andros, which was then part of the Ottoman Empire, becoming part
of the modern Greek state in 1830. In the nineteenth century Andros

244 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
became one of the main maritime centres of the Aegean. By the early
nineteenth century Leonardos had seven sons - Antonios, Nikolaos,
Matthaios, Ioannis, Georgios, Michalis and Konstantinos - who worked
as captains and skippers. This second generation of the Embiricos family
was active in the first half of the nineteenth century. But it was the third
generation of Embiricos seafarers that established a diversified business
group.38 The turning point in the history of the Embiricos family was
the establishment in the 1860s of an Embiricos shipping office and
merchant house in Braila, one of the most important grain exporting
ports of the Danube, by Konstantinos L. Embiricos's sons, principally
Leonidas (1836-94), Alcibiades (1842-1924) and Epaminondas (1858-
1924). The Embiricos merchant and shipping house became one of the
most powerful in the Danube grain export trade and shipping. Another
important step in the family business was the opening of the London
family office. Stamatios G. Embiricos (1868-1929), after first working in
his uncles' office in Braila, opened the first Andriot shipping office in
the UK, S.G. Embiricos Ltd, in 1896. By 1914 the family was the largest
Greek shipowning group, with 32 steamships, 92,500 dwt, representing
13 per cent of the Greek-owned fleet.
The family businesses diversified into trade, finance, banking and
industry. Investment in industry took place on the island of Andros in
1876 when K.L. Embiricos established a grain steam mill and another
factory for the production of silk. The leading personalities of the fam-
ily business group were Alcibiades and Epaminondas. Alcibiades main-
tained the family business in Braila, Danube, and was also involved
in trade and finance. Epaminondas K. Embiricos, after working with
his brother in Romania, returned to Athens where he expanded the
family business in Greece in the banking sector by participating in the
establishment of the Bank of Athens in 1893. Epaminondas, married to
the daughter of a Greek Prime Minister (Alexandros Koumoundouros),
became heavily involved in the political life of the country in the two
decades after 1890.39
The fourth generation of the Embiricos family continued the ship-
ping business in the first half of the twentieth century and further
diversified into banking, industry, mining and tourism. In line with the
fragmentation policy of the large family Greek shipping groups three
branches of the family stood out; it should be noted that the Embiricos
family firms functioned independently but also as a group in loose col-
laboration with each other. The first branch was from the grandsons of
Georgios, Leonidas (1872-1947), Michalis and Maris - known also as
the Embiricos brothers. The Embiricos brothers invested in industry,

Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso 245
banking and insurance. They bought the most important shipyards in
Piraeus, the Vassiliadis Shipyards and Mechanical Works, and they oper-
ated these from 1918 to 1926. They also invested in two coal mines,
those of Aliveri and Oropos, and created the Bank of National Economy
(1918-29) and two insurance companies in 1920.40
The second branch of activities of the Embiricos family business group
was formed by Georgios Miltiadi Embiricos (1875-1945) who, after
working at his uncles' office in Braila, Romania, came back to Greece in
1906. Apart from shipping he invested in a cotton spinning factory in
1926, in the marble industry in Chios and in a Greek hotel company.
The third branch of the family stems from Epaminondas Embiricos's
sons, who continued their activities in shipping and banking.41
As one of the most powerful families of Andros, related by marriage to
most of the families of the islands, the Embiricos had been involved in
politics since the formation of the Greek state, representing the interests
of the Andros shipping group containing all Andriot shipping families.42
In 1925, the Embiricos companies numbered a total of fifty steamships,
and in 1939 twenty-six steamships of 114,156 grt, which placed them
second only to the Kulukundis family in the ‘premier league' of Greek
shipowning.43 The economic crisis of the 1930s hit the Embiricos busi-
ness of the fourth generation hard; they fell back on shipping, leaving
aside initiatives in other economic sectors.
In the past - WW II period the Embiricos shipping interests con-
tinued through their office in Piraeus and S.G. Embiricos in London.
This office was actually founded in Cardiff in 1896 and transferred
to the City in the early twentieth century, remaining active until
the mid-1990s. With continuous operation for almost a century, S.G.
Embiricos was the longest-lived Greek shipping office in London. The
S.G. Embiricos office was the base for the business activities of all
members of the extensive Embiricos family at various times during the
twentieth century, as well as of Andriot and other families that had
placed the management of their fleet in its hands. Management was
assumed by the sons of Stamatios G. Embiricos. From the early 1970s
the S.G. Embiricos fleet decreased, as at that time some shipowning
families decided to manage the ships they had previously entrusted to
this office themselves or withdraw from the business.
However, out of the fifth generation a new company emerged that
brought the Embiricos family back into the top ten Greek shipowning
groups. The grandson of Epaminondas K. Embiricos, Epaminondas G.
Embiricos, started up his own company in London and Piraeus in 1969,
the Buenmar Compania Naviera in Piraeus and Aeolos Management

246 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
Company in London. By 1990 the Embiricos group had the biggest
fleet in terms of capacity in Greek-owned shipping. From the mid-
1990s Epaminondas G. Embiricos managed ships through the Embiricos
Shipping Agency Ltd in London, and he was elected president of the
Greek Shipping Co-operation Committee in the British capital for a
decade from 1999.
The Andreadis business group (end of the nineteenth
century to the present)
The Andreadis family from Chios, one of the main Aegean mari-
time centres of the nineteenth and twentieth centuries, became
involved in shipowning in the last third of the nineteenth century.
The founder of the Andreadis family shipping business is Captain
Georgios Hadjifrangoulis Andreadis (1875-1945), who in the early
1900s was operating a fleet of five sailing ships out of Chios, Syros
and Constantinople. Between the wars, he bought and sold at least ten
steamships and on the eve of World War II, the family shipping firm
owned two steamships of 8,338 grt.
It was the second generation of the family, son Stratis G. Andreadis
(1905-89), who made the company grow dynamically in the second
half of the twentieth century. Stratis Andreadis married the daughter
of Alexandros Koryzis; his father-in-law became one of the directors of
the Agricultural and National Bank of Greece and also Prime Minister
of Greece. Andreadis started out in the early post-war years with two
Liberty ships. In 1958 he was managing twelve cargo vessels and tankers
totalling 220,000 dwt, and in 1970 a fleet of 18 ships of 600,000 dwt.
Stratis Andreadis emerged as a leading figure in the Greek shipowning
community, serving as Vice-president of the Union of Greek Shipowners
from 1950 to 1960, and as President from 1960 to 1974. Thanks to his
exceptional links with the economic and political élite of Greece, he
developed impressive activity in the sectors of banking, industry, insur-
ance and tourism. In the 1950s during the Karamanlis government he
acquired the Commercial Bank of Greece, which controlled three other
smaller banks, the Ionian and Popular Bank, the Bank of Piraeus and
the Bank of Attica. Through the banks, the business group invested in a
series of industrial plants in the food, chemicals and transportation sec-
tors.44Andreadis group also gained control of top insurance companies,
of the Hilton Hotel in Athens, and of the shares of the London-based
Commercial Bank of the Near East, which operated under the group's
control until the 1990s.

Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso 247
The activities of the Andreadis business group peaked at the time
of the military dictatorship (1967-74) in Greece.45 In 1975, the first
post-dictatorship democratic government of Karamanlis nationalized
the Commercial Bank of Greece. Despite this great blow, the Andreadis
shipping business, based in Piraeus and London, continued its activities
and reached its peak in 1981, with 30 ships of 1,200,000 dwt. The third
generation took over in the 1980s, his three sons continuing the fam-
ily's shipping business to the present day.
Discussion
We stated in Proposition 1 that business groups are more common in
economies with less developed market institutions. All three cases show
that the business groups in southern Europe originate either before
or after the mid-nineteenth century and are connected with regional
growth and increasing business opportunities. The factors behind the
groups' origins are not only related to problems or ‘market imperfec-
tions' but also to specific capabilities and/or skills of the founders and of
their business environment. True, all three business groups emerged in a
period with scarcely developed external capital markets, and the found-
ers resorted first to family and business connections to get the capital
they needed, then to the cash flow generated by the early companies.
But these were the result of entrepreneurial skills based on the organiza-
tional capabilities and human resources of their maritime region, which
gave them competitive advantage.
As the Embiricos case shows, business groups that owned and man-
aged fleets of ships involved in the long-distance Mediterranean and
Black Sea trade thrived in the island maritime centres. Working in
the volatile environment of the eastern Mediterranean in a period of
political turmoil and continuous movement of boundaries, the island
communities developed their own institutions and diversified into
vertical and horizontal economic activities that involved shipping,
shipbuilding and repairing, finance, insurance, trade and ship supply-
ing. In order to survive in the international shipping business island
shipping groups developed organizational capabilities and structures.
By 1830s two types of business were clearly formed in Greek entre-
preneurial families.46 Both were involved in international sea trade
and developed as international businesses carrying the trade of third
countries. The first type were family businesses involved in trade and
shipping and established on the islands of the Ionian and the Aegean

248 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
seas; the second were the diaspora family businesses involved in trade,
shipping and finance and established where the ships traded, in the
main ports of the Black Sea, the western Mediterranean and northern
Europe. Almost all of the diaspora entrepreneurial families also came
from the islands. These bipolar types of business, the diaspora mer-
chant houses abroad and the shipping companies of the Greek islands,
worked closely together but also evolved independently. As they were
usually connected through family bonds, however, they also formed
a Greek business network that functioned on a European level from
the ports of the Black Sea (Romania and Russia), to those of northern
Europe (UK and France).47 In this way, the groups created the organi-
zational structures of the companies and networks that made up their
production systems.48
Equally, in the case of Spain, particular areas such as the north-
ern Basque country where Bilbao lies had a long maritime tradition
involved in trade, shipping, shipbuilding and finance from the early
modern period to the nineteenth century, with very close connections
to northern European ports, particularly in France and England, to
which Castillian wool and Basque iron bars were exported.49 During
the nineteenth century Bilbao shipowners and merchants set up com-
mercial houses in London and Liverpool, first related to the trade with
the Spanish colonies of Cuba and Puerto Rico, then to the iron ore trade
from Bilbao to England. They imported from the UK not only modern
ships, but also new systems of finance and ownership.50
As regards Proposition 2, our case studies offer empirical support
for the existence of a sort of positive relationship between the degree
of groups' activities and the existence of market imperfections in the
economy. Two critical issues here, closely related, are the extent of
internal rivalry (or competition) in every industry and the degree
of openness of the home country economy. In countries such as
Greece and Spain, not very open to foreign competition until the
end of the twentieth century, powerful existing groups could rein-
vest excess capital and human resources into new sectors, benefiting
from the organizational capabilities and expertise accumulated in the
incumbent firms to discover and exploit new local business oppor-
tunities linked to a growing and developing economy. The Aznar
group reached its highest degree of diversification during the fourth
generation (1950s to 1970s), where the level of business rivalry was
lower and there were a lot of business opportunities in a developing
Spanish economy. Conversely, as economies became more open and
competition fiercer, entrepreneurial families tended to concentrate

Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso 249
their businesses on core activities, for example, shipping in the fifth
generation of Aznars.
The Embiricos group reached its highest degree of diversification
during the third and fourth generations, under the highly protectionist
environment of the Greek economy in the first half of the twentieth
century and particularly during the interwar period. The economic
crisis hit their non-shipping businesses in Greece hard, hence the fifth
Embiricos generation concentrated on the core activity of shipping.
Equally, the evidence of the Andreadis group indicates that it reached
its apogee in its diversification during the 25 ‘golden years' of the post-
WWII period during which the Greek economy had developed highly
protectionist measures. After the nationalization of the Andreadis busi-
ness group, mainly due to political reasons, in the mid-1970s, the entre-
preneurial family again reverted to shipping, where the third generation
still remains.
The evidence presented in our case studies shows that business
groups always cultivated a close relationship with every government,
no matter what its political colour, as indicated in Proposition 3. In all
three cases, family members were connected with governments; often
they were MPs or cabinet ministers. Moreover, more often than not,
as the evidence of the Embiricos reveals, members of various branches
of the family were allied to opposing political parties. In economies
with a high level of state intervention and low internal rivalry, this close
relationship resulted in discretionary benefits to the groups' firms, thus
contributing to augmenting their market power and the scale and scope
of their business activities. That was the case of the fourth generation of
the Aznar group under the Francoist regime, when it reached the apex
of its political and economic power. But this close political intimacy
became a heavy liability after the death of Franco and the change of
political regime, and the group's head could not successfully cope with
the new political and economic changes that happened thereafter. The
same occurred in the Andreadis case. Andreadis was close to Karamanlis'
right-wing democratic government in the 1950s and early 1960s; he
also became very close to the dictatorial regime during 1967-74. It
was then the Karamanlis post-dictatorial democratic government that
nationalized his business group.
Our case studies also reveal that family considerations play a key
role in the birth and evolution of business groups, as indicated in
Proposition 4. In all our three cases multi-generation succession was
effective as there was availability of male heirs. In the case of the
Embiricos family, fragmentation of three branches of the family in

250 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
the fourth generation brought expansion and further diversification
of business.
The families developed structures to keep ownership together, such
as London offices, which were opened by all three families, Aznar,
Embiricos and Andreadis. In the Greek case, the London office acted
like a management office for the shipping businesses of all members
of the family: it was the next stage of the transformation of the
diaspora and island entrepreneurial family business of the nine-
teenth century, a new ‘modern' formation of a management office
that happened after the 1890s. Major technological developments
like the advent of the steamship in combination with the unprec-
edented growth of international trade changed the established pro-
duction system of the sailing ship period and brought the need for
drastic transformations. The Greek London offices proved pivotal
for development of the Greek shipping business. In fact these offices
became recipients of the organizational capabilities of the business
groups. As their owners came from specific islands, they became,
in fact, the new form of the island shipping business groups. These
offices were really management firms, ‘umbrellas' of the activities of
the shipping companies of the Ionian and Aegean islands. Most of
these evolved as the modern shipowning groups of the second half
of the twentieth century.51
Another important aspect of long-term survival was access to capital.
Family was usually the first source of capital to start business all over
Europe in the nineteenth century, regardless of the situation and devel-
opment of capital markets. But family firms could successfully cope with
more developed capital markets, too. In Spain, entrepreneurial families
forged stable alliances with the strong German-style investment banks
created in the early decades of the twentieth century in order to ensure
tighter financial control of the family firm and/or business group, as
happened with the Aznar family. In Greece, shipowners created new
banks to enter new businesses, or allied with governments to gain con-
trol of existing ones, as the Andreadis group did. Nevertheless, it must
also be stressed that this close relationship between entrepreneurial
families and banks to control family firms had its risks and disadvan-
tages. In the case of Spain, it collapsed in the economic and financial
crisis of the late 1970s. In the case of Greece, the Andreadis family lost
its business group through the nationalization of its banks after the fall
of the dictatorship in the mid-1970s.
In Proposition 5 we posit that shipping business groups tended to
develop a path-dependent ‘business culture' deeply rooted in their

Gelina Harlaftis and Jesús M. Valdaliso 251
maritime regions of origin. In both our cases the business groups came
from traditional maritime regions, the Basque country and the Aegean
islands, where long-term involvement in shipping had formed path-
dependent institutions along with the ‘memory bank' of the know-how
of shipping operations. This memory bank acted as a sort of buffer
mechanism that facilitated the groups' long-term survival. Besides,
the existence of an international shipping business network allowed
the reproduction and renewal of business groups originating from
shipping.
Evidence of the evolution of 24 leading Greek shipping business
groups is presented in Appendix I. It should be noted that two catego-
ries appear: the traditional and non-traditional shipowning groups. By
‘traditional' shipowners we mean those that after World War II were at
least second generation.52 The importance of traditional shipowners is
evident until the 1970s; some groups had started operations in ship-
ping as far back as the 1810s or 1850s and continue operation to the
present day. In the first half of the nineteenth century they operated in
trade and shipping while almost all were involved in informal financing
within their island communities.53 Half of these business groups diversi-
fied into banking, insurance and manufacturing in the first half of the
twentieth century and the other half in the second half of the century.
There is a remarkable endurance of four entrepreneurial families from
the 1810s to the 2010s and of the traditional families to the 1970s. The
shipping crisis of the 1980s was a watershed for many of the traditional
families and a start-up opportunity for new ones. The first most impor-
tant non-traditional Greek shipowning groups were those of Onassis
and Niarchos, which diversified into air transport and manufacturing
in Greece from the 1950s to the 1970s. There was an important wave
of newcomers in shipping after the 1960s, forming powerful Greek
business groups that later diversified into banking, manufacturing, air
transport and media.
Evidence on the evolution of the top twenty Spanish shipping
companies from the mid-nineteenth century and throughout the
twentieth century points to a remarkable continuation of the same
entrepreneurial families in shipping until the 1950s, but only a few of
these created bigger groups.54 In the 1960s many newcomers appeared,
linked to new ships and markets (tankers), and well connected to high
government officials, but few of these were able to survive the crises
of the 1970s and 1980s. On the other hand, some of the old entre-
preneurial family shipping dynasties managed to successfully cope
with the new scenario (internationalization, changes in markets and

252 Business Groups and Entrepreneurial Families in Southern Europe
types of ships, and so on) for reasons that are difficult to ascertain,
but that, no doubt, are rooted in the path dependence and buffer-
ing considerations described above. Interestingly enough, these cases
of longstanding entrepreneurial families come from very specialized
shipping firms: successful early entrants in new markets in the late
1960s, such as short sea container routes; old families well established
in auxiliary shipping services, such as tug boats - Aznar and Boluda,
among others - which gave them the cash flow needed to re-enter the
shipping market; and others.55
Conclusions
This chapter has analysed the formation and path of southern European
shipping business groups, drawing evidence from Europe's most south-
westerly point, Bilbao in Spain, and from Europe's most south-easterly
point, the Greek Aegean islands. Evidence has revealed that business
groups that start from shipping can be traced back at least to the early
nineteenth century as international shipping business was almost
always diversified into banking and trade. Spanish and Greek business
groups developed informal institutions (networks) based on organiza-
tional abilities and human resources, in which lay their competitive
advantage.
It has been found, thus, that multi-generational business groups have
periods of concentration within shipping and other periods of high
levels of diversification into various economic sectors. Another charac-
teristic is the development and maintenance of close connections with
formal institutions, political parties and governments. They also inter-
nally developed organizational structures and buffering mechanisms
that provided multi-generational longevity and survival. Moreover, in
both cases path dependence provided a remarkable multi-generational
survival that at times endured more than two centuries.
This chapter brings the southern European business groups into the
forefront of discussion. It clearly indicates three outstanding issues:
their familial nature; their remarkable multi-generational succession
and longevity; and finally, the importance of shipping as the sector
of origin. It seems that shipping provided international mobility and
flexibility and gave opportunities for the development of buffering
and survival mechanisms. It might be interesting to investigate and
compare with more case studies of business groups from traditional
maritime nations, such as the UK, and from new maritime powers such
as China and Korea.

264 Epilogue: A Key Industry or an Invisible Industry?
seas and oceans to economic growth; ‘for an effective understanding of
the emergence of rapid economic growth in the nineteenth century and
beyond it is necessary to understand what happened to shipping'.5
Global economic integration has been one of the most striking phe-
nomena of the last two centuries. In a 1986 article, Skip Fischer and
Helge Nordvik summarized the preconditions for the emergence of an
integrated international economy. Their presentation of the ‘consensus'
preconditions emphasizes five crucial areas: efficient markets, natural
resources, innovations, population development and the changing role
of government.6 We think that these five areas provide a good point of
departure for a discussion of how shipping has contributed to global
economic integration.
In mainstream economic analyses, the role of shipping in the devel-
opment of efficient markets has been far from absent: ‘ocean shipping
became one of the major exogenous variables to explain the rapid eco-
nomic growth'.7 Indeed, one might claim that the preoccupation with
this ‘effect' of shipping has led to a neglect of the sector's contribution
in the other areas. The sharp decline of freight rates and its implications
for market efficiency has been one of the main focal points.8 The con-
tribution of shipping to international integration is not, however, solely
linked to the question of costs.
Although economists have usually emphasized the political aspects
of market integration in the second half of the twentieth century, ship-
ping has been a crucial ingredient in this process. As Ekberg, Lange and
Merok indicate in Chapter 6, the question of the quality of transport
services should also be considered. Indeed, the global production net-
works that have appeared over the last decades depend on shipping for
their functioning and viability: ‘firms that have relocated their produc-
tion of manufactured goods do not see shipping costs as an obstacle'.9
The market is the meeting point of consumers and producers. For these
two groups to engage, an enormous, though ‘invisible', infrastructure
has been created by maritime entrepreneurs. This infrastructure relates
not only to ships and ports, but to all the formal and informal institu-
tions that are necessary to produce the transport services. As indicated in
Gordon Boyce's chapter, this infrastructure has been developed, not by
the visible hand of governments, but by maritime businesses seeking effi-
cient solutions to organize their own activities. The study of the maritime
industries therefore reveals the origins, expansion and mechanisms of the
‘hidden' system that enables consumers and producers to do business.
Nowhere is this more evident than in the case of natural resources.
C. Knick Harley shows in Chapter 3 that centuries ago shipping

Gelina Harlaftis, Stig Tenold and Jesús M. Valdaliso 265
integrated the staple producers of the periphery into the global econ-
omy. Even today, raw material transports make up the bulk of the world's
trade and shipping activity, laying the fundament for a global system
of production. The ‘transport component' of an iPod manufactured in
China and sold in France is of course the movement of the container
with the iPod from Asia to Europe. More importantly, with regard to the
demand for shipping, it also includes the transport of petroleum, coal,
chemicals and other raw materials needed to produce the electronic
components and, ultimately, the iPod. The low cost of shipping goods
by sea enables manufacturers to ‘slice up the value chain' and relocate
parts of the production process in the manner of the iPod producers.10
However, they also depend upon the stability and efficiency of the sea
transport system to accomplish this.
The vast majority of the goods that form our daily lives depend upon
the shipping industry. When you buy a bottle of shampoo, a tube of
toothpaste, a sports jacket, a refrigerator or a car, you also implicitly buy
a number of sea transport services. International trade and disintegra-
tion of production is totally dependent upon this myriad of transport
services. As the OECD has pointed out: ‘[W]ithout the new advances
achieved in the shipping industry in terms of technology and organiza-
tion, the growth in international trade in manufactured goods would
not have been so robust as it has been'.11
Advances in the technology and organization of shipping have
occurred continuously and had multiple effects on the process of inter-
national economic integration. Growing trade has enabled the mari-
time sector to introduce cost-effective innovations, which again have
increased the benefits from international trade. The archetypal ship-
ping innovation is the steamship. Armstrong and Williams show that
though the introduction of this new technology was gradual, its effect
on communications may be considered revolutionary. The shipping
industry has been a necessary condition for improvements in several
areas beyond the ship operation. For instance the international web
of submarine cables, the mail service or the mass European immigra-
tion was carried out by steamships. Thus through the indirect effect on
communication and international connectedness, shipping's role as an
important catalyst for international integration and economic growth
was further strengthened.
As Kaukiainen in Chapter 5 has shown, after the steamship, techno-
logical advances in shipping have primarily been related to increases
in ship size. The introduction of mammoth vessels utilizing economies
of scale reduced not only transport costs, but also the visibility of the

266 Epilogue: A Key Industry or an Invisible Industry?
industry. The sheer size of the new ships reduced the number of ports
on which the vessels were able to call. Moreover, it meant that ports for
the most part had to be moved out of the city centres and into ‘distribu-
tion nodes' far removed from everyday life. As a result of technological
innovations, ships and ports are hardly an observable aspect of daily
economic life any longer. The technological and institutional innova-
tions have made the shipping sector more efficient and, ironically, more
invisible.
Up until the 1950s shipping and seafarers were the lifeline to the
world outside. The ship was the gateway to the rest of the world: the
only means to go around the globe, to trade, to conquer, to learn. In the
days before mass tourism a seafaring career was the most common way
in which to be exposed to far away continents. In this respect the cul-
tural dimension overshadows the economic one. However, through the
shift of worldpopulation, shipping enabled the utilization of previously
untapped resources. Indeed one of the most dramatic developments of
the nineteenthcentury was the ‘Europeanization' of the world: from the
early nineteenth century to the early twentieth century some 44 million
Europeans changed continent. They all moved by sea.
All the leading European economies have been leading maritime
nations that expanded to the New World and Asia. State maritime
policy in the last five centuries has struggled between protectionist and
liberal policies as to the movement of goods at sea. The main problem
for the national governments has been that the shipping industry is
truly international. Shipowners have introduced self-regulation and
self-liberalization by breaking the genuine link of ownership and flag.
The massive introduction of flags of convenience has characterized the
global shipping industry since World War II. The role of governments
has, on the one hand, been limited by shipowners' exit opportuni-
ties; only in periods of distress have shipowners turned to the state for
support. Sarah Palmer's chapter is indicative of this awkward power
relationship.
On the other hand, governments' role has been crucial in the adjust-
ment of shipping infrastructure, for instance ports. Technological inno-
vations in shipping had multiple effects on port systems and waterfront
services to which governments had to respond. Reveley and Tull reveal
the challenging problems governments have to face to change the old
maritime infrastructures. Moreover, global politics and national poli-
cies have an important impact on the formation and transformation
of ports. The importance of Chinese trade for the development of the
international economy in recent decades is indisputable. Adolf K. Y. Ng

Gelina Harlaftis, Stig Tenold and Jesús M. Valdaliso 267
and Ka-chai Tam illustrate the enormous transformation of the coun-
try's port system that has been necessary to facilitate the impressive
trade growth.
The five features discussed above reveal to varying degrees the close
link between developments in shipping and international economic
integration. The driving force of the global shipping industry was
businesses. Technological changes in shipping, associated with higher
requirements for fixed capital, transformed the nature and the organi-
zation of business enterprise in the nineteenth century. The advent of
steam shipping brought about new specialist firms with a more complex
business structure. Steam shipping, given its highly capital-intensive
nature, was one of the sectors where specialist and limited firms did
appear earlier.12 But contrary to what happened in other high capital-
intensive industries, instead of large-scale single firms, business groups
and networks pervaded this industry, as Harlaftis and Valdaliso have
indicated in Chapter 14.13 There was substantial heterogeneity; Barnard
and Starkey in Chapter 12 show that even traditional private companies
could still be prominent at local or national levels.
Three further characteristics of the shipping business help to explain
the high level of international competition. First, the fact that it was
a truly international business as early as the 1870s, not affected by
tariffs and other barriers to competition erected within the national
markets in the last quarter of the nineteenth century. Second, through-
out the twentieth century, shipowners have acquired their factors of
production - ships, labour and capital - in the international markets,
motivated by a constant search for as low costs as possible. Third, ship-
ping's highly cyclical nature affects the mode of competition. As Tenold
shows in Chapter 13, shipping is a risky business where the market cycle
is very prominent.14 It is also a ‘romantic business' in Stopford's words,
‘in which the lucky' - but skilled, we would add - ‘few can accumulate
wealth beyond the dreams of even lottery winners'.15
Shipping businesses have been a forerunner in the globalization pro-
cess; depending extensively on international demand in the nineteenth
century and combining global factors of production in the twentieth
century. Broeze showed that international mergers among shipping
operators were quite common in the 1980s, while for other industries
that movement began to happen in the 1990s.16 Containerization
brought about further changes in the nature of the shipping business,
broadening its scope. It became an intermodal or multimodal industry.
Shipping operators had to achieve better coordination with other agents
in the logistics value chain, and some of them started to integrate into

268 Epilogue: A Key Industry or an Invisible Industry?
some of those activities (port terminals, containers, trucks, railways,
and so on).
The discussion above has shown that the shipping industry, through
a number of mechanisms, has played a crucial role in the process of
global economic integration. This prompts our second question.
Why has shipping traditionally been absent from
mainstream research in economics and economic history?
The ‘invisibility' of shipping in academic debates or mainstream eco-
nomic and economic history journals is not only the result of the
failures of maritime historians and economists to provide context and
importance to their field of study. It is also a reflection of a combination
of factors that have to do with the nature of shipping itself and the ways
in which economic growth and development have traditionally been
analysed. At least four factors are important in this respect.
The first reason is the common neglect of the service sector in eco-
nomic and historical research. Studies of the emergence of modern
economic growth in industrializing economies usually focus on manu-
facturing, while seldom emphasizing the importance of service sector
activities. There have been attempts at rectifying this omission. The
importance of the international services sector, and particularly of ship-
ping, in the early economic development of a number of countries was
brought to the attention of the academic community more than half a
century ago. It involved the connection of shipping with the economic
development of the United States up to the 1860s argued by Douglass
North.17 In the early 1970s Shepherd and Walton, along the same line
as North, emphasized the importance of invisible payments, and espe-
cially of shipping income, during the early years of the development of
the United States economy: ‘The importance of shipping income has
often been omitted or at least not appropriately discussed by economic
historians.'18
The second reason that the maritime industries have been absent
from mainstream research is their international character, which blurs
the links to individual economies. The product of shipping, sea trans-
port, takes place beyond national boundaries, and its income is earned
abroad, removed from the economic structures of a specific country. It is
indicative that economists analysing national economies have classified
shipping income as ‘invisible earnings'. By making earnings from ship-
ping ‘invisible' their impact on the economy is devalued and is shown
to affect, say, only the balance of payments. More than a century ago,

Gelina Harlaftis, Stig Tenold and Jesús M. Valdaliso 269
Sir Robert Giffen, a member of the Royal Statistical Society, was one of
the first who publicly recognized the importance of shipping income in
national accounts.19 However, despite the fact that the importance of
shipping was evident in the economy of the earliest industrialized state,
Great Britain, the difficulties involved in calculating shipping income
have resulted in few such attempts.20
Often, statistics of national accounts depend on the understanding
(or lack of it) of top administrators of what shipping income is about.
For example from 1960 to 1998 the Bank of Greece, which provided
the most reliable figures for the Greek economy, calculated as ‘shipping
income' only the wages of Greek crews. The return of capital from
abroad by shipowners, either for their personal expenditure or for
investment in the Greek economy, was placed in some vague category
of ‘foreign capital'. Moreover, the employment of about 200,000 people
in the Piraeus shipping companies, as well as companies affiliated to
shipping, were not considered as shipping-related.21 The ‘invisibility' of
shipping in the national accounts adds to its neglect by economists and
bureaucrats to the present day.
Even in the branches of economics where the national dimension
is less important, such as international economics, shipping has been
neglected; transport ‘just happens' and is often even costless. The ‘new
economic geography' acknowledges that location matters, but still
considers transport as an ‘automatic' function. Many models analysing
international trade make the proposition that ‘transport costs are
assumed to be zero'.22 While the continuing technological and organi-
zational advances have led to a dramatic reduction of freight costs, this
is not yet the case.
The third reason for the invisibility of the business of shipping is that
it is ‘intangible', and its absence from the core of economic analysis mir-
rors the situation of many other service industries. The economic output
of a ship is not as easy to see or value as the output of a factory or a plot
of land. The fact that the ship and its crew spend most of the time at sea
further contributes to the detachment. Ships are demolished, they live
and die like people and they leave indirect traces. The wealth of prosper-
ous maritime cities or towns is still reflected in the North Sea, but more
so in the Mediterranean, on the decorated churches, large schools, pub-
lic buildings or houses of wealthy shipowners or even common seafarers.
The home town of the seafarer usually thrived when he was ‘invisible',
away from home at sea, but the creation of this wealth remains vague.
Finally, there is an indication that even the participants in the indus-
try would like it to be invisible. Many of the agents in the shipping

270 Epilogue: A Key Industry or an Invisible Industry?
sector, in particular the ‘old' shipping mavericks, have been shrouded
in secrecy. Indeed, some of the most prominent shipowners - Ludwig,
Onassis and Reksten to name a few - created elaborate organizational
structures, the aim of which was to obscure questions of ownership and
strategies. This may be a conscious adaptation to the fact that informa-
tion has tended to flow very rapidly in shipping - through brokers,
customers and competitors - making secrecy a necessary component
of a competitive strategy. Alternatively, these structures may have been
created purely for tax reduction purposes. Tax planning or tax evasion is
common in most multinationals today, but the international character
of the shipping industry made such arrangements possible at an earlier
time.
The four points presented above are our attempt at providing some
explanations of shipping's ‘invisibility'. The underestimation of the role
of shipping in mainstream research is far from new; in 1986, Fischer and
Nordvik stressed that economic historians tend to ‘treat the existence
of shipping as a given'.23 These aspects of the nature of the shipping
industry should not, however prevent maritime historians and econo-
mists from providing context and importance to their field of study.
This book is part of an attempt at doing exactly this.
A recent review of the state of maritime history praises the ‘increasing
professionalization of maritime history over the last thirty years'. The
author then goes on to suggest that several challenges have to be met
in order to ‘maximize its full potential'. According to the author of the
article, one Lewis R. Fischer, we have progressed - but there is still some
way to go.24
